THE AUSTRALIAN COMMONWEALTH!
Sounds well—doesn’t it ? But the DOMINION OF AUSTRALIA
would sound better —for
We have a Common Wealth in the
Price and Quality of
CAMEO CIGARETTES.
THE RED PAGE.
Some time in the eighties Australia, for
her sins, was visited by Douglas Brooke
Wheelton Sladen. Australia has never re-
covered from the shock. In vain has she
urged her kangaroo to prodigious leaps of
disavowal : behind her the incubus sticks
like a burr. Let us writhe.
• » * *
Sladen came to this country in the eighties,
a young man of 25 or so. He was con-
nected with Melb. University, and joined
Melbourne degrees to his Oxford bachelor-
ship of arts. Then, for a short time, he
came to Sydney. He was in Australia four
or five years altogether, spending nearly the
whole time in the cities named. Ten
years ago he returned to England, and be-
came ‘ a representative Australian. ’ He has
been ‘ a representative Australian ’ ever
since. Let us writhe.
» * * •
In Australia, from a literary point of
view, Sladen seemed a consummate type
of British impudence. Modest natives
fairly wilted before him. His ‘ Haw ! ’
pervaded the whole continent, and sent a
resounding echo \as far as Maoriland. He
had limited brain, and limitless energy.
And he wrote ‘ poetry ‘ poetry inspired by
life and scenery in Australia.’ Australia
writhed.
* * * *
This is some of Sladen’s * poetry,’ from a.
volume which is called ‘Australian Lyrics,’
and contains not one verse that is lyrical, not
one idea that is essentially Australian.
Mbs. Watson. A Queensland Heroine.
Yes ! bury this woman as heroes are buried
A daughter and type of the conquering race—
With bayonets sheathed and with ranks un-
serried ;
For she fought with the savages face to face,
And conquered. There’s many a chapter in story
With heroines’ names writ in characters fair,
But never a one that outglitters in glory
The wife of the fisher of b4che-de-mer.
‘With ranks unserried.’ But why ‘ un-
serry ’ the ranks ? Alas ! the rhyme will
have it so. Sladen has learnt that it is good
to lead off with a strong sentiment, strongly
expressed ; but he is no poet, and Pegasus
has thrown him off’ in two strides. The rest
is trite and tumty-tumty.
» * * *
In nine verses Sladen reduces tragedy to
bathos, and trails abysmal commonplaces
over an inspiring theme. Anti-climax
follows anti-climax. Chopped lines tumble
on top of chopped lines as if verse-writing
were a matter of throwing logs on a domestic
wood-heap : Sladen, the purveyor, heaving
from the cart whatever is handiest. E.g.
This woman was born of a nation of freemen, —
Their birthright to dare and to die on the wave;
Yet even to Britain’s adventurous seamen
’Twere hardly disgrace if they seemed not as
brave.
What wonder if Gilbert, who sank in the
“ Squirrel,”
Or Davis, or Baffin, or Frobisher shrank
From facing the strange supernatural peril
Of crossing the sea in a worn water-tank ?
Amiable instances —lugged in by the ear
and a waistcoat-flap ! Drake is absent, poor
fellow !—the line wouldn’t hold him. And
ai! for Captain Cook. Observe, too, the
faithful touches of the master ! the ‘ corro-
borative detail, intended to give artistic
verisimilitude to a bald and unconvincing
narrative ’ ! —in a word, the water-tank ! In
the next verse it appears as a boiler—
A pitiful story ! —this val-i-ant woman
Who tempted the sea (here a dozen leagues
wide),
Thus shipped, after routing the barbarous foeman
When she came to a haven to shelter her, died.
Eight days on the waves in a rust-eaten boiler—
(She had better by half have been slaughtered
at first).
And the foe that outflanked her, her only one
foiler,
Stands dim in her diary—“ Dead with thirst.”
* * * •
Note the airy, off-hand parenthesis— ‘ here
a dozen leagues wide.’ Sladen is not really
concerned about the sea’s width ; but his
prescient imagination has already leaped to
the‘died ’in line 4; and . . . exactly! And
mark ‘the barbarous foeman King Billy of
Buckaroo !—how purely Australian in refer-
ence and emphasis ! how thrillingly lyncal .
And the ‘slaughtered’ ! and the ‘only one
foiler ’ ! and the rest ! But Sladen is gather-
ing himself for his famous, his most tre-
mendous effort . . • watch him soar 1
No drain left to drink—yet she would nob be fear-
ful
But in painful and feeble handwriting had writ
That her baby “ was better and more cheerful,
And condensed milk appeared to agree with it.
Not even the steamer when passing so near her,
Unheeding the signals she hoisted in vain,
Could sicken the hope from her heart or die-
cheer her;
She noted it down, but she did nob oomplain.
» » * •
Is that poetry, hein ? Is it Australian ? is
it lyrical 1 does it combine grandeur with ten-
derness, pathos with melody ? is it full of
nobility, sublimity, sawdust? But there is
another verse —hardly less touching, less
graceful; and here are Drake and Cook, and
Nelson into the bargain !
If only a watch had been kept on that steamer i
What has not that captain to answer for,
(Well, he has to answer for one of the worst
attempts at verse ever made in English.)
Sent thither by Heaven in time to redeem her !
He will surely be haunted for evermore.
(Not, let us hope, by Sladen’s rhymes. No
offence, however rank, could deserve that!)
Were she living who fought then and wrought
then so well, sons
Might perchance have been born in our own far
north
(‘ Our own far north ’!—Sladen's own !
the far north of the Condensed Milkman !
Now, by Emu ! . . .)
To match with the Drakes and the Cooks and the
Nelsons
Whom the Mother of Continents has brought
forth.
* • * *
And this is the work of a B. A., Oxford;
B.A. and LL.B., Melbourne ! This is an
‘Australian lyric,’the product of a ‘repre-
sentative Australian,’ of a man whose name
is often heard in London minor literary
circles, often paragraphed in London minor
society papers ! . . . Well !
* • • •
It must be owned that all Sladen’s ‘ lyrics ’
are not as bad as this ; but they are nearly.
There is always the same dull mouthing of
dull ideas ; the same class of puerilities and
stupidities ; the same utter absence of
melody and style, of anything that looks like
sincere feeling or original capacity. And
Sladen went ~to England and published
‘ Australian Lyrics ’ and several other
volumes of verse—of one of which a ladies’
paper, ‘ The Queen ’ (to which Sladen con-
tributed), wrote that ‘Mr. Sladen pays a
tribute to the memory of Lindsay Gordon,
the “Poet of Yictoria ; ” but that erratic
and unfortunate genius left little behind
him that is so good as many parts of Mr.
Sladen’s work. Taken altogether it gives him
a high cloAm to be considered the best poet that
Australia has yet produced. ’ Doff cabbage-
trees to Sladen, Australia’s premier poet !
And Sladen solemnly printed this testi-
monial in the forefront of ‘ Australian
Lyrics’—in the vestibule of the temple
where the Condensed Milk Poem is en-
shrined ! . . . Well ! !
• * • *
British impudence ! —was there ever im-
pudence in the world to match it ? Every
week new Sladens reach our shores, with
as much brain-quality, intellectual acumen,
as the original Sladen—that is to say, with
little or none. But they enter our Parlia-
ments ; they fill our profitable employments ;
they marry" our daughters and sisters (and
sometimes thrash ’em into divorce); they
own our lands; they rule our businesses ;
they dominate our country. Everywhere
the modest and brainier Australian stands
aside, or nearly everywhere : the Briton
has far more share in the destiny of Aus-
tralia than his intelligence warrants, or his
numbers entitle him to. And all this from
a capacity to eat heavily and drink heavily,
and present letters of introduction at Govern-
ment House, and bilk a tailor. No, not all ;
for something must be allowed for that
British energy which is to British impu-
dence as root to flower. In Emerson’s esti-
mate of the * English the recurrence of re-
ferences to this quality shows how strongly
it impressed him.
I find the Englishman to be him of all men who
stands firmest in his shoes. They have in them-
selves what they value in their horses, mettle and
bottom. ... It was an odd proof of this impres-
sive energy, that, in my lectures, I hesitated to
read and threw out for its impertinence many a
disparaging phrase which I had been accustomed
to spin about poor, thin, unable mortals; so much
had the fine physique and the personal vigor of
this robust race worked on my imagination.
Emerson would have left in those phrases for
an Australian course of lectures.
* * * *
Doff cabbage-trees (and this time in
earnest) to British energy! There is no
doubt that in the average man the failure
to push himself forward implies a cer-
tain slackness of fibre, feebleness of
constitution ; and modesty, like ‘ dowdyism,
is an expression of imperfect vitality.' But
British energy cannot score all round. The
beer-swilling, beef-eating John Bull type is
the type most opposed to the poetical and
artistic temperament; and when by defect
of education or force of circumstance it
is found in the poetical or artistic held,
where body - quality yields to brain-
quality, the result is ... a Sladen. Yery
likely a successful Sladen, as worldly
success goes ; a Sladen who puffs and is
puffed by men of his own calibre ; but still
. . . a Sladen.
* * * *
Some ten years ago Sladen went to Lon-
don, and he seems to have prospered by
log-rolling. He collected a book of Austra-
lian verse which is full of blunders and
inadequacies $ gives Sladen’s own verse
almost as much space as Kendall and
Stephens ; contains one ‘ Rondel ’ which
appears to be the only decent thing Sladen
ever wrote ; and is readable and useful in
spite of all. Then Sladen travelled in Japan,
and wrote a valueless book about his trip ;
he travelled in Canada and wrote another
valueless book ; and he compiled a collection
of verses from ‘Younger American Poets,’
which is really well done, and is about the
best book of the kind issued. At forty-one
years of age the Sladen of to-day is much
more capable than the' callow and cocksure
young man of the eighties.
• * • *
But he is still the same old Sladen. And
now, very late on the page, we are getting to
the gist of the matter —the only reason for
probing these old sores. A few months ago
Barrere and Leland’s ‘ Slang Dictionary ’
was reprinted in cheaper form : the original
edition was expensive, and sold only to sub-
scribers. This dictionary is a good diction-
ary, in that it contains much out-of-the-way
information on interesting questions of word
and phrase. It is a bad dictionary, in that
thousands of references are supplied without
a date—which robs them of a full three-
fourths of their value ; and in that, being
very incapably edited, no attempt has been
made to reduce the items to a qualitative
standard. The editor (whom one judges to
be Barrere, working on Leland’s materials)
appears to have put in everything forwarded
to him as slang, in the endeavour to swell his
volumes to imposing size. Expressions con-
fined to a limited time, or district, have been
placed on an equal footing with those used
over a long period, a whole country ; and
there is no clue to the extent of vogue.
Further, the derivations are for the most
part sadly inadequate, as if the editor had
wished to make his book with as little pains
as possible. And . . . this is also ‘ the
first Slang Dictionary to which the rich and
racy slang of the fifth continent —the mighty
Australian commonwealth of the future—
has been contributed by one long resident
in the country and familiar both with its
life and its literature.’ Our prophetic soul!
* * » *
Some examples of Australian slang from
‘A Dictionary of Slang, Jargon, and Cant’
(Lond., Geo. Bell, 1897).
Anty-up (Australian and American), a game of
cards.
As they ride up, a savage-looking half-bred bull-dog
yelps hoarsely, and two or three men creep out from
underneath the tarpaulin of the nearest dray, where they
have been playing anty-up (a favorite game with cards)
for tobacco. John recognises a teamster who has been
employed by him.— D. Sladen.
Artesian (Australian, popular), colonial beer.
People in Gippsland, Victoria, use artesian just as
Tasmanians use cascade, in the sense of ‘beer,’
because the one is manufactured from the oele»
brated artesian well at Sale, Grippsland, and th«
other from the cascade water.
Australian flag (Anglo-Australian slang), the
bottom of a shirt. The Australian who lives up
the country generally wears a belt instead of
braces, the result being that, when he exerts him-
self, there is usually a great fold of shirt protruding
between his small clothes and his waistcoat, which
Englishmen have called in scorn the Australian
flag. The Cornstalk talks of him as a ‘ new
chum ’; he talks of the Cornstalk as ‘showing the
Australian flag. ’
Australian grip (up-country Australian), a
hearty shake of the hand (compare Masonic grip).
The bushman shakes hands very heartily—a long
grip with the whole hand, following three deep
shakes. He does not crush your hand ; but he is
sarcastic about the ‘ limp shakes ’ and 1 one-tinge*
shakes ’ of people ‘ newly out from home.’
None the less
Was he a graceful, well-bred host,
But he was hearty in accost.
And giving the Australian grip
And good up-country fellowship as bushmen.
— D. B. W. Sladen: A Summer Christmas.
Back block (Australian), the country outside the
margin of the settled districts.
Like the brief flight of a sparrow upon a wintry night,
Out of the frost and darkness into the warm and litrht.
Is the advent of a stranger in the hack blocks out west,
Here to-night, and gone to-morrow, after food, roof, and
rest.
— D. B. IF. Sladen: Out West in Queensland..
* * * *
Isn’t it awful ! And so it goes on through-
out the dictionary —ignorant or misleading
explanations of actual slang ; disquisitions on
imaginary ditto ; attributions to all Australia
of expressions which, a dozen years ago, had
temporary vogue in a limited district—backed
up and enforced by scores of references to
Sladen’s reminiscences, or quotations from
his unspeakable verses ! Occasionally there
is a reference to ‘Dr. Kendall,’ to ‘J. B.
Stephens, the Australian comic poet,’ and to
other writers on Australia ; but first and
last and foremost is Sladen. Isn't it awful!
* * * *
It is useless to protest, apparently. Sladen
has been puffed into London prominence as
‘a representative Australian,’ and ‘The
Bulletin’s ’ affirmation on behalf of Australia
that in that capacity he is an impudent
literary humbug will assuredly never reach
the minds of all the purchasers of the ‘ Slang
Dictionary ’ —whom Sladen’s pretensions and
misrepresentations will continue to lead astray
as long as the book lasts, and after that. For
Barrere and Leland have each done good
work in fields they know aiid understand;
and future editors will argue from the part
to the whole, and will quote as gospel the
blunders which they have quasi-authorised ;
and five centuries hence, in all probability,
some learned philologist will dig up a Sladen
myth and a Sladen ‘ poem,’ and found
thereon an appalling argument to prove that
the representative aboriginal of this epoch
had very large teeth and a very small skull
and an exceedingly low facial angle, and
went to battle waving his flag in his ‘ small
clothes ’ and excited to frenzy by ‘ artesian.
One can only hope that the historian of the
period will be able to refer to this notice (it
is written to that end) and authoritatively
contradict the philologist ; and if after
five centuries, in enumerating the blessings
which Britain has conferred upon this
country, he is unable to decide whether we
have most reason to be grateful for her con-
victs, or for her rabbits, or for her Sladen,
he will at least have this excuse, that it is
not so easy to settle the question even while
the fruits of each blessing visibly remain to
us. And in the meantime —let us writhe.

SURGEON LANGSTON, M.R.C.S., Eng.,
THE GREATEST AND MOST SUCCESSFUL PRACTI-
TIONER IN THE WORLD
But few of noted specialists have sprung- into pre-emin-
ence and an immense practice as Surgeon Langston, a
resident and practitioner at 121 Collins-street, Melbourne.
His name has become almost literally a household word ;
almost every other man you meet has a wonderful story to
relate of some almost miraculous cure he has performed
in an acute or chronic case of long standing, where other
physicians have failed. He addresses himself in particular
to cases that have baffled the skill and science of other
doctors. His name is a sufficient guarantee of a perfect
cure of every case he undertakes. Consult him this day.
It may save you much mental and physical suffering, and
add golden years to your life.
YOUNG MEN. You will find this distinguished doctor
your best friend and benefactor. If you are troubled
with want of sleep, a tired, gloomy feeling, and loss
of energy, which impair both your body and brain,
and absolutely unfit you for study, business, or the
proper enjoyment of life; if you are suffering from these
or similar symptoms you know the cause. Before it is
everlastingly too late, consult this reliable old specialist,
who thoroughly understands your trouble, and can treat
it with unfailing success.
MIDDLE-AGED MEN. There are many of you afflicted
with weak, aching back and kidneys, loss of mental and
physical power and vigor, and not unfrequently accom-
panied by an impairment of the general health. In justice
to this celebrated specialist it can be truthfully stated

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hunk of pure domestic felicity as reported
in Auckland police-court
The woman deposed that since their arrival from Mel-
bourne, six months ago, she had been confined to the
kitchen, not being allowed to enter any other room in
the house except her bedroom, where she slept with one
of her daughters. The children were not allowed to call
her mother, that name being reserved for the eldest
daughter, who was empowered to do aTl the punishin,. in
the house. The mother had to do all the hard work. On
one occasion the children tied her in a chair and beat
her and the father did not reprimand them. On several
occasions he struck her. When he went for a holiday she
had to remain at home in the kitchen or cellar, all the
doors of the house being locked She had never received
any money. .
WATTLE TIME.
Once again Spring’s brimming tide
Flows and floods on every side;
Joys and hopes like music throng,
Make the day one living song :
And the pulsing of delight
Holds me wakeful oft at night,
Lulled in passionate content
With the balmy stillness, rent
All at once by rapturous notes
Poured from thrushes’ bubbling thro:.ls.
Open-eyed and calm I lie,
Feel it sweet to live or die ;
While along the shadowy, dim
Chamber-ceiling, like a hymn
Swelling up from far away,
Creeps the tremulous flush of day.
Through the open window steal
Dawn’s pure breathings, and I feel,
O, my love, as if your breath —
It could waken me from death —
Softly came upon my face
From your dark and silent place.
Gathered sprays of golden bloom
Waft within my lonely room
Fragrance faint, that seems to s'. ;■ 7
From a springtide far away.
One with memory it seems,
Yivid as the light of dreams,
One with feeling, one with sense,
With the past—that, as in dense
Purple fume of altars, curls
Round me with its drowsy whirls ;
Till I lose the silent room,
Only scent the suave perfume,
Feel your hand, your lips, your hr \
Sweeter than fresh violets prcssc I ;
Hear your tender, haunting words,
Clear as early songs of birds.
Soul of my soul, heart of heart,
Then I know we cannot part ;
Though grim Death has had his v. .11,
You are near me—with me still.
It was in the spring-time, dear,
Once you dallied with me here—
Spring-tiine of our lives and lien ' s.
Blossom falls and spring depart* ;
Life rolls in with fuller flood,
Flower and fruit succeed the bud,
Waking to delicious dreams,
All that is to all that seems.
While we hold it, pleasure flics ;
Clasping, clinging, passion dies.
Thus the wattle-bloom at last
Breathes not only of the past,
But, with all its memories,
Linked in subtle melodies,
Threading each harmonic chan"7
Through my life’s fantastic range,
Leads me onwards to the close,
Far or near, of joys and woes.
Soul of my soul, heart of heart,
Yours to me has been the part
Spring to Summer plays, or sho——s
June sends down to coming flows, s.
In my life is one thing fair ?
Yours the hand that sowed it there.
Is there blossom ? Is there fruit ?
In our love it had its root.
So, though memories fainter grow,
You are with me still, I know ;
From my life and from my heart
Never, O lost love, to part.
Vic.
ALEXANDER LESLEY.
THE DAYS OF THE ROSES.
‘‘Days that were golden— days that were wreathed with
a garland of roses.”--Novaus.
This is the golden and sweet youth-tide,
When the world is fair and the world is wide,
And our hearts are as wide as the world and as
fair
And blithe as the song of the bird m the air;
So, comrades, sing with a cheery chime :
This is the golden and gracious time—
These are the days of the roses !
Free is the heart and free is the song,
And free is the lad as he laughs along ;
And the kiss of a maiden coy is free,
Though daintily shy may the red lips be ;
Where a kiss we find and a song’s sweet rhyme,
There is the golden and gracious time
There are the days of the rases !
So here’s a deep health to the heart that’s high,
And here’s to the love in a maiden’s eye !
And here’s to the lad and here’s to the lass,
And here’s to the foam on the sparkling glass,
And here’s to our land with its Lydian clime,
Here’s to the golden and gracious time —
Here’s to the days of the roses !
Q.
P. LUGTIG.
REALISATION.
If truth it be, as sages say,
That every wish and every dream
We cherish in our earthly day
Have real life in Nature’s scheme,
And live for us, though far away,
Where things exist and do not seem ;
Then, brother, sister, dread no more
The worst malignant Fate can do ;
Elsewhere, upon some radiant shore,
Wealth, Fame and Pleasure wait for you ;
And, O ! lost loves of mine, what store
Of’bliss for me when dreams come true !
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 18, 1897.
To the People of Victoria.
At the general election, which is due next
month, the first sober Government which
Victoria has had for the best part of 20
years will appeal to the people for an expres-
sion of confidence and a renewed lease of
power. The Bulletin trusts that it will get
both in a most liberal spirit. It is just
possible that the country will, by-and-bye,
regret having given them, for no one can say
for certain what the Democratic politician of
to-day may not develope into to-morrow, but
on its past record the Tuiiner-Isaacs
Ministry deserves the largest majority the
province can give it. And on its past record
the Opposition is utterly hopeless. Very
often indeed the politician who is a Demo-
crat in his youth grows to be a bitter Tory in
his old age, therefore it is unsafe to prophesy
that the present Victorian Government will
always continue to deserve public confidence.
But it is not once in a thousand times that
the politician who is Tory in his youth is
anything save more Tory in his old age,
therefore it is almost absolutely certain that
its opponents will continue to deserve public
mistrust as heretofore. There is no possible
hope for Victoria in Gillies, and MTntyre,
and Thomas Bent, and Baronet O’Loghlen,
and Murray Smith, and the rest of the
miscellaneous bygones who make up the
Conservative party in the southern province.
Most of them have already been tried and
found wanting ; some more should have been
tried only an obliging Attorney-General re-
fused to take proceedings ; and the country
knows hardly any good, in a political sense,
of any of them.
♦ * * *
George Turner is certainly not an ideal
Premier or the head of an ideal Government,
as Australians used to understand that
liazy adjective. The men whom Australia
used to reverence were the “men who made
the country.” Vogel was of that type ; so
were MTlwraith and, in a less degree,
Parkes. Duncan Gillies would have been
of it also if he was a foot taller and several
inches more round the chest, and he had a
huge beard, and was full of bass blasphemy,
and if he had gone off with somebody else’s
wife occasionally ; he had all the qualities of
the men who made the country, save the
large piratical exterior and the swashbuck-
lering habit of mind. Dibbs would have
been another of these gods if he had been in
power ten years earlier. John Forrest, of
Westralia, is the last sample of the race.
The “men who made the country” were a
curse from which Australia will not recover
for many, many years. They w r ere re-
verenced because they opened up Australia
at an amazing rate, built railways and
bridges, constructed roads, made harbours,
and brought the outward signs of civilisation
into a new continent with amazing velocity.
Looking back now, the nation dimly discerns
that all the notable things they really did
were to howl and look imposing and borrow
money. The veriest fool can borrow money
if there are plenty of people willing to lend
it, as was the case then ; and the veriest
fool, when he has borrowed the money, can
hire expert—more or less expert—men to
plan out and construct the railways, roads,
bridges, wharves, harbors, and other tilings.
The qualifications of a great statesman of the
Australian pioneer type were these three :
He had to be masterful and imposing and
hard and pushing and big of voice and chest
to climb into power. He required the
courage to borrow, which everybody has, and
the courage to spend, which every Juggins
has. And he required to be ignorant enough
not to see the disaster which his policy would
bring by-and-bye, or else callous enough not
to care. With a little more economy, more
honesty, more foresight, and a little less de-
sire* to shirk taxation in the early, rich,
golden days, most of the Australias might
have built their early railways out of revenue,
and the income derived from them would
have helped to build more lines, and finally
the whole system could have been con-
structed, more slowly yet quite rapidly
enough, without debt at all. The chief
reason which Australia has to be grateful to
the statesmen who loom so large in its early
history is that they had the biggest oppor-
tunity given to any set of men since the
days of Columbus, and they boomed it away.
* * ♦ *
The useful politician of to-day is a man of
a different order. He exists because the
ideal politician of some years ago made
him absolutely necessary. Ballance was
about the first of the new order.
Turner is another representative of it ;
so is Kingston, though in a less degree.
The Premier who succeeds Forrest
will have to be of the same stamp,
unless Westralia is to be the first defaulter
in the Seven Provinces. The days of the
politician who was great because he furiously
damned the expense are over, for the suffi-
cient reason that the expense declines to be
damned any longer, and the political genius
of to-day is the man with an infinite capacity
for repairs. Among all the many political
reforms required in Australia, and they are
practically innumerable, the first and most
urgent is to straighten up the financial
wreck which the bygone men of mark left
behind them, and to make the Australians a
self-reliant community, instead of a nation
whose very soul is up the spout of the British
pawnbroker. Financial independence is
every whit as important as political inde-
pendence, and because The Bulletin is a
Republican organ it wants to see Australia
dominated neither by a British Government
nor by a British usurer. But if it had to
choose between the two—if it was possible to
get rid of one burden only and not of the
other—then it would be more than half
inclined to choose freedom from the British
money-lender. Salisbury and Chamber-
lain are types of the political boss ; Hooley
and Barnato and the Cohen tribe in general
of the financial one ; and as a suzerain Salis-
bury looks more dignified than Hooley.
* * * *
The present Victorian Ministers have done
more than any other political combination in
Australia to save their country from selling
itself into worse bondage to the tribe of
Hooley, and The Bulletin regards this as
a truly democratic work, and. supports them
on democratic principles. Though they have
never labelled themselves Democrats, they
have worked all through on straighter Radical
principles than any other Government in
Australia. A Ministry with the State Land
Bank, the Companies Act, the income-tax
policy, the Factories Act, and the minimum-
wage law to its credit deserves the best sup-
port the masses can give it, no matter how it
labels itself. It certainly hasn’t done nearly
so much as was expected of it, but that is
mainly owing to the unmanageableness and
the desperate slowness of the political
machine with which it has to work. Probably
the worst charge brought against it is that it
hasn’t once attempted the biggest and most
urgent reform of all—the reformation of the
Legislative Council. Against this must be
set the fact that, unless the House of 48
Rich Landlords chooses to pass an Act for its
own reformation, which it almost certainly
would not do voluntarily, there is no power
short of revolution, or a general attack by an
infuriated populace armed with clubs, which
can shift it. An elective institution whose
members must consist of wealthy landholders,
in a country where wealthy landholders are
few and rigidly Tory, can’t be made Liberal
by any legal means. If the Turner Govern-
ment had spent the last three years in work-
ing up a furious crusade against the House
of Rich Landlords it might, or it might not,
have aroused sufficient national indignation
to force that dreadful body to mend its ways.
But, in that case, all the other work that has
been done would probably have had to be
postponed, and, with so many urgent matters
on hand, it was no fit time for crusading.
* * * *
The present Victorian Government doesn’t
consist of great men, and it is well for the
country that it doesn't; another five years of
really great men of the old Australian type
would reduce the richest province in the
seven to public insolvency. But it is the
best, most honest, and most courageous
Ministry that, so far as the public is able to
see, can be made out of the materials avail-
able. Even if the country were mad enough
to go back to the old crowd which asks to be
restored to power—the Bent and O’Loghlen
and Gillies and M’lntyre and Davies push
—there is no element of cohesion among
them. Their policy consisted only in float-
ing big loans and sitting down to watch the
land-boom as it whizzed past ; and now that
there is no land-boom to watch and the
country’s credit is no longer good enough
for immense loans, there is nothing left that
they can do. The only definite principle
they have is that half-a-dozen of them are
theoretical Freetraders, but as Victoria has
practically no land revenue the Customs
duties are its main source of revenue, and, if
they were abolished, the biggest land-
tax the world has seen in recent years
would be required to fill their place.
And as the Victorian Freetraders—barring
the weird Max Hirsch —are not land-taxers,
the inference is that if the only Opposition
section which has a policy were returned to
power it couldn’t carry out its alleged policy
after all, and wouldn’t attempt to, and
doesn’t want to. Under thebe circumstances,
the present Victorian Government promises
to come back from the poll with a stronger
majority and a surer seat than it even now
possesses. Nothing is absolutely certain in
politics—a profession in which so strange a
person as T. Bent could keep afloat for
many years must have many unaccountable
phases in it—but this seems as certain as
anything in politics reasonably can be.
A Year of Frectrade.
The new Reid tariff came into force—or rather
the earliest instalment of it did—on Ist January,
1896, therefore the period ending 31st Decem-
ber, 1896, was the first complete year of the
new policy. Last week the New South Wales
Statistical Department issued the industrial re-
turns for that year, and the results are only
moderately encouraging. At the same time they
are rather difficult to sort out. Up to the end
of 1895—the last year of Protection—the official
definition of a factory in New South Wales was
a place where five or more workers were em-
ployed under certain conditions, and only such
establishments were reckoned in the official re-
turn. This year the definition has been altered
to include places where four, or even a smaller
numberof workers are employed, and this altera-
tion takes in 4000 extra workers, and confuses
things badly. The change comes just at a time
when it was desirable that the facts should be
more than usually explicit, so that fiscal con
troversialists, hurling jagged masses of statistics
at each other in defence of their respective
faiths, could do so with a clear conscience, and
with as little room as possible for obscuring the
issue. Still, despite the change, and the con-
sequent obscurity, a few points are sufficiently
clear.
* * m m
Going on the old basis there are now 45,222
“ hands ” employed in the factories and works
of New South Wales. This is 1389 more than
were employed last year. Since the great
paralysis in 1893, the year when Australia’s
financial curses came home to roost, there has
been a continuous increase. In 1894 the number
of employes advanced by 3833 ; in 1895 the
improvement was 1082 ; in 1896 the increase, as
above stated, was 1389. The result is that
things are now almost, but not quite, as good
as they were in 1889. Taking the last ten
years, the number of people employed in Corn-
stalk industries increased in 1887, 1889, 1891,
1894-5-6, and it diminished in 1888, 1890, 1892,
and 1893. The net result is that the country
hasn’t quite got back to where it was a long
time ago, but it is gradually getting there, so
far as the number of industrial employes is con-
cerned. Beyond t his there are few possibilities
of accurate comparison. The tables show the
number of persons employed in each industry
in former years on the old basis, and compare
them with those employed in 1896 on the new
basis—which includes 4000 workers of which
the old system took no notice whatever. Still,
a few interesting facts can be dug out here and
there.
* * » *
The new policy of reckoning any place where
four people are employed as a factory increases
the numberof New South Wales boot-factories
from 66 to 82. Nevertheless, the number
of persons employed has decreased from
3743 on the old basis to 3499 on the
much wider new basis, and the number of pairs
manufactured is 150,000 less. Probably if the
old system of comparison had been adhered to
the output would have been 200,000 pairs less.
The brewery employes, despite the new and
more liberal method of computation, have
diminished by 57. There are seven fewer soap
and cindle factories ; 106 fewer employes, and
the output is 149,0001 b. soap and 900,0001 b.
candles less than that for 1895. Woollen mills
employ 10 less hands, and the output has gone
down slightly. The number of employes in the
slop clothing business drops in one act from
1777 to 1255. Sawmill employes declined from
3402 to 3187. There is also a decline in the
number of persons employed in boiling-down
establishments, biscuit factories, meat-pre-
serving works, joinery works, agricultural im-
plement factories, shipbuilding yards, and
among basket-makers, dyers, rope-makers,
tobacco-factory employes, and various others.
In many cases where there is a decline it would
have been larger but for the new method of
computation, and in others where there is a
small increase, there would probably have been
a falling-oft if the old method had been adhered
to. Also, where there is a legitimate increase,
the new system has often made it larger than it
would otherwise have been.
* • » *
There has undoubtedly been an increase ir.
the amount of employment apart from the
artificial inflation created by the change in the
methods of the Statistical Department, but it
is difficult to find out exactly in what industries
it has arisen. The largest visible increases are
in ice-works, brick-making establishments,
stone-quarries,potteries, smelting-works, docks,
printing establishments, and a few others. The
Statistical Department formerly refused to re-
cognise tailors and dress-makers at all; now it
includes them (3592 of them) among factory
employes, and this new departure further dis-
organises comparisons. The number of female
emplojAs, taking both years on the old basis,
has decreased 301, but this is wholly through
the slump in the slop-clothing and boot-factory
business, where 607 women have lost their
means of livelihood. In most other industries
they are either holding their own or increasing
steadily.
* * * •
Ihe one point on which the industrial
statistics are absolutely coherent is the rate of
wages. Apart from miners, whose industry
depends not on tariffs but mainly on the price
of metals in the European markets, and Govern-
ment employ6s who are also practically
independent of tariffs, the Government
Statistician fills nine pages with informa-
tion about the rates of pay in every
kind of private industry. Among the
trades enumerated there are 73 in which wages
were higher in 1896, the first year of the new
Freetrade era, than in 1895, the last year of
Protection. There are also 161 in which wages
are lower, so the balance, in this respect, is dis-
tinctly against the new order of things so far.
The most notable reductions are in boiling-
down establishments, tanneries, and wool-
washing establishments, among aerated-water
makers, confectioners, condiment - makers,
flour-millers, and ice-makers ; in jam, meat,
boot, clothing and waterproof factories ; in
woollen-mills, and among shirt-makers, brick-
makers, joiners, saw-millers, masons, carpen-
ters, machinists, bricklayers, painters, tin-
smiths, many classes of engineers, furniture
makers, electric-lighting people, box and brush
makers, and shop assistants. In one or two of
these industries the wages of an adult Cornstalk
Christian (sex not always specified, but presum-
ably female) are quoted as low as Bs. per
week—in one instance ss. is mentioned ; in a
good many they go as low as 10s. The wages
for adult males get down as low as Bs. per
week ; there are several instances where 12s. is
quoted ; £1 is quite common ; in one solitary
instance where adult males are apparently
alluded to (for boys and girls are specified
separately) 4s. is mentioned as the minimum
wage. New South Wales industries—some of
them, at all events—may be increasing under
Freetrade, but it seems to be only done bv
cutting down wages to a miserably low figure.
* * * *
It is a frequent assertion among Freetraders
that Protection claims to raise the standard of
wages and fails to do it; but as far as the facts
just issued by the State Statistician go, Free-
trade, in New South Wales at all events, has
little to boast of in the same direction. It is
also a common argument that Protection should
justify its claims in this direction by establish-
ing a legally-specified minimum wage—a living
wage in a liberal acceptation of the term—and
if protected industries can’t afford to pay it
they should go, and the discredited fiscal
system which supported them should go also.
The Bulletin, in view of the revelations in
the 1896 statistics, suggests that Freetrade—
which claims to be richer and better able to
stand the racket than Protection—should also
be handicapped, per act of Parliament, with
the obligation to pay a living wage to the
people employed in its industries, and if it can’t
stand the shock that it should go also. Appar-
ently the Freetrade industries of New South
Wales want a minimum wage law very badly
indeed.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tillett on Federation.
Ben Tillett, the British Labor representative,
loses no opportunity to damn the Federation
movement. And yet he damns it very feebly,
as witness the following remarks in an Adelaide
interview :
I consider that the “ second Chamber ” difficulty and
the voting difficulty, and also the demand for the posses-
sion of certain repressive powers, constitute the Common-
wealth Bill a most undemocratic measure. It seems to
me to be rather a collusion on the part of the present
governing classes that accentuate class-differences as
well as the economic injustices that have been inflicted
upon the people. . . . I do not think they will gain
anything. I think they will lose. It seems to me that
even the best advocates of the measure have frittered
away their time, talents, opportunities, and speeches on
mere fiscal rather than domestic politics. The working-
classes, it may safely be said, are indifferent to the whole
matter. They have not been consulted. The elections
were a triumph for cliqueism, from a workman’s stand-
point. I venture to say that, if the Commonwealth Bill
in its present form is submitted to a referendum of the
people, that it will be rejected by ten to one. The Com-
monwealth Bill should be a constructive measure, and
devote itself to the building of a constitution and the
machinery of government that shall be representative of
the people in such a democratic manner that everybody
with an interest in the country shall be able to’ avail
themselves of its machinery But, instead of making the
constitution democratic, every effort seems to have been
made to rob the citizens and people of Australia of the
power to express their opinions by the ordinary methods
of representative government and election. The efforts
of the leading lights seem to have been directed in the
interests of mere property, of mere fiscal matters, and of
mere party shuffling.
The Convention represented five provinces, with
3,160,000 people. In three provinces, repre-
senting 2,838,000 people, the delegates were
elected on such popular principles that the male
population, almost to the last man, could vote
if it'wanted to. In one of these provinces, the
last woman could have a vote also. For years
beforehand the question was debated, talked,
preached, written and lectured about ;


every possible effort was made to rouse popular
enthusiasm, and the work of consulting the
working classes was going on incessantly. In
the Convention the ten nominee members from
Westralia left very early, and the business was
almost entirely done by four provinces—the
three where the elections were conducted on
democratic principles and one other—Tasmania.
When the Constitution is really drafted—if it
ever is—it is to be submitted to a popular vote,
and the working classes will again have every
opportunity to express their opinion upon it.
Under these circumstances Mr. Tillett might
explain how, in the three principal provinces
anyhow, these classes could have been con-
sulted more than they have been. Mr. Tillett,
apparently, holds a brief for the parochial
party—the fatted Heydon “push ” in N.S.W.,
the Six-Family crowd of Westralia, and the
Nelson-Tozer assemblage in Queensland —
though how he came into such company it is
difficult to imagine. And his objections to the
proposed Constitution are the vague utterances
of an oracle that doesn’t know its own meaning.
He doesn’t say what “repressive powers” are
in the Draft Bill, or in what possible way it
will “accentuate class differences as well as
economic in justices,” or what “ domestic poli-
tics ” should have been embodied. Also, the
large, loose statement that the Commonwealth
Bill “should be a constructive measure
and devote itself to the building of a
constitution and the machinery of government
that shall be representative of the people in
such a democratic manner that everybody, with
an interest in the country, shall be able to avail
themselves of its machinery,” is a mere
nebulous mass of meaningless polysyllables. If
fhe British labor authority would come down
out of these vasty wastes of language and say
exactly what kind of a constitution he thinks
would be a fair thing, how the working-classes
should be consulted, what are the “ repressive
measures,” where the efforts of the constitu-
tion-makers have been directed in the interests
of “ mere property,” how the elections were a
“ triumph of cliqueism,” and a few other
things, he might do some good. With refer-
ence to Federal matters he seems to be a very
hazy windbag, who does his level best to
damage the cause by saying, in long words like
Mesopotamia and Mahershalalhashbaz, that
things are wrong, without indicating where
they are wrong or how they should be made
right.

Unseaworthy Foreigners.
“ A Barrister ” to The Bulletin :
“Phillis,” a “ British ship,”on the ground of its alleged
nnseaworthiness, has drawn attention to a curiousbutvery
proper provision of the Marine Act. It is to the effect
that when a ship has been detained under the Act, she
shall not be released by reason of her ceasing to be a
British ship. As the Phillis has ceased to be a British
ship on account of her sale to a Norwegian since the de-
tention, and as the Yio. authorities refuse to allow her to
have the chance of drowning her crew, the provision in
question is likely to come very prominently before the
public shortly. At first sight it would appear that Vic.
Parliament was totally incompetent to pass such a sec-
tion, dealing as it does with what, in law, is, in this case,
a piece of Norwegian territory ; but as the Act has received
Royal assent as a reserved bill, and as an exactly similar
provision was contained in a British Merchant Ship-
ping Act of 76, it may be taken that the provision is not
ultra vires, but is an assertion by Britain of her right
to compel foreigners sailing from British ports to do so
only in safe and seaworthy vessels. It does not say so
except so far as ships which have been previously detained
as British ships are concerned; but in saying so with
respect to them, it implies the power and the willingness,
if properly urged, to say so with respect to all foreign
ships. Now, this is a power which might very properly
be taken to herself by the alleged “ mistress of the seas,”
and in the interests of both our own and foreign sailors
should be urgently demanded by unions, philanthropic
associations, and other bodies interested in the welfare
of seamen. If the wise English provisions relating to
seaworthiness were made to apply to all ships in Austra
lian and other British waters, we should earn the grati-
tude of imperfectly-protected thousands of foreign sea-
men, to say nothing of the removal of a large part of the
present tendency to make bogus transfers of ships to
other flags in order to escape the humane provisions of
the British Acts. To do this, of course, would be to offend
foreign nations, and to raise diplomatic complications. But
the British Parliament has already studied that phase of
the question or it would not have passed the clause
alluded to. It passed the .clause well knowing that it
meant “ Foreign nations be damned ! We are not going
to let sailors set out in coffin-ships ! ” It is quite possible
that, despite the clear wording of the clause in question,
the Vic. authorities may be bluffed into caving in. But
in such a matter as this, even if the provision were
unconstitutional, there is a clear moral duty on Victoria
to act humanely in a matter like this, and there need be
no fear that if she does so act she will have the civilised
world behind her. Anyhow, there’s the Act, and if Scan-
dinavia doesn’t like it, let her send her navy and rescue
the Phillis from the grip of the Vic. people. British
legislation applicable to the colonies* is generally a
reminder of our dependence, but we’ll be willing to put
up with that a little longer if it is used, as it can be, by
extending such a provision as the one under consideration
for the benefit of the shamefully-abused sailor-folk of
Britain and Europe.
A Federal Customs Swindle.
The position of New South Wales, as the one
Freetrade province of Australia, raises a com-
plex problem which will have to be faced in the
first year of the Commonwealth. Until the
Commonwealth tariff is enacted, the separate
provincial duties will naturally remain in force.
The day the Federal tariff comes into force,
border Customs will cease throughout the
Federation, also Customs duties on goods carried
by sea from one Federal port to another.
Therefore, when the date comes near, specula-
tive merchants will probably pour into Sydney,
and store up there, some millions of pounds’
worth of all kinds of goods which are free
under the N.S. W. tariff. The day the Federal
tariff eventuates the free influx will be stopped.
But, as there will then be no more border
Customs, these goods will be sent by rail
or ship all over Australia, and while the
accumulation lasts they will do much damage
to a number of manufacturing industries.
Also, as this accumulation will pay no duty, it
will make a ghastly hole in the first year’s
Federal Customs revenue ; in quite a number
of lines there will probably be next to no
imports at all, and as a result practically no
Customs revenue, for months. If theN.S.W.
merchants alone did the trick it would matter
less, as the profits wmuld at least remain in the
country, but the market will probably be also
swamped by speculative consignments from
traders in England, Germany, America, and
elsewhere. Another complication is that Mel-
bourne, Adelaide and Tasmanian merchants,
when the Federal tariff is about due, will pro-
bably export all they ban spare of their stock
of duty-paid goods to Sydney, collect drawback
on them at the port of shipment, land them
free in Sydney, and then, when the tariff comes
in force and interprovincial duties are abolished,
ship them back again to their own cities and
land them free as goods imported from a place
inside the Commonwealth. This will mean
another loss of Customs revenue, and no one has
yet presented any promising remedy. Premier
Turner proposes that N.S. W. should adopt a
Protective tariff a year before the Federation
begins, but, as the N.S.W. majority intends
instead doing its best to make the whole
Commonwealth Freetrade, this idea is natu-
rally received with a whoop of derision.
Another suggestion, which also finds little
favor, is to maintain the interprovincial Customs
for a year after the Federal tariff commences,
so as to confine the speculative importer to
New South Wales. Probably the best scheme
of all is to let the speculative importer do his
worst. At the very worst, the Federation will
get over the shock in a few months, and it is a
swindle that can only be perpetrated once.
Nothing great can be done without something
being broken in the doing, or some fraud hap-
pening by way of a side-issue, and Premier
Turner’s inclination to make a large stumbling-
block of this matter is a narrow and parochial
proceeding.
An Odorous Political Matter.
“ Lictor ” from Kalgoorlie, on the fishy Hain-
ault affair :
Dear Bulletin,— Be Hainault. When the bill to re-
habilitate the Hainault lease went before W.A. Upper
House last week, the President ruled that, as private
interests were involved, a select committee should be
appointed to enquire into those interests. Government
accordingly “selected” five members, three of whom are
remarkable for little except their strong J. Bull tenden-
cies, the famous W.A. Minister for Mines also being one.
Although these persons were “selected” to “ take evi-
dence, call for papers,” &c., and enquire into the merits
of the claimants, they didn’t ask to hear those whom
even the Attorney-General called “ the present holders”
—the men who hold the ground under miners’-rights;
and all the “papers” they read were the evidence taken
before the warden at Kalgoorlie, in a case where the
miners’-rights men objected to their ground being granted
to Rutherford, who had since passed out of the conten-
tion. In the case referred to the only evidence brought
forward by the miners’-rights men was what they con-
sidered necessary to oust Rutherford ; no question was
taken as to the position of the Hainault Co., since that
matter was not before the Court. And yet it was on the
reading of this evidence that the committee framed its
report advising the House to pass the Bill; and passed it
was accordingly. Next day (Wednesday) the Premier
moved in the Assembly that the Standing Orders be sus-
pended to allow the Bill to be passed through all its
stages in a single sitting. This course, however, met
with strong opposition. Members urged that, as the case
was then before the Supreme Court, the matter should
be left to that tribunal. The “indecent haste ” manifested
was warmly condemned. The Premier ‘ ‘ assu red the House ”
that the Hainault rent had been paid before forfeiture of
the lease, but that the amount had been credited to an-
other lease through “ an error in the department” ; also
that the clerk who had received the rent “ could not be
found just then.” He did not say that the books had dis-
appeared also!!!!!!! Mr. Moran had given notice of
motion to produce the books and papers, but his request
was simply brushed aside. On division, the House refused
to supply the majority necessary to suspend the standing
orders. Next day (Thursday) there was to be a two-hours’
sitting, and the House was then to be prorogued—sug-
gestive word! During the Wednesday night the
Minister interviewed honorable members. Next morn-
ing, the House met, good-humored jokes were passed
from side to side ; the Premier thanked the Opposi-
tion in anticipation for what they were about, to do ;
the arguments of the previous night against “ indecent
haste ” were forgotten ; no further discussion took place ;
the bill was passed through all its stages in a few
minutes, and is now law. The Premier had previously
promised the House that “reasonable compensation”
should be offered “the jumpers,” and he has since
tendered them £5OO and costs —this, after collaring
from them a property worth £lOO,OOO. Hon. mem-
bers played to J. Bull in the gallery, and spoke
earnestly' about “ honor and good faith” —very suggestive
of a man singing a hymn while running off with the
church-plate. The Government laid great stress on their
assertion that the forfeiture of the Hainault lease had
occurred through “an error in the department,” as if
that were quite sufficient to justify them in evading their
own responsibilities. The same argument would apply to
a big railway smash, and probably will some da.y. As to
whether the Hainault rent was paid in time—which affects
the equitable, though not the legal, aspect of the case—
I think I can guess under which thimble the pea is.
Anyway, the State is liable, and some day there may be
a Parliament that recognises the moral responsibility of
the province—a responsibility that will remain till justice
be done. Meanwhile, J. Bull is looked to for gratitude ;
but I question whether he will see the matter that way.
One “argument” used in the House against “the
jumpers” was that they were “speculators.” What
about the Hainault Co. itself, which ran the shares up to
£4, then down to 55., and now up to 30s. ? What about
hon. members themselves, who speculate in shares and
beef, &c. ? What about their own J. Bull himself ?
The Worm that Turned.
Nobody was hurt but everybody was a good
deal scared by the bomb which William
Stephens, M.L.A., exploded, the other day, in
Queensland Assembly. The Stephens episode
was full of anomalies. That amateur dyna-
miter is generally a man of peace, a vigorous
Government supporter, and a hard, consistent
follower of- the mild and the commonplace. He
is understood to have advocated the appointment
of the recent gamey Land Commission, and the
fact that he didn’t get a billet on it surprised
everybody—probably including Stephens him-
self. Despite this disappointment he re-
mained a cordial supporter of the firm of
Tozer and Co. The Land Commission lived,
and smelled badly, and drew its fees, and died,
and was buried, and still Stephens, M.L.A.,
made no sign. On this particular night he was
acting as Government whip, and was the same
consistent admirer of Tozer and co. as of yore
—right up to the last moment. Then an
astonished House saw its patient Gibeonite get
up, apropos of absolutely nothing, and denounce
the Commission as a “clumsy, wilful, and un-
dignified attempt at political corruption.”
Having uttered this roar, he waited for
Speaker Cowley to interfere, but as
Cowley was too astonished. to move,
Stephens kept heaving the same mass of
language at his head, till at last he had to sit
up and tell the hon. member to stop. Then
Stephens repeated his language, and Acting-
Premier Tozer, being brought in apparently
against his will, moved that the worm which
had turned should be “ suspended ” for 24
hours. The House voted against Tozer, so it
either meant that it endorsed Stephens’ re-
marks about political corruption, or that it
approved of the spectacle on general principles,
and wanted the row to continue. Then Tozer
sat down under the insult for some days, and
apparently he would be sitting down
neacefully under it yet, only the organs
of his party told him be must rise
and repudiate the charge, and clear his
character, and so forth. Soafter a week’s interval
the Government rallied up its majority one
evening and voted that Stephens’ charges were
wholly unfounded. Stephens said nothing on
this occasion, barring a few interjections ; he
wasn’t ejected or suspended or anything; the
Government simply passed a motion as quietly
as possible declaring its own spotless purity,
and it didn’t harry the worm that turned into
any serious attempt to prove his statements.
And why its long and patient supporter, after
keeping the peace for nine years, got up and
exploded all in an instant as he did has never
been explained.

Ben Tippett’s account of that “Queen’s
Health ” episode, at the Melb. Trades Hall
banquet, is not very satisfactory. At Ballarat,
last week, little Benjamin said : “ When the
loyal toast was proposed everybody thought the
toast was to him. When the thing was even-
tually understood he spoke with the greatest
respect and heartiness, because the Queen was a
woman and a mother. He rallied the company
in the cheeriest words possible, and as he was
resuming his seat he said, ‘Yes, let us
drink to the Queen or any other old
lady.’” Isn’t this a weak evasion of the
main point? The Queen was toasted as a
monarch, not a mother. Tippett should have
stood at the time, and should stand now, on his
right to be always antagonistic to the Queen in
her much-toasted capacity. If Tippett is
superior to the folly of Queen-worship, which is
the religion of the classes who over-rule the
masses, he can’t honestly revere the Heir
Apparent’s mother. As for her being a woman,
well, she comes naturally into the unpolitical
toast of “ The Ladies, Gorblessem ! ”
The proposal to leave the financial question to
be settled by the Federal Parliament rouses
John Haynes, M.L.A., to fury. “Our cup is
full,” wails John in a letter to the Herapd ;
“ The people of N.S.W. are to surrender to a
Parliament in which they must stand as a
minority as of four to one such questions as the
finances, the capital, the tariff, the railways.”
The poor provincial John can’t realise that the
other provinces also have to surrender exactly
the same questions, and each one of them will
be in a worse minority, so far as representation
is concerned, than N.S.W. If five provinces
federate it would seem that they must all be in a
minority of four to one—just as John Haynes,
while he lives in N.S.W., must be in a minority
of 1,300,000 to one. Yet the huge majority
has never taken advantage of its power to
oppress the minority of one Haynes, or passed
special legislation against him, and it is just
possible that the Federal majority will be
equally fair in its dealings.
In the case of David Feapy, solicitor, of
Sydney, who was recently called upon to
answer allegations which, if they were true,
amounted to a charge of subornation of perjury,
N.S. Wales Supreme Court gave a very un-
satisfactory decision. David Feapy, though
tacitly relieved of the burden of the accusa-
tions against him, was “ not exonerated alto-
gether,” and was ordered to pay all costs.
That is, the Supreme Court shirked the specific
question whether the statements made by
Griffen, who alleged that he perjured him-
self at Feapy’s bidding, were true or not; and
merely decided that Feapy acted foolishly.
And now—what is going to happen to Mr.
Griffen, who, according to his own affidavits,
is guilty of perjury? In the public interest,
Mr. Griffen should be taken at his word.
Ben Tippett’s principal financial theory seems
to be that the Fat Men voluntarily broke the
banks in ’93 for sinister purposes of gain.
“ Aafor the breaking of the banks, that was an
old dodge of the capitalists,” is one of his
favorite phrases. Everyone has realised by
this time that the capitalists wrecked their
own banks by running a boom that was pure
lie and steal and robbery. But the idea that
they wanted to break their own banks and stop
receiving dividends, and that any capitalist
deliberately laid himself out to make the sale-
value of his shares go down from many pounds
to nothing, and that Munro was yearning to
sacrifice his big pile and his Agent-General-
ship, and Matthew Davies schemed to lose his
Speakership and his palace at Toorak, and old
C. H. James played a deep game to drop his
estate and be struck for £175,000 calls, is very,
very steep. The capitalist in many cases is
plain, unvarnished ass, and of such was the
Australian bank-boomer in very many cases.
But the school to which Tippett belongs re-
gards him with child-like faith as an intellectual
demi-god of the worst kind, and as it can’t
understand how he could have been such a
fool as he was just before ’93 it assumes that he
broke his banks voluntarily so as to make
money by losing his property, and being sold up,
and—in some few cases—getting into gaol. The
capitalist came out all right, according to
Tippett, though “ the workers lost all their
little savings.” But the capitalist was struck
by a demand for seven millions in calls, and out
of the £90,000,000 of little savings which were
“re-constructed” the biggest part were his
own. He was only a blundering, clumsy,
mendacious, grabbing fool, after all, and when
his institutions came down on top of his thick
head no one was more astonished than himself.
In most cases he hadn’t even the sense to see
that they were coming down till it was too late
to escape with a decent amount of his plunder.
Charpes Happ, the Eaglehawk miner, made a
desperate attempt to dodge the hangman by
“confessing” that, he only half-murdered his
wife. Happ’s narrative stated that he first of
all ducked his poor little woman’s head in the
fatal tub. “ Her head went under the water.
She got her head out, but she fell back again.
She again got her head out, but again fell
back.” Then Happ — vide “ confession ”
lifted the wife of his bosom from out the tub,
and she rolled over on the floor. He told the
inanimate body that she had better go back to
bed now that her temper was cooled, and after
imparting this wholesome advice Mr. Happ
went into the kitchen to dry his hands. Re-
turning to the bedroom he was surprised
to find his wife lying where he had
left her. The narrative goes on to re-
cord sundry appealing exclamations uttered
by Hall when he found his wife getting no
better. At last, said the confessing penitent,
“ the terrible idea rushed to my mind that she
was dead, and I cursed myself for my silly
action. . . . Knowing that she had been in the
habit of bathing in the tub, I decided to carry
her out and place her in the tub to make it
appear she had been bathing.” It was a poor
enough yarn, Heaven knows! yet it might
have sufficed to save Hall’s neck if his
beloved barmaid hadn’t previously told a Judge
and jury that the man prattled of wife-murder,
in connection with the tub, weeks before the
fatality came to pass. So the statement which
he laboriously put together in his dungeon cell
merely served the moral purpose of proving,
beyond question, that it was Hall’s best girl
who brought him to the gallows. A (married)
man can’t be too careful in the choice of a best
girl.
Australian “ disloyalty ” may discover food
for meditation in the case <?f one Wilfrid
Charles Rundle, a London solicitor, who was
sentenced to a month’s imprisonment with hard
labor for venting his Jubilee feelings upon a
democratic fellow-citizen. The fellow-citizen
didn’t remove his hat whilst the Jubilee-day
crowd was singing the Hanthem, therefore
Rundle took the law of grovel into his own
hands and smote the stranger on his disloyal
jaw and other places. Rundle, on being
brought before a magistrate, was found guilty
of an unprovoked assault, and he had to do his
month’s oakum-picking in Her Majesty’s gaol.
Supposing that jubilee time were still with us,
what would happen to a solicitor who fell upon
an inoffensive stranger for not baring his head
to “ Gorsave ” in the streets of Sydney or Mel-
bourne ? In the first place, it would be hard to
get the local Wilfrid arrested by the police.
His victim would have to proceed against him
on summons, and meanwhile the victim afore-
said, if it were a Melb. case, would pro-
bably find himself charged with insulting
behaviour. Anyway, Wilfrid would have
the sympathy of the “respectable” daily
press, and Fat Man would intervene on
his behalf, and when the Jubileetic bench had
fined him, say, 405., Wilfrid would march
from court in triumph with his friends to the
vocal strains of “ God Save the Queen.” A
lawyer, in Sydney or Melbourne, is a protected
person who never, or hardly ever, gets sen-
tenced to hard labor for any sort of offence.
Certainly it would be impossible for a battered
republican to enforce the law against a lawyer
in any case where he could plead “ outraged
loyalty ”as an excuse. And this is the point
for local republican sentiment to feel annoyed
about. Australia with its manhood suffrage,
and its labor members, and many other out-
ward professions of democracy, is a less demo-
cratically governed country than England,
where enthusiasm for the Throne is an occasional
weakness rather than a confirmed habit. In
England your (or the other man’s) political
opinions or social rank can help you precious
little when once you get within the grasp of
the law. It is hard, very hard, to get out of
gaol in England.
Vic. Leg. Council made another attempt to
graft a hampering growth of notices of action
and notices of accident on to the poor man’s
rights of action (such as they are) against his
employer. The Vic. Regulation of Mines Act
contained the first provision in the English
legal world, freeing the workman (in mines)
from the operation of the iniquitous and Judge-
concocted doctrine of “common employment,”
and compelled the mineowner to rebut the
presumption of negligence which arose when-
ever a mining accident occurred. This law is
practically ancient history in Victoria. But on
its being consolidated in the present Mines Bill,
the Council inserted conditions similar to those
that make th'e Employers Liability Acts prac-
tically worthless to the poor and those ignorant
of the technicalities of notices of action. And
it was only the fear of their congeners in the
Assembly suffering severely in the mining dis-
tricts in the coming elections that forced them
to let the law remain as it is.
Any lingering doubts as to the law being made
for lawyers, rather than litigants, are now
swept away by the committal of one Nicholls
on a charge of bigamy, heard at Melbourne
City Court last week. The case is probably
unique in matrimonial annals. Nicholls,
having obtained a decree nisi of divorce from
his wife, didn’t settle-up with his solicitor, who
—in the sweet, ordinary way of legal practice—
held the power to take out” the decree after it
was granted, said power being held practically
as security for costs. When Nicholls put in
a formal application “on his own” for his
divorce to be made absolute, the Judge refused
him his legal rights, inasmuch as the receipted
bill of costs was not forthcoming. In short,
the Judge ruled that Nicholls must remain
married to his wife (and she to him, of course)
until he liquidated his lawyer’s claim. A further
application by Nicholls to the Full Court
met with a like refusal. In March
last, which was long after the time when he
would have been absolutely divorced but for
the trouble re costs, Nicholls married again,
whereupon “somebody” laid an information
against the man, and the Crown is now trying
to “pot” him for bigamy. It doesn’t trans-
pire that the original Mrs. Nicholls is married
again on the strength of her husband’s decree
nisi, but she may have found a Number Two,
and in that case the non-payment of his law-
yer’s bill renders her likewise liable to prose-
cution and imprisonment. The Judge who
tries Nicholls for bigamy will surely be bound
to uphold the Full Court’s ruling. The Law
was made for lawyers, and when a client’s inno-
cence or guilt turns upon the question of his
neglect to feed a hungry “shark” the Law
holds him to be about the deepest-dyed criminal
on earth. A hanging of Nicholls by special
Act of Parliament would not surprise The
Bulletin.

Nobody by any chance came along and
opened a new drama or comedy at Sydney this
week. New dramas or comedies are about as rare
as hens’ teeth nowadays, or as the anthem of the
alligator. Barring variety, and burlesque, and
things with song and dance in them, and edu-
cated mules, and other side issues of the ham-
frill order, there has been practically nothing new
m Sydney for about half-a-year. The theatrical
business just now is all lace and insertion and ice-
cream and high-kick and fritters and other unsub-
stantial things, and if somebody would only arrive
with a good co., and a perfectly new drama
m four indigestible acts and one big explosion, it
would fill a want as big as Mount Kosciusko.
Sydney begins to long for something it can get its
teeth into—not merely a mouthful of flounces,
therefore the promised dramatic season at the
Lyceum comes as a welcome change. Alfred
Dampier opens the ball on Saturday with a very
short spell of “Robbery Under Arms,” which
isn’t exactly new—still it and Dampier together
should crowd the building for their short stay.
This is understood to be the old hero’s farewell to
Sydney, and for the sake of the many good pieces
he has played, and the many good things he has
done, and the better and pleasanter times which
he recalls, he ought to get a goodly welcome. A
new co. and a huge military drama follow Dampier,
and as the prices vary from 2s. to fid., the public
should be able to raise the necessary amo’uit by
leaving the flat-iron at the Mont de Pi4te.
** * *
Maggie Moore’s last week at Sydney Royal
is devoted to the immortal “Struck Oil,” the
ancient city of Oilville being erected on Saturday
night in the presence of a good audience. Harry
Roberts, with the record bad cold on his chest and
in a voice that was reduced to a whisper, dragged
John Stofel manfully, and the latest oil-
strike was a notable success. To-morrow (Friday)
afternoon Miss Vivianne Dagmar, formerly of
the luckless Elsie Adair combination, is to have a
benefit at the Royal. The disaster which struck
that lamented Criterion show and knocked it out
in one round left this lady a beautiful but badly-
stranded stranger in a land which hasn’t even a
reliable sand-shifting job to offer the stranger just
.now; so an effort—which, it is hoped, will be a
highly successful one—is being made to give her a
lift. After the close of Miss Moore’s season the
Royal will be vacant for a fortnight, when George
Rignold takes possession with “In the Ranks.”
** * *
The French Maid,” afc Her Majesty’s,
progresses as indicated in last issue. It is less
substantial than the “ Gay Parisienne ” which pre-
ceded it, inasmuch as the “Parisienne” started
with a definite incident for a foundation, and the
people kicked off each other’s hats for certain
understandable reasons, whereas the “Maid ” is a
cyclone of skirts at the commencement, and re-
mains at that all through, with no visible motive
ft all. The characters simply run to comic riot
because they darned well please to do so. Perhaps
a burlesque is as well without a motive ; it is such
a fluffy insurrection that anything so heavy and
concrete as a reason seems about as much out of
-»lace as a mangle would be. Some of the melodies
from the “Maid” are already beginning to be
howled in the streets, which is a sign that the pro-
duction has caught on. There is a faint whiff of
'he briny deep in it, supplied per medium of the
moral admiral and the British sailors, and some
of the songs are reminiscent of “Pinafore”
The only really painful' thing in the two acts is a
new song by Ernest Shand, with a refrain which
isserts, without any reliable affidavit to support
it, that we “all take off our hats to the Queen.”
Taking off the hat is supposed to be a relic of a
more ancient custom according to which the loyal
subject took everything off in the presence of his
monarch ; but that is a mere detail. Anyhow,
4 he less said about this melancholy Jubilee gibber
fhe better. This country is full of undigested
Jubileo already.

Two new people, Charles Dillon and Dolly
Eicon, made a mild impression at the Tivoli this
week, but the principal support of that establish-
ment is still the Selbinis, who have hitherto kept
tome hair-raising bicycle feats in reserve, and are
now letting them loose on a staggered planet.
The Palace show goes on as heretofore, barring
the fact that sweet Lilian Tree has been added to
fhe company. Evidently Australia hasn’t for-
gotten its Lilian, for on her appearance the house
blew most of the buttons off its joint and several
weskit with enthusiasm, and when she sang the
“Song that reached my Heart” she plainly reached
the horsey, cricketing', football-corroded soul of
her audience right enough. The rolling years
haven’t damaged Lilian much, and have left no
footprints on her voice, which is as bright and
bell like as ever. There are many kinds of bell,
including the dinner-bell in our boarding-house,
but the above remark is meant kindly, anyhow.

A thoroughly enjoyable “ function ” is the
N.S.W. Art Society’s annual smoke. Last Satur-
day night’s shivoo was a huge success. The whole
affair was frank, honest, and unconventional.
The usual programme was gone through, with,
mayhap, more than the usual success. The
society is to be congratulated upon having as
President a real, live working artist in the person
of bluff William Lister Lister, and no frilled ham-
joint of a Society man. Think of it, ye cripples
of colonial Mayfair, Lister was actually asked to
retire his claims in favor of—Captain Ferguson,
the Governor’s son-in-law. Reverting to Saturday
night, the event of the evening was undoubtedly
the rendition by Mr. Alfred Hill of his own song,
‘‘The Volunteer Soldier,’’ re-written from Kip-
ling’s verses. Mr. Hallewell and Herr Staedtgen,
and Major Wells, and the Feldwick quartet sang
with generous frequency ; Messrs. Perry, Leon
Pole, G. Taylor, A. R. Coffey, Percy Spence,
Salvana, and others, sketched ; Mr. Edgar Strauss
’eelloed ; and Messrs. Lawrence Campbell and
Harry Leston spoke things ; and everybody else
looked on and smoked, and drank, and applauded.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s, the failure of
“ Too Much Johnson ” was succeeded on Saturday
by a rather gigantic success for “Niobe.” A half-
filled house took so kindly to the Paulton-Stanley
combination in this instance that the management
straightway announced “Niobe” for six nights,
instead of three, and if the tide of Australian
prejudice against stolid Paulton and his versatile
companion in neglect can be turned by their pre-
sent performance “Niobe” will carry them into
popularity. Harry Paulton was part, and prin
cipal part, author of the joke. He fitted the
character of Petramos Dunn to himself, and
played it for two years at a stretch. Paulton
interprets the author’s meaning, which now
appears to have been extravagantly cari-
catured by Brough. The other Petramos was
amusing in this part. Paulton’s matter-of-fact
style will seem more amusing to such as find
humor in it, and the humor will be found by all
who feel so disposed. Miss Alma Stanley, as the
reanimated mother of a murdered family, is a
majestiCj hugesome matron, with a sweet voice
and an irresistible pair of arms, and a general
capacity for lengthening and breadthening the
pretty character that Mrs. Brough introduced to
Australia. The new Niobe is the more striking
figure, without doubt. Not quite such an artless,
girly creation as Mrs. Brough’s was, but artless
enough considering her family trials in the long
ago. The real Greek lady was not a spring
chicken, anyway, and her arms were probably
inferior to the outstretched limbs of the Alma
Mater who hailed Petramos Dunn’s relatives on
Saturday night. Cyril Keightley is about the
best item in a weak cast of minor characters, but
“ Niobe ” is a comedy built for two, and the other
people’s shortcomings make no difference in the
result. “In a Locket ” will follow.
