“ The Bookmaker,” at the Bijou, has
been airing his good ’eart and his ’ossiness to
large audiences for the past week or so Thorn-
ton is a favorite under any circumstances, and the
impossible turf-leech in the check-suit makes a
direct appeal to Melbourne’s particular weakness
when he tells the tale of ’ow he onst lifted a norse
fust past the post by a nose, and thereby saved
from destruction a young gent, wot was agoin’
for the gloves. The glamor of that youthful
achievement envelops Thornton’s performance.
For the sake of his having swung his orse round
a nasty turn on two legs a grateful public is ready
to forgive him much. “The Bookmaker” might
have lasted for another week, but the season is
ebbing fast, and “Sweet Lavender” will be
staged on Saturday.
****-
“ In the Ranks,” revived at Mclb. Royal
on Saturday last, is likely to stay there for some
little time. The city has not been overdosed with
this old reliable show—in fact, a large portion of
the city has never yet taken any of Sims’ best
melodrama, and the effect of it, when taken for
the first time, is much better than the average
person anticipated. “In the Ranks ”is not a
garish ewlogy of British valor in foreign parts,
where all the “rebellious” niggers die fighting
for their native precipice, and the only loss on the
Hinglish side is a young recruit who writes a fare-
well message to mother on the colonel’s shirt-cuff.
George Rignold wears a scarlet coat simply
because he can’t afford to buy a plain, ordinary
garment, and he requires the Queen’s uniform to
keep out the cold. As a soldier, he dots nothing
remarkable, beyond escaping from baira ks on a
visit of condolence to his long-lost bride, or some-
thing to that effect. Ultimately, he is bought out
of the army by a penitent uncle'from India, and a
scowling Gideon, who has been heaping calamity
on his head through four long acts, is at last over-
thrown. Also, an apocryphal father of George—
a shabby, unprincipled interloper, with a thirst
that never fails —is proved to be another
parent altogether. “In the Hanks ” con-
tains a pleasing infusion of rural scenery
and a long list of characters, some of
whom pass just a smart remark or two, and go
away as suddenly as they arrived. George
Rignold s robust presence, in the hero capacity,
would be improved by a touch of hair-dye, per-
haps, for then he wouldn’t look as though Gideon
were bringing his grey locks in sorrow to the grave.
Miss Lilian Wheeler is an adorable Ruth through-
out; «J. F. Cathcart plays a strong scene with
Rignold in the first act; Lawrence Dunbar makes
a neat, unaffected captain, and Hazlitt a whole-
some variety of villain, whilst useful work is done
very well by Frank Hawthorne, Stirling White,
and others too numerous too mention. Altogether
a level, easy-going performance of a reasonable
plot.

The two or three latest additions to the
Opera House bill were well received on Saturday,
and the new company at the Alexandra hang on
to their convict drama as though it were paying
expenses, which it probably isn’t. Nevertheless,
they are a deserving company at the price.
Bracy’s Opera Co., at latest, held the
boards at Perth fW. A ) Theatre Royal, where
“ Beggar Girl,” “Bohemian Girl,” and “Sultan
of Mocha” had been staged to crowded houses,
with Martha,’ “ Nemesis,” and other good
things, to follow. The shapely chorus and ballet
have caused quite a flutter of excitement among
Perth johnnies and remittance-men in general.
Even the typhoid germs were to be heard whistling
the breeziest airs from “The Girl ” and “The
Student,’ likewise the town councillors have
laid up ” all their “ deep draining ” and money-
borrowing schemes until the co. has started back
to the East.
At Poverty Point.
The two clever Majeronis, Mario, the dark and stupen-
dous, who is remembered in Sydney for his clever acting
in a number of small parts, and his rather less stupendous
brother George, are to join the Brough Comedy Co for
its Indian tour.
The Majeronis are quite replicas of their father and yet
more distinguished uncle, who took part in the greatest
representation ever known in Italy, when Madame Ristori,
M.M. Salvini, Rossi, and Majeroni appeared together in
“ b rancisca di Rimini,” at the Dante Centenary. George
Majeroni, in the “Terrors of Paris,” is a startling reminder
of his sire. Madame Ristori takes a lively interest in her
talented nephews, whose Scotch college education, how-
ever, did not intend them for the boards.
Alma Stanley, as Niobe, did more than take Melbourne
by surprise. The statuesque amazon fairly staggered a
lot of people who had been asserting that her voice and
manner were vulgar, and that she couldn’t act a bit.
Henry Burton, the well-known old-time circus-proprie-
tor, is stricken down with paralysis in Melbourne. He is
said t.o be destitute, and a subscription has been arranged
Mr. Burton’s character is of the whitest—a good com-
rade, a generous employer ; honest, energetic, compas-
sionate ; and though it is long since the heyday of his
brave old show there are surely many who well and plea-
surably remember him and it, and will extend a helping
hand to the gallant old ex-king* of the sawdust-ring l . &
Lewis Wingfield told a droll yarn of how he got up a
Lord Mayor’s show. There was to be a pageant called the
Inumph of Beauty, with the Queen surrounded by her
maids-of-honor. Reaching home one afternoon, Lewis
found his housekeeper in a rage. “ Look here, sir 1 the
place is full of ladies of the ballet! There’s three of them
gals in the front room, half-a-dozen in the droring-room
and as many in the dining-room. I didn’t think it proper
to show any into the bedroom, and as for the kitchen I
wouldn’t have none of the hussies there 1”

Haresfoot: “An incident in the comedy of the “ Book-
maker” was evidently suggested by the life of Sir Joseph
Hawley, the eminent racehorse owner, of Blue Gown and
others. In Italy he fell in love with an English ladv not
knowing she was married. She said to him :* I have a
sister at home, handsomer and cleverer than lam ” On
this hint Hawley posted off to England, and started
courting that lady, ‘as per sample,’ as he said. He mar-
ried her, too. See ‘ Dictionary of National Biography ’
for verification of this incident.”

Dear Bulletin, —Sydney City Council doesn’t quite
clear £'6oo a year from recitals over and above organist
vviegand s stipend. Numerous incidental expenses occur
in connection with lighting, door-keeping, repairs, and
advertising, so that the sum actually netted by the coun-
cil can’t go very far into hundreds.' Also, the aldermen
take advantage of their positions to fill a gallery full of
deadhead friends and relations at every recital—a further
loss to the revenue.—T.D.
It seems that the late Melb. Lyric Club went to its grave
in a clean-winding sheet, after all. The club’s final
operatic outbreak —a creditable performance “ Mar-
jorie,” in which Miss Mabelle Darley (nee Davidson) played
her first, speaking part—resulted in a £2OO loss A call
upon the members brought some £7O for the payment of
needy creditors, and the club thereafter worked itself
free of liabilities by means of open-air concerts at the
M.C.C. ground.

home daily—psycho-clairvoyance.”
A Japanese play is the entertainment for realism. There
is not much pretence in symbolism. If there is a murder
in the piece, they will occasionally provide a condemned
criminal, who is killed in full sight of the audience (so
travellers say). At Thursday Island (N.Q.), once upon a
tune a Japanese play was in full season. The white folks
rolled up to see it. The Jap. actor (generally struts and
performs almost " mid codings on,” but, in deference to
the racial stpieamishness of the whites, those actors were
each hidden in a 1% garment like a dressing-gown, but-
toned up to the chin. The white did not appreciate the
delicate compliment, in view of what followed. It was a
il niora l Anglicised, it might have been en-
titled Is mama-re a failure?” Departing from his
cherished traditions of realism, the heavy villain did not
enact the choicest scene as he would have enacted it
before his countrymen ; but he made most violent and
suggestive gestures with an elbowed arm. The whites
began to quit. The Government-Resident was treading

graceful! ’ 11 ) es, it is-ha! ha! ha! ho! he! ho!”

“ Shades of Shakespeare ! Bernhardt as Hamlet! Will

Dear Bulletin,— Another injustice to Ireland. You
say, re Barton M’Guckin, that “ Australia will he glad to
hear the English— -i.e. , Scotch—tenor before he drops into
decay. Barton is English—i.e., Irish. A quarter of a
century or so ago he was principal tenor at Paddy’s Opera
Dublin—called by some archaiologists St. Patrick’s Cathe-
dral. Besides, his nose (tip tilled like the petal of a bed-
room candlestick) proclaims him Irish, short and broad.—
Yours, Eblan. [Yes, he looks Irish, rather than Scotch.
—Ed. B.]
Soucais’educated bears (a late Tivoli attraction) were
put to a novel use at Brisbane Exhibition. Five particu-
larly beauteous barmaids having failed to prove a
“draw,” the bears were placed behind the bar, and a
fickle public came to gaze and remained to drink. Host
O’Connor, by the way, who paid a heap of money for the
right of the refreshment rooms, is said to be the only man
who made money out of Brisbane Exhibition.
The Melb. Alexandra management has had new frosting
done on the ground glass of the stage-door, to obliterate
an inscription, the offence of which was Greek to an out-
sider, hut it gave mortal annoyance to the ladies. Simply
“ Members Only, One Shilling,” traced by an impudent
fore-finger. The occult reference was to the Galvin case,
a talk of the town.
“The Bookmaker,” at Melb. Bijou, will help to bring
the theatre into touch with Kennedy’s ’orse entertain-
ment anon. The dumb animal show will waft its stabular
odours across the footlights in about, five weeks from now.
Bandmaster Hutchinson’s Cantata, “ Federation,” which
underwent so much preparation, is now announced for
performance, at Sydney, on 23rd inst. The setting is for
full chorus and orchestra of patriotic verses by Austra-
lian and British alleged poets, among whom Lady Jersey
and Parkes have been included. The composer himself
will conduct a chorus of 300 voices and an orchestra of
about 70 players.
Pattie Browne has been definitely engaged to appear at
Drury Lane shortly—as a young peeress. The famous
Langworthy case is responsible for the plot of the play,
and there is to be much spectacular display.
Miss Winifred Dibbs (daughter of the general manager
of the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney), who was one
of Staell s most accomplished pupils, has been taken
in hand by Prof. Remy, of Paris Conservatoire. The
latter, who hardly ever accepts private pupils, has for
once broken his rule, ow ing, it is said, to Staeil’s good
offices and the lady’s exceptional promise.

H. : “A curious recollection of old Melbourne is the
gondola. James Ellis, a restless speculator, who estab-
lished Cremorne Gardens in London, also founded them
in Melbourne. He had a notion that a fortune could be
made by running gondola steamers on the Yarra. He
built one by the Upper Yarra, opposite Toorak. It had a
rudder at each end, to avoid turning, and there was a
mighty corroboree over the launch. Afterwards Cre-
morne fell into the possession of Coppin and Brooke.”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
It appears that the rumor alluded to in last
week’s Bulletin that the committee of investigation had
presented a more or less horrifying further report about
the condition of the Queensland National Banlc was un-
founded, no report being yet presented. Therefore a
perfectly new joke to the effect that the institution
should henceforth be called the Queensland National
Blank, as a condensed way of describing the prospects of
the shareholders, misses fire.
* * * *
The Federal Coffee Palace (Melbourne) for the
year ended 31st July, 1897, realised a profit of £1237.
The capital has been written down from £129,112 to
£19,122 (£86,063 being written off building alone), and of
this only £12,763 is paid up, so the Federal is now earn-
ing nearly 16 per cent, on its reduced capital. If it had
kept at the original figure it would have been earning a
trifle less than 1 per cent. Still things are much better
than they were. For years it kept losing money till it
piled up a debit balance of over £16,000, and any kind of
a profit is a change for the better. If only the long-post-
poned whisky-license comes along, and a beauteous bar-
maid dispenses blue ruin on the premises, the old coffee-
institution should boom again,
• * * «
Brisbane Permanent Building- and Banking Go.,
which reconstructed in the black days of 1893, is releasing
the last of its deposits. It owed £229,880, and by its
reconstruction agreement it pledged itself to pay up
within six years with interest at the enormous rate of
6 per cent. The Brisbane Permanent is one of the few
institutions which carried their load successfully to the
bitter end, and it paid up its 6 per cent, to the last.
Last year, under the first instalment of Free-
trade, the N.S.W. soap imports were double those of 1895,
and the candle imports increased one-half. The local
manufactures dropped in almost exactly the same pro-
portion and 106 employes—about a fourth of the whole
number in the business—were discharged. Melbourne
Age publishes an interview with a N.S.W. soap-and-
candle manufacturer who says that the business is going
to perdition, and the Argus comes out on the other side
with an interview from another soap-man—the re-
cently-established Sydney representative of the Eng-
lish firm of Lever Brothers—who says the soap
and candle business is simply on the topmost pin-
nacle of a great and glorious boom, or words to that effect.
Meanwhile it is announced that the directors of the
Sydney Soap and Candle Works at Newcastle are shutting
down their candle-making department for an indefinite
period in order to work off the surplus stock which has
accumulated owing to the glut of imports, and 60 more
men are thrown out of work. Altogether, the English
representative person seems to have the evidence heavily
against him.
* * * •
After four days’ hard legal argument at great
expense, Vic. Full Court gave its sanction to the forma-
tion of an Assets Co. under British management to liqui-
date the remains of the Federal, City of Melbourne, Mer-
cantile, and English and Australian Mortgage Banks.
The new arrangement means that there will be a board
of about half-a dozen lawyers to pay in England and an
English office to keep up, while the Australian expenses
must go on very much the same, the assets being here.
The properties will be under the control of the London
board, which never saw them and knows next to nothing
of their value. Theenormous law-expenses comeout of the
assets, also there will be a big item for stationery and other
things. Theonegreatadvantageof the British management
is that it is going to issue 3 per cent, debentures to the
creditors—the Briton’s yearning to issue debentures being
absolutely unquenchable. The" assets won’t be any more
valuable, and the creditors won’t get any more out of
them through the issue of debentures. The only differ-
ence is that where a creditor would have got ss. in the £
dividend under the old system, he will get 4s. 9d. dividend
and 3d. interest under the new one. Chief Justice Madden
decided in effect that the scheme was a piece of compli-
cated foolishness, but, as the shareholders had accepted
it, the Full Court seemed to have no option in the matter.
The scorn which Madden, C.J., heaped on the 3 per cent,
debentures might not be exactly law, but it was remark-
ably good horse-sense.
* * * *
Under the present N.S.W. Municipal Act rates
are chargeable, not on the actual value of property, but
on the nominal rental. Therefore, the rich man who has
a large piece of vacant land which he is holding for a
rise nominally lets it, perhaps to an obliging relative, at
next to nothing per annum, and that rental fixes
the rates. One Sydney suburban council states, as an
example, that a piece of land with 700 ft. of frontage to a
main road pays 7s. 6d. a year in rates. And for this
7s. 6d. the borough has to maintain 700 ft. of pavement
and gutters, and to do scavenging, lighting and general
supervision as well. Consequently a demand for power
to charge rates on the value, not the rental, is setting in
strongly.
» • * *
Even the S.M. Herald, which usually takes
things as they come, rose up quite suddenly last week to
endorse The Bulletin’s contention that the glowing-
returns given in the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners’
reports are wholly unreliable, Among other things, the
Herald complains that no allowance is made for interest
on borrowed money sunk in railways in course of con-
struction, which is a perfectly true bill, though it applies
to some other provinces as well as N.S.W. Apart from
this, it objects to the “ misstatement annually of the cost
of the debt,” and, as the department puts down the
amount of money borrowed for railway and tramway
purposes the amount on which the department
should pay interest —at some millions less than the
figure mentioned by the Auditor-General there should be
a pretty bad misstatement somewhere. For instance, the
railway and tramway capital in 1896 was under
£39,000,000 according to the Commissioners, while ac-
cording to the Auditor-General it was £44,351,490. Then
there is more trouble over the rate of interest, where,
also, the two narratives fail to hang together, and over
expenses which one authority reckons in and the other
apparently omits. Anyhow, there is a large, aggravating
discrepancy between the alleged good results shown by
the Commissioners and the alleged very bad results shown
by the Auditor-General, and it is a pity that the two
authorities can’t agree a little better.
* * * *
In Maoriland last year -£854,822 went through
the totalisator, and the Government took 11 per cent, on
this, equal to £12,822. The racing clubs made a clear
profit of over £64,000, so, out of the 10 per cent, deducted,
the State gets U per cent.; the clubs about 7£ per cent.,
and 1 per cent, goes in expenses. When the tote is legalised
throughout Australia, The Bulletin’s idea of a fair deal is
1 per cent, for expenses and the rest to be divided between
the State and the horse magnates —4£ per cent, each. On
that basis the machine would yield about £40,000 a year
in Maoriland, and probably about £70,000 in N.S.W. or
Victoria. The present division is entirely one-sided.
The recent discoveries of bank-note forgeries in
Sydney draw attention to the actually insignificant
amount of crime of that class standing to the debit of
Australia. The Parramatta forgeries discovered in 1873
are the only big ones on record. And yet forgery of
banknotes was the crime for which scores of English
criminals were sent to Botany Bay.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners have fixed
the “ specially” low rate of id. per ton per mile for the
carriage of mineral ores in truck-loads. Owners of mines
in the far west —and the bulk of ores are coming that way
—would be better pleased with a sliding scale for long
distances. It seems hardly fair that ores travelling 500
miles should pay the same mileage rate as those only
running 50.
* * * *
Melb. Herald, which has hitherto consistently
upheld the justice of the Salvation Army’s pauper com-
petition with the legitimate firewood and toy traders, now
lashes out furiously at the Army’s audacity in daring to
go into the more profitable and better-advertised bike and
tea trades. The Bulletin is wondering, in a casual sort
of way, why the firewood and toy trades never no make
their whereabouts known in print, and is also endeavour-
ing to derive some great moral lesson from the Herald’s
sudden change of front.
* * • *
The Queensland Railway Commissioners, unlike
their Southern brethren, are going to stand no nonsense
from the much-paragraphed scalping bogey. The repre-
sentatives of Southern ticket-brokers had only just estab-
lished themselves in Brisbane when one of them was
promptly fined ; also two constables, a railway official,
and a search-warrant managed to secure and confiscate
some 70 tickets, to the value of close on £3OO, in the
agents’ offices. This has squelched the “ outside ” traffic
in railway-tickets up North—where the fine for unauthor-
ised sale has been raised from £5 to £2Ol
* * * *
A Sydney chemist has, it is said, found out how
to easily and cheaply extract methyl from white spirit.
Hitherto it has been supposed that when alcohol had
been methylated it had been rendered quite unfit for
human consumption. Now it is being treated and used
for essences, perfumes, and medical extracts, and no one
suspects that methyl had ever been near it. The struggle
to reclaim methylated spirit has long been proceeding in
Australia. The present writer has, on several occasions,
distinctly recognised in alleged whisky dispensed at city
groggeries the painty taste and odor of methylated spirit.
* » * ’•
“ Yerax ” asks The Bulletin :
(1) Is it true that the supply of sulphides in sight at
the (a) Broken Hill Proprietary Co., (b) British Broken
Hill, (c) Broken Hill Block No. 10, is practically inex-
haustible?
(2) The estimated quantities at each mine?
(3) What do the sulphides assay?
(4) Are the present methods of extraction successful,
and what is the percentage of loss?
It is impossible to give a plain yes or no to most
of these questions. The supply of (a) sulphide ore in at
least the B.H. Prop, and Block 10 is practically inex-
haustible. It all depends upon the price of metals
whether there is much or little of that ore payable.
Estimates of quantity are for the same and other reasons
valueless. Also, the quantity of payable quantity de-
pends upon the progress of metallurgical science. What
can be said positively is this : that the body was tapped
in Block 10 at 1200 ft. or so. The width of the lode at
that depth is uncertain, but it most likely varies from
120 ft. to 10ft.—that is the variation at the upper levels,
and there is no appearance of a pinching. B.H. Prop,
has only prospected to about 700 ft. Its management has
not made an estimate of the quantity in the mine. (It
should have been said that the sulphides begin at from
300 ft. to 500 ft., according to position along the lode). The
B.H. Prop, has three 40-acre blocks, surveyed square, on
the line; Block 10, one; British, one, and a second
block of about 58 acres. Whether the latter
block has much lode remains to be seen. Com-
paratively little prospecting has been done on this
British property; attention has been given mostly to
repairing a very big collapse and working carbonates.
Sulphides vary in assay value. B.H. Proprietary has cer-
tainly hundreds of thousands of tons that contain 20 per
cent, lead, 16oz. silver, and 20 percent, zinc ; it may have
millions. Block 10 has vast bodies (again it is impossible
to be anything but indefinite) that contain 22 per cent,
lead, 250 z. silver or more, and 22 per cent, zinc—a low
estimate. British sulphides are higher in lead, lower in
silver. But, again, this isn’t much of a guide, for
tractabilit.v is another element in calculating values. Thus,
if the zinc is in very intimate association with
the silver, much of the latter is rejected with
the zinc. The process of treatment now is a
preliminary concentration, then a roasting, then smelt-
ing ; or, if there are carbonate ores in plenty to blend with
the concentrates, the roasting may be dispensed with.
The object of the concentration is to get the zinc away
from the lead. With the zinc about 50 per cent, of the
silver and from 6 to 10 per cent, of the lead goes; also, of
course, the silica and other gangue. Whether that is
“ lost ”or not is doubtful; for zinc is more valuable than
lead, but, as it is also very costly to operate, and as this
part is weighted with the gangue, it is practically value-
less to-date. Only Block 10 has done anything on a
marketable scale with this portion of the product; others
are experimenting,
* * , *
The B.H. Prop, smelting furnaces on the mine
will be run down gradually during the next two or three
months (this was written before the fire broke
out in the mine on Sunday last). The Tarrawin*
gee flux quarries will then have lost all their value.
Already 13 of the leases have been forfeited for non-pay-
ment of rent. What shall be done with the 50-mile rail-
way line remains unsettled ; there is a little wool and
wood traffic, but not nearly enough to keep the concern
going.

B.H. Extended shareholders met in Adelaide
the other day, and were treated to the customary vision
of the promised land. But they were more interested in
the announcement that attempts were being made to hook
J. Bull.
• * * *
Reckoned that, with silver at 2s. and lead at
even £l3, B.H. British product is worth a small fraction
more than it was at last half-year’s average prices—2s. 6d.
for silver and £ll ss. for lead. Every £1 above £l3 for
lead means an extra profit of £5OO fortnightly.
In connection with the fall in the price of silver,
it is to be remembered in making any calculations of the
effect upon the B.H. mines, that ore-buyers usually deduct
a certain quantity of silver per ton for losses, especially
if there is any intractable constituent —zinc, for instance.
B.N. North concentrates assay about 68 per cent, lead,
9oz. silver, and 8 per cent. zinc. If any silver at all in
this material is paid for, it is not 90z., but 4oz. at most;
so that the loss to the co. through the fall is proportion-
ately reduced.
Apart from the Broken Hill Prop., which suffers
very severely from the silver fall —in other words, the rise
for lead doesn’t compensate it for the fall in silver
about the w'orst trouble is likely to result from the blow
given to the treatment of what have been called zinc-
tailings. Roughly speaking, 90 per cent, of the lead and
just less than 50 per cent, of the silver are contained in
the concentrates proper; 50 per cent, of the silver and 80
per cent, of zinc are in the zinc-tailings. It was in the
treatment of the latter that most experiments were lately
being made ; and Block 10 had shipped large quantities
to German works. Naturally the very big percentage of
silver in this material helped largely to make its
treatment profitable, though the profit has never been at
all large. Now, unless the zinc market hardens very
much, the “tailings” heaps mu3t continue to expand and
be nothing but cumbering refuse.

The fire at Block 13, Broken Hill Proprietary
mine, which broke out on Sunday morning, and suffo-
cated three of the working-party sent below to subdue it,
seems to be located at time of waiting, after severe suffer-
ing bv the workmen engaged in fighting the flames.
Meanwhile, all the works are closed down, and there is a
big slump in shares.

So far the N. Nymagee alleged copper-mine is a
mere shadow, a mere gambling-counter, yet what big
business has been done in it! One Sydney broker alone
claims to have pocketed £2OOO commission in handling
the stock. The mining tipster who has been booming it,
when appealed to the other day, publicly recommended
N. Nymagee shares (at 6s. 6d.) in preference to Burra
Burra (at 2s.)—though, so far, N. Nymagee isn’t a mine at
all, but simply a piece of ground with nothing in sight
but scrub and scrip—and most likely plenty of calls to
follow. This North Nymagee business has gone so far as
to be now regarded as a huge joke. After a puff of North
Nymagee, the tipster remarks: —“ Shares of the Burra
Burra Go. came into some notice. We should v'ish this
company had more funds at its disposal, and had proved
its mine to possess the permanent lodes necessary for
success when dealing with a copper proposition.” Ye
gods! And how much funds has N. Nymagee —and who
on earth knows whether it has the lodes (permanent or
other) necessary for, &c., &c. ?
* * * *
In another column appears the prospectus of the
Klondyke Prospecting Association of Australia, which is
forming in Melbourne (of course in Melbourne) “ to ac-
quire and work auriferous ground in the Klondyke dis-
trict." S. B. Bagiev and Co., of 408 Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, are the brokers, and 200 shares are offered to the
public at £lO each. Also, at the moment of writing,
there appears a cablegram to the effect that the Yukon
River has frozen over, and the conveyance of supplies by
this route has consequently stopped till about next May.
As the Yukon is a fast-flowing river of immense size, this
means a powerful freeze. It is further cabled that there
is famine at Dawson City, and £lOOO are alleged to have
been given for a sack of flour and a small quantity of bacon.
Evidently the Klondyke Prospecting Association can’t do
much real work till next May, though, if it could strike
some workable scheme of getting bacon to Dawson City
per balloon, it might fill in the winter profitably and
declare dividends of about 2,000,000 per cent. The
Canadian Pacific Co., it is announced, intends building a
line to the Yukon —some day. With the eye of faith The
Bulletin sees that line in the far distance, with the
express arriving at Iceberg City on a winter night and
discharging torrents of steam to try and scald the polar
bear off the platform so that the passengers can get out
and have some hot coffee and boiled fox.
* * * *
The White Pass, which only 50 out of 5000
prospectors have succeeded in crossing, is the easiest
entrance to Alaska from the east, being 1060 feet lower
than the Chilkoot, at top whereof is a quarter-mile climb,
during which the slightest slip would mean kingdom-come.
One way and another, the gold-god is pretty certain of a
big harvest of victims over this Klondyke craze.
* * » *
The Bulletin observes some difference in the
conduct of the Klondyke boom by that noble institution,
the great Australian press. In one column we read of the
great quantities of gold taken out by individual diggers—
names and quantities given. In another appear cables
showing how many thousands of men and animals are
blocked, that disaster is pending, that the gold-yields
have been grossly exaggerated, and that certain big news-
paper correspondents say so. It was not that way in the
W.A. boom. The Australian press was better played
upon in the interests of the boomsters of that province.
Of course the prospectus of the Klondyke Prospecting
Association is out—in Melb. Only one-third of the entire
capital is wanted by the promoters. “ The services of
two men have already been secured and, immediately
upon registration, they will proceed to this wonderful
field.” How very modest not to ask for a little cash 1
When one remembers the fate of the W.A. prospecting
syndicates, the wonder is how and where they find men
enterprising enough to put up money for advts.

Editor Bulletin.—Sir, —Kindly inform Aus-
tralian miners that it is difficult to obtain employment on
the British Columbian goldfield. Also, that no matter
how good reports may be, there is no use attempting to
go to the Yukon rush until next spring (March).—A. B.
Fraser (late manager Octagon syndicate, Coolgardie),
Vancouver, 8.C., 6th Aug., ’97.
* * * •
The M.L. Govt, has settled the cyanide trouble
by buying out Cassell & Co’s local rights for £15,000.
Dirt cheap at the money—and much more dignified than
chousing the patentees out of their moral rights on the
strength of any technical blunders made by them in
claiming patent.
* » * *
Auckland (M.L.), as was here predicted months
ago would be the case, is feeling very tired : too much
boom and a very heavy baby. It’s just as hard to pick the
Cup-winner as to name the exact date when certain bull-
john mines will have their first crushing.
Waihi mine, M.L.’s chief bullion-producer to
date, has put out ore valued at £571,195 : Aug. crushing,
3120 tons —£11,668. 160,000 shares ; last quote, £7 10s.
Body of reef 30ft. thick. The mine never looked better,
and the block of stone now being met with in No. 2
Martha Lode, once thought absolutely barren, is the
cause of much jubilation among local shareholders. The
reduction-works, in course of erection at Wharoa, include
200-head of stamps.
Waitekauri, another mostly-Bull-owned M.L.
mine. Last crushing, 1841 tons, £5462. Difficult ore to
treat. The experiments being made to discover a less
expensive reduction process are resulting in partial
success.
Some M.L. Aug. crushings: (North Island)
Waihi, Silverton, 1000 tons, £1398 bullion ; Kapai-Ver-
mont, 260 tons, £580; Woodstock, 1000 tons, £2758 :
Hauraki, bullion valued at £1546 ; Kuranui, Caledonian,
490 tons, £876 ; Waiotahi, 135 tons, £506. (South Island):
Achilles, 484 tons, £1894; Morning Star, 324 tons for
5340 z. gold.
Crown mine (M.L.) has declared a div. of Is. a
share, and ore reserves are large enough to anticipate re-
gular divs. Last crushing, 1200 tons for £3300 bullion.
Talisman, Upper Thames (M.L.) putting
through 1000 tons first crushing. Result not through at
time of writing. Boomsters expect ore to value £6 a
ton ; writer thinks £3 10s. more likely. Question
whether body of stone is metallic enough for the plates of
a 20-head battery.
Preservation Inlet, Morning Star mine, Otago
(M.L.), nestling among the snow-capped peaks of Southern
Maoriland, is probably the most obstinately expensive
mine to drag gold from in Australasia, outside of W.A.
Water the trouble. Cost of working, 17dwt. gold per ton
of quartz mined. Quartz averages loz. 9dwt. 3jgr. Last

Dredging boom on in Otago (M.L.) rivers. The
Olutha and its rivulets have all been pegged. Eleven
dredges, for Julv-August months, mopped up £12,176
worth of gold ; working expenses for same, £2,500.
For the first half-year of '97 the yield of gold
from Otago (M.L.) shows an increase over same period of
’96 of ll,141oz., valued at £44,463, due to recent suc-
cessful dredging operations.

* * « •
Mt. Hepburn co., that was going to be the
saving of Cassilis, Gippsland, looks very sick indeed at
time of writing. It got a move on the mill with a
2000-ton pile to work on, and after a week the plates
looked as barren as a zinc roof, and the men below were
given a holiday pending developments. Three weeks’
crushing left the plates very much as they were, and now
Cassilis looks blue, the Hepburn 30ft. reef being the last
and only fond hope of the district.
* ♦ * «
Tas. August mineral export, .£90,687. Corres-
ponding month last year, £33,450 : ditto 1895, £70,987.
West Coast mines exported more than half the Aug. value,
£46,725. Were the West Coast of the Land of Sleep-
a-Lot cut off, the rest of the province wouldn’t have much
Federal value—despite Stagey Douglas.
* * * *
Tas. August dividends, £10,660. Mt. Lyell
pays another 4s, div. Oct. 1, making £92,000 for the
half year.
* * • *
The petition for the winding-up of the West
Australian (gold district) Trading Corporation was being
inquired into when the last English mail left. This co‘.
took over the affairs of the W.A. (gold district) Trading
Syndicate. A certain O. D. N. Grant was sent out as
manager, and shortly after he arrived he suggested busi-
ness in galvanised-iron. Three months before any ship-
ment came, however, he received this coded cablegram
“There will be a meeting shortly, statutory. Suggest
telegraph me (us) as follows: ‘ Transactions are enor-
mous. Continue shipments steadily ; expect profit to be
£25,000.’ ” To which he replied :—“ Yesterday I received
a cable from you suggesting that I should actually tele-
graph a deliberate misstatement of facts to blind the
shareholders. You know full well the ‘ transactions are
enormous’ is utterly false. How can they be when no
goods have arrived ; and even if they had I have no
money to pay dock charges.” The managing director
denied to the court that he had ever sent the cable mes-
sage; but he admitted that the prospectus on which the
corporation was formed (Major - General Tulloch was
hooked, among others) stated that the syndicate had
“ declared a dividend at the rate of 100 per cent, per
annum,” whereas all the profits were “ only anticipated,”
no substantial trading having actually been done ; also
that at the first meeting of the corporation he had told
the shareholders that £60,000 worth of goods had been
shipped, whereas, in reality, syndicate and corporation
together had shipped only £27,000 worth. Enquiry ad-
journed.

During the first week of Sept, five different
storekeepers in Coolgardie were entertaining gentlemen
in possession.
% * * *
M. : The Sons of Gwalior, N. Coolgardie, is,
Tub Bui, mot in is told, a lode formation from 4 to 14ft.
wide, and running 10 head of stamps. Its first crushing
went 7oz. ; its most recent one is 720 tons for 619oz.
It has all the appearance of being a good property.
* * * *
From Kanowna, W.A. : Just come in from the
Gordon, where things are very sick, and likely to stay so
for this year at least. Owing, I suppose to the parent
mine’s poor crushing they all seem to have lost heart; out
of five mines that were at work only one (the Extended)
is doing anything, and there are but three men on it.
The main lode seems to be done altogether ; the Gordon
is getting six months’ exemption, the Gordon North three
months, and the Gordon South is also applying. At
Mulgarrie, nine miles from the Gordon, there are 130poor
devils waiting for the Hit or Miss to start; in fact, labor
matters are not rosy just now. At the Great Boulder, a
few weeks ago, as the day-shift was going on I counted
over a hundred men waiting to see if anyone was away, to
get his place ; but now most of the mines about there get
the men to sign a book and wait their turn. And the
wait threatens to be a long one.
* * * *
“ Cudmore and party’s Little Wonder mine (N.
Coolgardie), though only a small leader, is the most con-
sistent gold-bearer on the field (says a Bulletin corres-
pondent). It has been followed on the underlie to water-
level, about 130ft., and has produced for its owners
£20,000 worth of gold. At water-level they found a
fair-sized reef in the hanging-wall, carrying good gold.”
* * * *
“The country north of Mt. Malcolm to Earlston
will receive much attention in the near future (writes a
Bulletin correspondent), owing to the number of new
finds, many on formations other than quartz. The great
drawback to successful mining in that region will be the
scarcity of mining-timber and engine-wood, which may
hasten the construction of a railway from Menzies in that
direction.”
* * * *
“ Coolgardie just now (writes an ex-Sydneyite to
The Bulletin) is about the stiffest town for its size in the
island. In this morning’s paper there are no fewer than
nine selling-up advts., most of them in re prominent
citizens. Nearly all the brokers are down at Block 50
rush, dry blowing. The share-market is knocked clean
out, and if the share-exchange sells 500 shares in a day it
is a good day. Unless things get a little more lively
everyone will be off to British Columbia. Adelaide is, of
course, the hub of the Western market.”
♦ * * *
The cyanide plant at Bayley’s (to treat the pile
of tailings) is nearly ready. Work is being done in a new
main shaft, and some fair stone has been raised from the
Coelcshott underlay shaft. Manager Mackinnon has
ceased work on the Ford’s Hill and No. 1 North leases,
which were rung into the co. at the time of amalgamation
with the South. Possible that Bayley’s may have a good
crushing or two yet, but look at the capital!

Ross, Ross and Co., financiers, 352 Collins-st,,
Melbourne, want freehold and c.p. securities for any part
of £100,000 at about 4 per cent. Owners wishing to bor-
row should write this firm, who make no charges if securi-
ties are not accepted, and do not control wool or stock
sales.*

The ex-member of an Australian Cabinet, who
took ’Ome for flotation a lot of Westralian mines, all
“ proved and fully equipped with machinery of the latest
pattern,” has not only failed to get any of them off, but
is badly left with the properties and a frightful lot of
machinery which would cost more to bring back to a
market than it is worth.

Coolgardie Courier gives a London share list
of 137 Coolgardie mines, whose nominal capital is over
£19,000,000, and their closing price, on July 10th,
averaged about 13s. The list looks something like the
prospectus of a monster consultation, in which there will
be a few prizes and the rest nowhere.

Can a man be said to have deserted his
children or left them without sufficient means of
support when they are taken away from him under
a Divorce-court order, and handed over to the
custody of other people? Such is the question
pending in a Vic. Court. When the Acts now in
force in Vic. and N.S.W. re the support of
deserted and destitute wives and children were
passed, “ voluntary ” desertion was clearly in the
view of the Legislature, and not the “ compul-
sory” desertion involved in a custody-order.
* * * *
Lecturing on the wrongs of children, Miss
Rose Scott ventured on delicate ground when
she reminded a Sydney audience that “ the chil-
dren of thieves and persons with stains on their
characters were liable to inherit these traits from
their parents.” Recalls Charles Lamb’s queries
when writing, long ago, to a friend in Sydney
(Judge Barron Field, wasn’t it?) —“ Is there much
difference between the son of a thief and a grand-
son ? Where does the taint stop ? Do you bleach
in three or in four generations ? ”

wanderings has been put up by a little six-year-
old girl between Mossgiel and Cobar. Lost from
a bush pub. on a Monday morning, she was found
on Saturday afternoon in the back-country of
Marfield station. It is reckoned that she had
covered 90 miles, and had lived largely on gum, a
lump of which she was chewing when a black-

Through the good offices of the churches,
Australia fuses with the Flowery Land. On
Wednesday (Sept. Bth) the Rev. James Fong
Kam Yee, a Chinese missionary connected with
the Presbyterian Church, married Miss Annie
2?“ AS Eh“iSrS”
The bride is a native of Cambridge, England, and
has relatives in N.S.W. The bridegroom is a
full-blooded Chinese, and has been 18 years in
Australia, for nine of which he lias been connected
with the ministry. He is about 38 years old ; the
bride about 25. They will reside at Newcastle,
N.S.W.

One “Reformed recently sent Premier
Reid £3—“of which I am very sorry to have de-
frauded the Government.” Curious that it’s only
the twopenny-halfpenny peculator who ever is
sorry to the cashing-up point. Nobody ever heard
of “ notes for the £5OO of which we regret to have
defrauded the Customs,” or of “ herewith £lOOO,
approximate difference between value of stuff I
contracted for and stuff I supplied.” “Con-
science ” isn’t apparently able to get much above
one figure.

The recent Christian Endeavour Conven-
tion in Sydney was desperately funny in spots.
One of the societies, it was announced, “supports
a Bible-woman locally, and a child in India.”
Whose? Another society “collects eggs laid on
Sunday for distribution to the poor.” Presum-
ably because the Sabbath-breaking hen-fruit is an
accursed thing, and fit nutriment only for the
pauper un regenerate.

A recent Sydney churcli-robbery case sug-
gests inquiry as to what has become of a man
who, a good while ago, received for a like offence
the outrageous sentence of 15 (?) years from a Vic-
torian Judge belonging to the sect whose church
had been broken into? But that was for “sacri-
lege ” ; the Sydney offender was committed
simply for “breaking and entering.” It moves !
* * * *
A story of the prowess of a certain lady
art-union canvasser. She called on the Chief
Justice— Bulletin doesn’t say which C.J. —
and got three guineas out of him. Then she
went to Mr. Justice Blank, and said that
(say) Billy (using the Chief’s Christian name
with great unction) “has given live guineas, but,
of course, I can’t expect as much as that from
you.” Judge Blank immediately planked down
five guineas. Then she returned to the Chief, and
told him what she had said to Blank, hoping that
he would not betray her. “I don’t know,” said
the Chief, “I have been brought up to regard
absolute truth as a very sacred thing indeed, and
I really could not back up an absolute falsehood.”
“ There is one way in which you need not back up
a falsehood. Give me two guineas more, and you
will redeem my veracity.” Whereupon the Chief
bought the extra two guineas’ worth of chances.
« * * »
Melb. Evening Herald is becoming a
cheerful paper. A picture of a gory corpse is a
regular thing whenever a murder takes place, and
the older and gorier the corpse is the more promi-
nence the drawing gets. But the other day the
Herald even surpassed itself by giving an illus-
tration of a tumor removed from a woman’s
stomach at Melb. Hospital, said tumor consisting
of pieces of hair bitten off and eaten by the
patient. Same week the Weekly Times, issued
from the Herald office, gave a photo, reproduc-
tion of the frost-bitten feet of a man lost on the
Alps, just prior to amputation. It’s something to
be thankful for that papers issued from the
Herald office aren’t published in time to be read
at breakfast.

M‘Gr. : Adversity
makes us acquainted with
strange occupations. I was
passing through a small
street in North Fitzroy
(Vie.), last week, when a
draggle - trailed woman
rushed into the street, paced
by a red headed urchin of
about 10. Screeching male-
dictions all the way, she
caught up with the fugitive
at her front gate, and pro-
ceeded to wallop the recalci-
trant youngster. But the
boy wasn’t of the sort to
take it lying down, and
struggled so hard that few
blows, and those ineffectual,
got home. “I wish yer
father was ’ome!” at last
screeched the mother in des-
peration ; “ ’e’d learn yer
’ow.” This was the cue for
a beery-looking individual
in the crowd attracted by
the noise. Stepping for-
ward with a humble air, he
touched a battered boxer
andjsuggested, “I’ll flog yer
little boy fur yer, fur a
shillin’, missus.” The offer
was not accepted, but there’s
a job waiting—for Premier
Turner requires somebody
to flagellate those Mildura
boys on whom the local
police declined to operate.
Killjoy : The Melbourne book-fiend ha.s a
lovely new canvassing-trick. He calls round with
a small cookery-book which he insists on leaving.
He will call next day for it, or, if you decide to
keep it, will take sixpence instead. That’s how
he puts it. He knows perfectly well that in 19
cases out of 20 he’ll get the sixpence. For this
reason: Cunningly placed on the third or fourth
page amongst “Useful Hints” is the following
printed statement: —
By indulging- your wives in frequent excursions, thn e
or four times a year, you will enlarge their view of things,
increase their sociability, improve their health and tem-
pers, and, more, you will find they have an increasing
love for home.
The woman who would surrender such a priceless
treasure of a book for a paltry sixpence is fit only
for an asylum, and as a woman usually answers
the door, the sale is assured. If a man gets hold
of it first, of course he furnishes the one-in-twenty
case. He either gives it back emphatically to the
canvasser, or pays the sixpence and secretly de-
stroys the accursed thing. Fortunately, I answered
the door.
* » * *
Parry Okeden, Q. Police-Commissioner,
lately sent much - needed notices to several
police officers. He found them in the habit of
suppressing evidence in the favor of accused
persons, and tendering only evidence against, in
approved Botany Bay fashion; and enjoined
them that it was the duty of the police to get at
the facts, and not merely to obtain a conviction.
* * * *
“Papa” Pinnock, the ancient Brisbane
P.M., should really have been superannuated
long ago. The other day he ordered out of court
an attorney who protested against a police-
inspector whispering something to the bench,
despite the facts that an accused person has the
right to hear everything that is said to the magis-
trate in open court concerning the accusation
against him, and the magistrate is not supposed
to know anything about the case (save what is
beforehand in his own personal knowledge) except
what is stated publicly and formally.
* * * *
Echuca, the Cold-tea women lately dis-
covered, has one pub. to each 14 ratepayers. And
Echuca is in the midst of one of the greatest wine-
growing districts of Australasia, too. But, as a
result of a local-option poll, 40 pubs, are to be
closed.

boom days, although, last half year, its revenue
from rates amounted to only £l6. Silverton,
nowadays, consists mostly of a brewery for the
part supply of Broken Hill; and, more or less
appropriately, the council consists mostly of
brewery men, with the brewery boss in the chair
and the keeper of one of the brewery pubs, as
town clerk. There ought to be nine aldermen;

Melb. : “Ina lounge past the ‘Bull and
Mouth,’ to-night, I was met by four grown-up
women and three men, selling the War and,
a bit further on, by two more women. On Thurs-
days, the Salvation Army has about 30 stalwart
uniformed men in the city selling papers. How
gospel-like it would be for these men to go to the
Ar y’s Home and chop wood for the infirm, who
would do better selling the War Cry, and not be
insulted by the familiar cry of “ Go to Work ! ”
It. ; “ Apropos Evison’s ‘ Undertakory.’
I went to a funeral of a very old friend the other
day. The undertaker distributed tickets for free
coaches. Myself and my brother declined, and
told him we would travel in a common cab. He
actually grew abusive to the verge of blasphemy,
because we, who held more or less official posi-
tions, declined to occupy his hideous conveyances,
and thereby cheated him out of a free advertise-
ment.”

Maud Lita is the stage-
name taken by Miss Finlayson,
a daughter of the late Capt.
E. Finlayson, of Newcastle.
She has made a very promis-
ing beginning on the operatic
stage —under Mr. E. barleys
management in his recent sea-
son at Sydney Opera House.
Mr. Farley was Maud Litas
first teacher; encouraged by
his advice, she studied under
Annis Montague, with whose
company she travelled, taking
Phi line, in “ Mignon,” and
other second roles. Since then
the sweet soprano had a chance
to gain stage experience in
W. Cosgrove’s co. at Her
Majesty’s, and made a good
impression as leading lady. In
Maoriland Miss Lita has played
leading operatic rdles. In her
recent Sydney season it was as
Arline that she showed her
voice to the great' st advan-
tage. The Bell-Delroy Co.
have taken Miss Lita for a two
years’ tour through India, Eng-
land, and South Africa.

One effect of famine’s
cruel grip on Japan is the great
fall in the market-value of
young girls. Whereas the
little brown pa or ma could
aforetime rely on getting from
£2O to £4O each for their best-
looking daughters as “ supple-
mentary wives ” or concubines
to the Naice people of Jap.
society, want of food amongst
the poor has forced so many on
the market that a little brown
maiden, taught to be as docile
and affectionate as a dog, is
only worth about 20s. ! Govt,
recognises the traffic down to
12 years of age. After three years pa can repay
the money and get his ewe-lamb back. After six
years, the girl can demand her freedom.
A Charters Towers paper pleads eloquently
for the retention in Cairns of the 37 Japanese
ladies of easy virtue who haunt that skewbald
town. “ They are clean,” says the Towers scribe,
“ they don’t get drunk like the white Messalinas,
and are not guilty of filthy language.” There is a
world of pathos in this cry of an anguished jour-
nalist that his small brown Flossie may not be
taken away from him. That chrysanthmum girl
is neat, and nice to look at, and she is a very ar-
tistic colfee-oolor all over, and what would the
dreary North be without its brown Asiatic Flossie,
anyhow? The ordinary Port-wine Annie and
Beery Jane of the South don’t keep well in that
trying clime, and, what with rum and Kanakas
and breezy fights in the lane beside the public-
house, they are unpleasant to look at. Butthebrown
Flossie is a clean and sober Messalina, and in her
company a man can spend his money comfortably
and be chucked out respectably when it is done,
without a dishevelled head throwing cuss language
at him from the window just as his ear strikes the
bottom step. And as Cairns is mostly brown, in
any case, and mostly Flossie as well, the chrysan-
themum girl is just about suited to the place. Let
her stay—the whole 37 of her—by all means.
• * * *
The N.S.W. Charities Department (writes
“J.S.” to The Bulletin) has many well-paid
officers and an honorary staff of lady visitors to
whom free railway-passes are issued. And yet it
is unequal to saving the province from the dis-
grace of being indebted to a local benevolent
society for relief supplied to certain families with
whom State children are boarded out! Owing to
the department’s delay in paying the heads of
those families, they were obliged to appeal for aid
to Parramatta Benevolent Society. The incident
seems to point conclusively to a something rotten
in the state of the Charities Department; as also
to a something very inefficacious in the lady-
visitor system. It is not long since Dr. Renwick
was loudly asserting that the N.S.W. system of
boarding-out State children was the best in the
world. If it be so, the best stands in sad need
of reform.

“ Phono,” from Adelaide :—
Your sweater of Sydney women-typists is an angel com-
pared with certain firms in this city. They not only work
the women for all they are worth without payment, but
bind them for twelve months, and charge a premium ! A
pound a week, forsooth ! Why, that Sydneyite is an
absolute philanthropist.
A BALLADE OF BELLBIRDS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The earth has ceased from slumbering ;
Come are the sweet September days ;
Glad anthems now the birds all sing,
Staccato harmonies of praise.
Amid the notes their throats upraise,
One floats out like a jewelled rhyme :
With Springtide’s joyous lilt and swing
The bellbird rings his silver chime.
Tall spears of grass-trees now upspring,
The waratah’s red heart’s ablaze.
And lories with heads downward cling
Hid ’midst the gum-trees’ leafy maze ;
Here flannel-flower her white bloom sways,
Fair vestal of our sun-kissed clime;
While from acacias blossoming
The bellbird rings his silver chime.
Now feathery tree-ferns wave each wing
And form green aisles of shadowed ways ;
Where wanton winds their fragrance fling
The stately lyrebird struts and plays ;
And here the prowling dingo strays
Ere mists uproll or sun doth climb,
And at the dawn’s awakening
The bellbird rings his silver chime.
Envoy.
From all the trees and flowering sprays
Sing halladists of our spring-time;
And clear above their thousand lays,
The bellbird rings his silver chime.
Gosford, N.S.W.
F. W. HULME.
THE EROTIC BARD.
A tall lamp burned above his head,
And o’er his tumbled ringlets shed
A golden grace.
He wore a quaint and flowing dress
Of woven silks. The torturous stress
Of wrinkling thought made deep impress
On his pale face.
His hand lay on the creamy sheet
Where sprawled some lines of “ raptures sweet ”
And “wild desire,”
And on a rug as black as night,
In dreamy, indolent delight,
A fair girl basked, all lily-white,
Before the fire.
How fair she is, disrobed as then,
Not all the minted words of men
Can tell, I vow.
The poet from his labor rose,
And lisped in unaccustomed prose :
“ You know, dear boy, that I compose
From models now.”
SILAS SNELL.

A Leading Chemist, after suffering for years
and trying all his own Medicines, and being 'pro-
nounced incurable by three leading Sydney physi-
cians, and the authorities of Walker's Hospital
Parramatta , is restored
by Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Liver Pills.

the ex-patient’s letter will
prove the above).
King-st., Newcastle, 1
4th February, 1897.
Messrs. Gould, Sons
and Co.
SM
a
Dear Sirs, —I was a ' '
great sufferer for many Photo, of Mr. Hutchison.
years with congestion of the Liver and Constipa
tion. At periods my skin got quite yellow and I
suffered from Depression of Spirits and Loss of
Appetite, &c., &c. I was under three of the lead-
ing Sydney Physicians, and was ordered to give
up my professional duties and go into a hospital
which I did, going to Walker’s Hospital, Parra-
matta River. I was five weeks there, and only
got worse and came out to die. I was recom-

and taking the pills I commenced to improve, and
now am pleased to say I have quite recovered!
(Signed) D. Hutchison,
Registered Chemist.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 14, 1897.
Mt Dear Myek—
At Randwick, on Saturday, the well-filled
stand and lawn were bright with fussy and frilly
muslins. Frocking is decidedly frivolous this
season ; fluttering little frills meander up and
down skirts, long diaphonous sash ends agitate
themselves in their wearers’ wake, puffings break
nut in unexpected places, parasols are baggy
bundles of chiffon. Machine-pleating is more to
the fore than ever, and appears in a new variety,
“ sun-kilting,” of which there was one shimmer-
ing example on the lawn in quiet-toned grey.
Hats, worn at all angles except a right one, were
as gaudy as a florists’ window. It is the first
principle of style this spring that you mustn’t
have a hat straight. These vivid tints need care-
ful mixing by an experienced modiste. One dark-
brown straw had a miniature variegated poppy
arranged tastefully under the brim, but some
women were topped off with confections obviously
Home-copied from imported models with fatal
results. “Touch” is everything in a best hat.
Several “ early Victorian ” cowls hid pretty faces.
And where were the bonnets? Bonnets and old
ladies are going out together. Nobody dresses
now beyond 35 ; girlish grandmothers of 50 flutter
in muslin and high heels, and drag a curly frizzled
hangover forehead-furrows. Among the gowns
which stood out on Saturday one belonged to a
brilliant purple person, another of rose-silk under
black grenadine glowed like a fire behind
grate-bars. A theatrical girl, in bright green,
looked as grassy as a paddock after the
recent rains. The vice-regal box was colored
chiefly in white-and-blue. The latter hue was
very general a medico’s wife mingled it
airily and successfully with green ; a gilty Melb.
visitor wore one of the few handsome matronly
silk gowns of the meeting ; another of dark green
bore the London hall-mark. A Melb. girl flut-
tered gracefully in a pink-patterned foulard. The
new spotty foulards were universal in blue and
white ; a brown and white was evidently sug-
gested by the new water-color “ African leopards?”
A recent bride looked the part in frilly mauve
French muslin, and a newly-come-out damsel with
a Nellie Stewart type of face was artless in butter-
cup yellow. Two sisters spoiled their ensemble
by tying their heads up in mosquito nets.
It is mortifying that, just as all the pretty and
long-priced muslins have come in with a rush, the
city has been flooded with exactly the same pat-
terns in fabrics damaged by sea-water in the
fires on board two incoming steamers. Bargain-
hunters have eagerly snapped up the damp muslins
at half the price given for the fresh goods, know-
ing that a little cold water and a flat-iron soon set
matters right. And now every woman owning
one of the new muslins will be thought by every
other woman to have made it out of the damaged
stuff, and so all joy in her new gown will be
destroyed.
Miss Manning entertained the visiting delegates
and others at a mammoth at-home on Thursday
evening last at the Victoria Club. The room's
were full of guests and flowers and chatter and
light refreshments and music, and it was a bril-
liantly successful crush. It may have been acci-
dent, but all the songs were of the most senti-
mental kind. It was funny to see stout, solemn,
spectacled, bearded delegates holding up the wall
while impassioned songstresses gasped “ Robert
toi qui j’aime,” and implored, “(live me your
hand that I may press it gently,” or remarked,
“I love thee, ’tis all that I can say.”
In connection with the large Govt, scramble up
the Hawkesbury last Saturday “ in honor of the
Federal delegates,” the most joyful person is pro-
bably the owner of the little pub. at the river
railway-station. As soon as the Namoi landed her
passengers a hungry horde rushed the hotel par-
lor and demanded sustenance in any form, and
until the return train started all hands were busy
serving out tea and bread-and-butter to the
famished excursionists. The expedition was a
dismal failure, owing to the alacrity with which
folk always rush a free banquet, and the lax super-
vision of entry-cards. A long-felt want at all the
Convention reels is a stern, strong man at the
entrance to examine tickets, and ruthlessly forbid
more than six full-grown persons to pass in on one
invitation. The company gathered early at Mil-
son’s Point station, were hustled about from one
train to the other, and arrived at the Hawkesbury
sardine-packed into the carriages; then they
swarmed on to the steamer and forthwith began to
frenziedly hunt for victuals and drink. In the
crush the waiters were unable to get at their ork
everybody was yelling for supplies, and Premier
Reid uplifted his voice in remonstrance and told
the guests not to crowd the tables so. There was
a weak little band playing spasmodically at inter-
vals, and the delegates and their friends were
transhipped to the General Gordon to view the
beauties of the river while the outside crowd on
the Namoi waited for their return. These two
items, and the hungry glare on the guests’ faces,
coin prised all the amusement.
Nothing so brutal as the scramble for food and
drink, said an old politician who was present
has been seen since the historic day of L. l!
Smith and Co.’s “free banquet” to mark the
Duke of Edinburgh’s visit to Melbourne—on which
memorable occasion the crowd rushed the victuals
gnawed whole legs of mutton like famished dogs
and stove in the head of the thousand-gallon cask
of wine which was to have been doled out a glass
at a time to the loyal populace. The Sydney
federal festivities have been made too cheap all
through. The blame of the disgraceful food-
struggle at the Hawkesbury is said to be chiefly
due to the fact that the Ministry, in its desire to
curry favor, allowed invitations to be issued by all
and sundry of its supporters.
A tightly-wedged crowd of Conventioners and
others paddled round to Manly and back on the
Fair-light on Friday night. It had been intended
to rope off distinguished visitors on a reserved
portion of the ferry-boat, but the distinction was
done away with, and the Convention and populace
were all mixed up together. Mrs. Reid, who looks
as if playing hostess agrees with her, came up
smiling in a very radiant hat.
The Admiral’s open-air party last week was very
cosmopolitan, strange faces almost outnumbering-
local ones. Delegates with feminine belongings
meekly trotted them round. It is the Federals
“on their own ” who stay away from functions ;
family parties succumb every time to the fascina-
tions of the freely-scattered pasteboard invites.
Gowning was not sensational. Everybody felt the
party was premature for spring revelations, and
was lying low for the races. Victorian vice-royalty
clad herself quietly in grey, Two or three hats
were in screaming tints ; a hard blotting-paper
pink is a vociferously smart color this season.
Black vvas also chosen by many—it is the correct
wear since the Paris fire made it fashionable to
mourn.
A noticeable feature of the monster gathering
of school children at the concert in the Town Hall
last Wednesday evening was the clear enunciation
of the youthful singers as a general thing.
Croakers who deplore the spread of the “ accent ”
would have had nothing to grumble at, and the
juvenile chorus had some stiff phrases for childish
tongues to lisp. “ The eucalypti’s songsters
flash out their golds and reds ” was rather a jaw-
breaking line, but the children took that vocal
fence, as well as others, bravely, and showed no
trace of “ twang.”
A conscientious conventioner honestly wishing
to work steadily through the Govt.’s little pro-
gramme of business and pleasure, found himself
badly left through trying one day last week to
save time. As he was boarding in a distant
suburb he brought his night-clothes with him in a
bag to Macquarie-street in the morning, intend-
ing to make a quick change towards night. How
the bag was inadvertently locked up by the care-
taker and afterwards rescued, and how its owner
essayed to perform a hasty toilet in the room of a
friend (who was absent) in a swagger caravan-
serai ; how the chamber-maid came in to forage
for washing and took the stranger for a burglar,
and how he got away all right, but was not
allowed, on returning later for his tweeds, to
again enter the hotel on the ground that he was a
“suspicious character,” has been up till now the
Convention’s prize “good story.”
r I he opening of the Art Exhibition was a cur-
tailed ceremony. His Ex., accompanied by
daughter and son-in-law, murmured a few words
of introduction, and the sparse public dispersed to
view the works. The President’s roomy sea-piece
has the place of honor, and is seen to advantage,
which it wasn’t at the artist’s private view.
M'Cubbin’s “wallaby trackers” seem depressed
by the fact that their infant has no chin. Coutts’
anaemic portrait of a musician is remarkably like,
and P. Science’s “ Signor Hazon,” lurking in the
shadows of a deep frame, is also characteristic.
The Queen of Clubs ” is a harmony in coal-
smoke, showing a brown-eyed damsel in black
velvet entourage. J. Mather’s “Golden West”
is a poetic water-color with symbolic touches in
the evanescent red sunset-reflections and dreary
wind-tortured trees. Two artists have tackled
Mason’s Point railway-station, and both contrive
to give that palatial structure a back-yardy ap-
pearance. And R. Coffey sends two counterfeit
presentments of his favorite sitter, and many
square yards of smaller canvasses. A. Hanson’s
downright bush-feeling in his “Station Smithy ”
is balanced by the weirdness of liis bilious mer-
maids relating hair-raisers to each other at the
bottom of the deep blue sea. A. H. Fullwood
reminisces pleasantly of Tasmania in several land-
scapes. A strong black-white is a head by Mrs.
Moore-Jones, and the gem of the water-color room
is a “ Garden ” by a Maoriland artist, Mabel Hill.
The Diamond Jubilee Charity Carnival was
opened on Monday afternoon in the regulation
way with “ God Save ” and all extras. The open-
ing programme was well-arranged, but failed to
lure many inter-provincial visitors. Miss M
Wynne, Miss Daniels, Messrs. Strauss, Burns-
Walker, and Sykes were the musicians, and the
flower-basket presentation was made to Mrs. Fer-
guson by the president’s little daughter. At the
beginning the carnival seemed a misfit in the
town Hall. The bazaar was rather a microscopic
’ten* in that vast acreage of floor-space; but
exhibits have been got into place since Monday.
All the usual adjuncts of the fancy fair are there
with many side-shows. Among the dolls, which
had not all arrived on the first day, a completely-
ngged swaggie and a correctly-costumed court
lady show the neatest taste. The carnival will
keep on till the end of next week.
The hero and heroine of a sensational bolt of
some years back have returned, in company, to
Sydney, where the lady is filling a professional
engagement. They put up at a city hotel of good
class, whereat the guests were on the tip-toe of
expectancy to see the much-talked-of elopers, but,
after a few days’ seclusion, they departed for a
less conspicuous retreat.
New examples of fashionable frocking are found
in Waters and Co.’s “Spring Guide,” which hand-
book of style I see says that the sensation of the
moment, the trimmed skirt, is not imperative. A
woman can still wear her “ godets ” without feel-
ing as if she’s fallen out of the front rank.
Ilounces flutter on soft crushy muslins and wash-
ing-silks, and after thestruggle to be placed high up,
they descended to the hem of the garment, whence
they are again climbing ambitiously to the knee ;
also, they crisp and crinkle on shoulders and
sleeves. You can’t have too much pleating, and
goffering, and frilling, and ruffling about; to reach
the. present fussy ideal a woman must look as
much like a French hen as her money will run to.
Blouses are not possible without several miles of
narrow lace meandering over them. “Is my hat
straight?” won’t be the burning question this
season, as the great object of millinery to-day is
to sit on the head at a rakish angle and be adorned
with a highly-colored assortment of fantastic
raggery.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Here and there, one finds a female critic from
Lngland (not to mention ‘ johnnies ’) professing
what seems to be genuine surprise at the insin-
cerity of Australian blushes. Melbourne and
oydney girls are declared - to carry more paint and
powde.r to the square inch of countenance than
any other dolled-up. darlings on the inhabitable
globe. In a confidential whisper, the globe-
trottress proceeds to explain that the hair and
complexion commonly met with in sassiety here-
abouts are confined to the demi-monde of dear old
London, where ‘respectable’ beauty could afford
to wash its face in public at any time, as a guaran-
tee of good faith. All the same, the latest reports
upon English rose-culture are a flat contradiction
of this new-chum libel. Mrs. Premier Turner
has come back from the Jubilee prepared
to state on oath that a lot of respect-
able cockney complexion is laid on with
a trowel previous to having its pinky freshness
rubbed in by the second finger of the right hand.
The reckless candor of the British aristocracy’s
make-up quite shocked her, although her husband
is resident member for St. Kilda, and the St.
Kilda cheeks don’t usually get their glow of health
from the sea-breeze, anyway.
“Scenic artist Spong, who knows paint when
he sees it in the street, sends corroboration of
Mrs. T.’s testimony in a letter to his friend,
George Gordon. Hilda Spong’s youthful papa
had been ten years away from London, and it
seemed to him that the British matron’s ideas of
modesty were changed for the worse. As a new
arrival from Australia he was quite shocked at the
complexions he saw around him. After this the
daughters of the sunny south may reckon them-
selves comparatively natural-looking and bona-
fide.
“ Mayor Strong, who comes of pious stock,
attends hops and like frivolities with a sense of
divided duty, for conscience protests against such
pomps and vanities, while as Mayor he feels that
his presence is assisting to make the earth revolve
upon its own axis. That den of iniquity, the
theatre, has been steadily shunned by him all his
life until a few months ago, when he was tempted
by a lady’s invitation to a very fashionable mili-
tary night at the Princess’. Mis Worship sat out
the performance, but seemed to shudder all the
time as though cold water were trickling down his
soul. Rev. brother Strong from ’Oine, the rather
stouter double of Melb.’s Mayor, is an even better
boy. The parson has never been a frivoller until
he came to these antipodean wilds, when so sudden
was his backslide that he actually made his debut
at a ball. Rev. Charles Strong’s clerical garb
was one of the features of the recent civic shivoo.
It is somewhat confusing that the visiting shep-
herd should be labelled with exactly the same
name as the popular Melb. identity who runs the
new religion at the Australian Church in Elinders-
street.
“The passing of Julius Knight has left an
aching void in the affections of susceptible spin-
sters of all ages, many of whom had never seen the
inside of a theatre until wily Williamson and
Musgrove drove the devils out of the Princess’ by
the‘ Sign of the Cross.’ On so high a pedestal
had Marcus and his poetic legs bfeen placed that,
to this day, sympathetic souls mourn his hard fate
in having to go on making stage-love in Adelaide
to one whose nature, so he told his friends, was
‘of the earth earthy.’ Scoffers protest that until
they quarrelled, coming out in the ship, the com-
plainant thought as highly of Mercia’s ‘ nature ’as
he does now of Berenis’s. Mr. Knight and Miss
Elliott-Page, by the way, both :omt the rumor of
their alleged engagement. The latter, indeed,
declares positively that—well, her declaration is
most prosaic and practical.

plained that blarneying Chairman Neild distracted
their attention from ‘glove-saving scones’ (but-
tered before baking) and similar thrilling novelties

lecturess and her sister.
“The coach-parade, arranged in connection
with the Eindon Harriers meet (so English, you
know) ended badly in two cases. A party of ex-
Bananalanders tooled by a dashing militaire from
the Northern province were the chief sufferers.
The reputation of‘a grand whip’ is easy to live
up to with plenty of showy, well-broken horse-flesh
to pick and choose from. But let a beast come
along who really means it, and down goes the
house of cards. And your smart dames who doat
upon sport and all the rest of it, faint and are
helped into shops at the first kick-up. There are
not many like Lady Hopetoun, who when her
carriage-horses played-up in Collins-street with
her baby and nurse on board, jumped out and ran
to their heads before the footman saw what was
coming. And at Camperdown once she brought
a mutinous four-in-hand to their bearings, all on
her own, and never shed so much as a hair-pin.
Conservative old clans of Victorian ‘ Nicest ’ felt
their hearts burn within them in June last when
reports of the Jubilee grandeur of jumped-up poli-
ticians and their wives daily disfigured the
European cables column of the morning l paper.
As a counterblast, the equally aristocratic doings
of Mrs. Madden, her daughters and other touring
first families were officially chronicled in Melb.
society journals, Also the countesses, marchionesses
and honorable mesdames with whom they lunched,
afternoon-tead and dined. Also the frocks they
wore to state-balls and garden-parties. No item was
omitted which might chasten the parvenu pride of
yesterday mushrooms.
“A certain military junketter, who, since re-
turning from ’Ome, has been offered greetings at
the altar of Momus and, among others, by a re-
joicing Abstinence Society, had a well-earned
reputation while away for singing ‘ Champagne
Charlie ’ with the best of them. He shook him-
self free from the A.S. tormentor when he sailed
out of sight of Port Melb. pier, and neither on
board nor at Ome showed any recollection of his
distinguished connection with the teetotallers till
he returned to this sackcloth city, and was drawn
to its arms with an illuminated address.

Sassietv journalism is responsible for another
tragedy. The engagement-notice of a certain
middle-aged bachelor, appearing prematurely in
print, annoyed him greatly. He reproached the
girl of his heart for rushing round with the news
and his chagrin grew so aggressive that the bride-
elect, who is quite a spring chicken, gave him
back his liberty. Also, she gave the papers per-
mission to declare the fixture cancelled. Apropos,
tew engagements are kept seoret now-a-days—l
mean few are kept out of print. When a girl
nooks a man she begins to wind him up on her
loud-clicking reel, instead of letting him run
quietly with the cheek off.

On Saturday (Sep. 11) squads of spectators in
played-out winter clothes assembled at Caulfield
to sympathise with the numerous Harriers who
i a f?S' < 'd far behind in the cross-country race. It
was a touching sight to see each loiterer as he
arrived leaning his tired head on the manly breast
of a friend. Lily-white seems the correct com-
plexion for Harriers’ legs ; perhaps the preliminary
coffee-bath, affected by kilt-wearers, is not recom-
mended as an aid to speed.
“A very successful evangelist, who had predicted
the end of the world and fixed the date of the
resurrection, and was working large crowds of
people into a state of holy frenzy, visited Ballarat,
and piesently had a big percentage of the popula-
tion preparing for its end, and packing up for the
voyage over Jordan. Business became bad, par-
ticularly amongst drapers, it being held by the
women that existing styles were going right out in
favor of diaphanous robes and feathers. At this
juncture a Bridge-street draper hung this legend
on the outer wall: ‘ Bargains ! Bargains !! Bar-
gains !! ! Grand Clearing Sale in view of the
Resurrection! Cerements a specialty.’ It brought
business back. The last trump would not drag
the average woman from a bargain.
What fine scents women have for a ‘ bargain ’
sale! A suburban draper advertised that he
would open his shop at 11 a.m. on Saturday for a
two-days’ sale of shipwrecked dry-goods. At 10.30
about 200 women were surging round the door-
way, and when the gates of Paradise were opened
150 angels all tried to squeeze into a doorway wide
enough for two. Hats were lost and dresses torn
in the mad ■ rush, and the language and scrim-
maging would have shocked a footballer.
“The Mare-Cum-Jenkins divorce-case, heard
lately in Sydney, recalls an incident which
occurred in a certain city park when the
‘Nicest’ first made that place popular. A
flash and handsome bicycle - instructor was
pedalling around with the pretty wife of a local
medico, when the last-named party appeared in
sight. The bike-man took the first of it, and
hopped off his machine with a ‘ Good day, doctor.’
Sawbones paid no heed to the frisky teacher, but
sternly ordered his wife home. Her bike was sold
next day !
“ Pew-rents fell with a thud a while bacic, when
the handsome, high-church incumbent of a
fashionable tabernacle quitted his widowed seclu-
sion for the sake of a rosy, 10-year-old confirma-
tion candidate. For once his flock was unanimous,
and chief-churchwarden and virtuous verger alike
ba-aed in piteous unison at the sight of their cul-
tured shepherd piping pleadingly to a pink-cheeked
giggler who, so far from appreciating the
honor done her, seemed to sigh for the
smiles of her elbowed-off schoolboy-adorers.
Now the-love-lorn cleric, whose fickle flame has
cast off her brief allegiance, hangs his harp on a
willow-tree, so to speak, and has little heart to
welcome back his reconciled sheep as they frisk
home to him from foreign folds.
“ Prof. Marshall Hall is not one of Mark Ham-
bourg’s adorers, and even ventures to criticise his
style. He asserts that Hambourg does not know
the correct method of thumping the keys, and
sadly needs teaching. Hambourg retorts, with
that amiability which distinguishes musicians,
that he rejoices in not playing like Marshall Hall.
