Personal Items.
G. H. Reid has now completed three con-
tinuous years as Premier. His rule began
August 1894.
Dr. Bowker, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), entered
his 83rd year on 31st ult. The venerable doctor C
still in the active practice of his profession, and,
quite recently, successfully operated for cataract
on a patient as old as himself.
“This gentleman comes from Maoriland,
a country which it is well to remember is cele-
brated for its geysers—useless things which pour
forth great quantities of steam —and mud.” — Mel-
bourne Bung-President on Cold-tea Isitt.
King Billy, of Ercildoune, last of the Bal-
larat blacks, is to be monumented in Ballarat
cemetery by the local A.N.A. and the Australian
Historical Record Society. A granite-built mia-
mia, surmounted by a native spear, is the design.
A joke of (Q.) Judge Paul’s. A bully-
ragging solicitor was storming at a witness, and
wound up a torrent of vituperation with “ I never
came across a more unmitigated liar ! ” Paul
(quietly, and with all absence of accent, but with
much eye-twinkle): “ Don’t forget yourself, Mr.
Fogg.”
A Tasmanian gaol-governer, seeking to
inflict severe punishment on a prisoner who had
“given him lip,” sent for two J’sP. to try the
case. After hearing the man’s iniquities set forth
the justices gave him a severe lecture and dis-
missed him. Then arose the disgusted governor
with a savage scowl: “ Why, I could have given
him three days’ solitary, myself ! ”
At Sydney Mayoral luncheon to the Con-
ventionists, the other day, the failure of the
Crown to confer the expected jubilee honor on
Mayor Ives was discussed, and a conjecture
hazarded that if the Federal Convention success-
fully conclude its business in Sydney, that con-
summation will give Government a fresh chance to
jog the Pimperial memory. Looks a little like
holding the sober Isaac on a string.
Mr. A. W. Meeks was president of
Adelaide Chamber of Commerce in ’B7; presi-
dent this year and last year of the Sydney
Chamber; and president last year of the
Conference of Australasian Chambers of Com-
merce—the most important gathering in Aus.
commercial history. He is a Britisher of 40 years’
residence in Aus. ; resident partner in Sydney for
Gibbs, Bright and Co. He points out how in-
valuable to the facilitation of mercantile interests
is the chamber with its 200 merchant members.
He goes back to ’54, when the Sydney Chamber,
in its own words, advocated the erection of alight-
house on Moreton Island “for the better security of
the navigation of that important and growing dis-
trict.” He speaks of the Chamber agitating in ’53
for the establishment of a mint consequent on gold
discovery in ’52 ; refers to its urging in ’OB upon
the Govt, the necessity for telegraphic communica-
tions between coastal stations, and next, in ’G9, mov-
ing for telegraphic communication with Europe and
getting it in’72. He refers to the Chamber in ’6B
discussing the Melb. Chamber s subject—uniform
Mr. A. W. MEEKS, President of N.S. W.
Chamber of Commerce.
Photo., Kerry.
tariff and Customs union for Australasian pro-
vinces—and deciding “that no legislative action
could be initiated in this direction unless the
colonies were joined in a Federal Union.” He
mentions M. de Lesseps informing the Chamber
in ’(>!) that the Suez Canal would be opened in
Nov. that year, and he also reverts to the
Chamber s anxious deliberations on Labor troubles
in 75. The Costa Rica Packet case, in which the
Chamber took a strong part, is the biggest recent
success. He strongly favors more commercial re-
presentation in Parliament; “while the best of
the British House of Commons consists of com-
mercial men and manufacturers, N.S.W. Assembly
contains only six. Mercantile men hold aloof
from public affairs here because polities have be-
come a mere squabble for fads to such an extent
that Parliament has degenerated into an arena for
carrying on a scramble for place and pay at the
expense of the most vital public interests.” He is
very keen on the introduction of an arbitration
clause in all contracts; he says, “ In case of dispute
at law, how is a jury of barbers and stonemasons
to establish the equity of a case involving expert
knowledge of trade and the sifting and weighing
of a heap of conflicting evidence ? ” “ The Govern-
ment, he said, “ has long recognised the value of
the Chamber s expert knowledge for the enactment
or amending of mere iutile laws.”
Addison, who figures in the Nixon-Addi-
son stabbing case at Adelaide, is chairman of the
S.A. State Bank, and as such may be held to have
a right to make advances to married women.
Lewis Lloyd, of N.S.W. copper-mining
fame, achieved unparalleled distinction in Parlia-
ment—by never opening his mouth during the six
years.of his membership And yet there’s no talk
of a monument to h im !

Gov. Brassey has given up his habit of
making political speeches. During his first six
months in Victoria he said he approved of retrench-
ing the defence-force, that Victorian women
would undoubtedly get the • suffrage, that
“General” Booth should be prohibited from es-
tablishing pauper colonies in Australia, that Irish
Home Rule was bad, that there should be an Im-
perial Zollverein, and that protection to local in-
dustries was wise and statesmanlike. All of these
references were more or less unconstitutional, and,
as it was not known how far Brassey would go,
grave consultations were held by some of the
Nicest and Rightest-Thinking Persons—and Bras-
sey was at last induced to coruscate a little less.
John Langdon Bonython, editor and pro-
prietor of the S. A. Advertiser— now in Sydney—
is an eccentric person, though of Cornish descent
(writes a local scribe to The Bulletin) —especially
though of Cornish descent. He once got a screed
from a Sydney pressman, which was really valu-
able, from a newspaper point of view. That is to
say, it gave exclusive information long before
other papers had it. The Advertiser could have
made a “ scoop ” with it, but Bonython funked,
and didn’t put it in. After awhile, the whole
world had the news. Now comes in Bonython’s
eccentricity. Recognising that the stuff was
valuable, he mailed a cheque tq the man who sent
it in.
A. H. Henning, o le of the new Wesbra-
lians, runs the Tasty; man infant pretty close as
the baby of the Federal Convention—in years and
political experience. Henning is a tothersider, an
Adelaide LL.B., 31 years old, who began practice
in Broken Hill eight or nine years ago, but finding
it a cold place when the silver-boom collapsed,
went west early, roughed it on the goldfields, was
sent to the Council by a hundred or so votes a
couple of months ago (on the presumption, it
seems, that a lawyer must be the best legislator),
and now, though entirely new to politics, is sent
to frame a Constitution for N.S.W. and Vic. and
other places on the strength of 22 Parliamentary
votes. The Constitution will have to be pretty
good in itself to stand up against things like that.
Up South, there is a tall, wild-eyed bar-
rister, who may be often seen striding rapidly
along thoroughfares, each stride covering at least
sft. of space, a gaunt whirlwind, generally armed
with a stick or umbrella, and having short
pants, that show a good 4in. of socked shin-
bone between their leg-ends and the tops of his
boots. He once accepted a job to serve a man in
the country with a writ. To save train-fare and
pocket it, along with the mileage allowance, he
walked to the servee, about 120 miles, served the
writ, and walked back to the city. There, he dis-
covered that he had served the writ on the wrong
man—the son, instead of the father ! Vexed, but
undaunted, he got a copy of the writ, and walked
all the way again ! . . . This same joker once
wanted to join the “mounted infantry.” Attend-
ing at the barracks, he overheard the major-
general then in command swearing. “How
shocking ! Do the men swear, too ? " he asked of
the officials with whom he was treating. “Oh
they use strong words sometimes.” “Could it|
ah, be arranged that they will not swear in my
presence ? Ido not like swearing. ” This request
being artfully fenced with, he proceeded—“ Must
all the members of the regiment ride ? ” “Yes, of
course.” “ Well, Ido not ride. Could it, ah,’be
arranged that I could walk ? lam a very quick
walker, and I am sure that I could keep up with
the others. Could that be managed ? I do not
like horses.” Negotiations snapped, carrot-like,
at this request.

Melbourne has its weekly teetotal prevari-
cation. The one-eyed crank sees his facts all out
of perspective, and always gets the relative values
mixed. A recent statement made whisky the chief
cause, of the Phoenix Park murders. Marshall
Lyle immediately announoed that the chief mur-
derer was a teetotaller, that the band had been
chosen so as to exclude boozers, and that the con-
spirator who swore in the gang was a teetotaller.
These facts were announced in the Age, but one
statement was excised. Marshall Lyle actually
saw the men in court when on their trial, and he
testified that their appearance was so eminently
respectable that they might have been members of
the Victorian Alliance itself.

Yic. boss-warrior, Holled Smith, has at
last fallen heavily on Col. Templeton, the well-
known building-society militaire. A long while
ago Smith left Melbourne to inspect a dilapidated
fort somewhere, or confer with somebody about
something, and as he forgot to appoint a deputy
to act in his absence Templeton marched down to
head-quarters with the plain gamp of a peaceful
life in his hand, and as senior officer bounced out
the acting-potentate and took command. When
Holled Smith came back and found a despised
amateur tactician running the show his wrath is
said to have been too awful for words. Then
_ Templeton went to England with the Yic. team
which won the Kolapore Cup, and again the Pre-
mier Permanent Building Society hero scored.
Now he is chucked out after 40 years’ service by a
lordly Smith because he is over the retiring age,
while a lot of older men remain in the force.
A yarn of an Australian hangman : The
dead man had been cut down, and a photographer
was, by permission, present to take a photo,
of the neck-dislocatee. He fixed up his subject
against the wall, balanced the head straight and
retired to a distance. Just as the artist was
squinting through the camera, the defunct’s head
lolled over on to his right shoulder. The hang-
man had to go back and straighten it up again.
Once more the artist had his peep-hole shutter
ready, and—the head rolled over to the left and
caressed Ketch’s knot. Again the hangman had
to adjust, a third time the artist peered, when—
the unruly head fell forward on to the chest.
(Oh ! the neck was properly broken !) This time
the hangman was mad. He wanted the work
over and a drink. He walked back to the remains
of his client, surveyed them for a few seconds,
gave each cheek a vicious slap, jammed the head
firmly back to the wall, and said—“Carn’t yer sit
straight, yer (blanky) ? You always was onrooly,
but I’ll civilise yer ! ”
Writes “ Wurrym” from Perth, W.A. :
“ Jean Ingelow never married; and her poems are her
children.” I quote Bullktin “Red Page,” Aug. 14. So they
were, hut not her only ones. Kveryone’s child was her
child, as well I know. The little home in Kensington
was, five and twenty years ago, a sort of Mecca for bet* ’
and girls, good or bad, and our delight was to sit and
listen to the warm-hearted loving ‘ Auntie ’ —she was
aunt to every youngster that crossed her threshold —to
listen to her of a dark winter afternoon spinning enthral-
ling romances for her silent little audience. She had a
marvellous way with children, and dearly loved an “ out-
law.” I remember once a youngster being carried away
by a violent storm of passionate anger coming to his
senses with a touch of her hand on his head and the
whisper of his name. In her child-poems those who
knew can trace this magnetic power. Curiously enough,
she had a way of brushing adults the wrong way—or may-
hap they did it to her and she reciprocated. Anyhow,
she has gone to a heaven of her own creating where the
population is all children, among whom she walks with an
inexhaustible box of crystallised fruit and tells pathetic
little stories to wide-eyed worshippers in pinnies. Her
brother, who was manager of the Oriental Bank in Sydney
in the 50’s, is buried in Randwiek Cemetery near a burial
lot that was the property of Bishop Barker.

Ben Tillett, in his lecturing bills, quietly
notifies that he is an “ Alderman of the London
County Council as a kind of sign that he is not
exactly a Ravachol or a Bakounin.
The interviews with elegantly-barbered
English literary men which appear in the maga-
zines would indicate that the novelist of Britain
is a very different article from that made
in Australia. Only one real Australian story-
writer has ever been interviewed, and when the
newspaper-man came away he was too sick at
heart to put his impressions into writing ; so the
interview, such as it was, has never been published.
To a few intimates, however, the journalist has re-
vealed how some of Australia’s best stories are
manufactured. After a long struggle with a
directory, and a longer struggle with the thorough-
fares of one of the poorest suburbs of an Austra-
lian capital, the interviewer struck a grimy door in
an L-shaped lane, off a back street. In front was a
much-decayed verandah, scrawled with indecent
and insulting remarks and caricatures. In front
of the verandah was the gutter, which ran down
the centre of the lane, and in it lay a festering
mass of cabbage-stalks and filth. Knocking at
the door, the writer was found at home, and so
was somebody else—the bailiff. What possessed
the landlord to place the latter “ in possession ”
the journalist wonders to this day; for, with
the exception of a few old boxes and bags
there was nothing for him to take. The front
loom was bare, with the exception of a gin-case,
on which stood a candle stuck m grease, some
paper, and a pen and ink. Facing this sat the
novelist, with his legs extended before him on the
floor, and in that position he was working for dear
life, turning out yarns at record speed, to raise
money enough to show the bailiff out. But, mean-
while, there was no ill-feeling between the two
they had known each other too long—and from a
kerosene-tin containing beer, which stood at the
novelist’s elbow, they alternately quaffed long
draughts out of a sardine-tin.

Aboriginalities.
“Oro” to The Bulletin from Pahang,
Malay Peninsula. “A little blood-curdler for
you. In Perak (the adjoining state) a tew weeks
ago a tiger bounded into a Chinese bungalow in
whioh two men were sleeping, and carried off one.
The district officer and a d.o. who was on a visit
were sent for in hot haste, as they were both keen
sportsmen. They followed the tiger’s tracks, and
found the body, minus the head, which the tiger
had eaten. They opened the dead Chow and put
in plenty of strychnine, and were rewarded next
day by finding the corpse of the tiger—the brute
had come back over-night to finish his repast.”

There has never been an actual pistol duel
in Westralia, but there was going to be one. A
certain well-known ex-military officer of high rank,
whose name and beard have been known on most
of the Australian r»,he, for many y.ars, went out
on to the field of honor with a fellow oountryman.
The general furnished the pistols—having two
beauties in a jewelled case, and the combatants
stood back-to-back preparatory to walking ten
paces. The moment the word was-given, Jack
Headed for the nearest pub., and the (funeral could
only manage to let fly at and miss him as he was
turning the corner. Undaunted, he set out alter
his enemy, but being a much older war-horse, he
only managed to reach the pub. when Jack had
successfully negotiated the pistol for 155., and
was taking his first drink on the strength of the
bargain. The coolness with which he asked the
general to join him ended the quarrel, and it
dawned on the general that the cause of the whole
business was the accursed drink, for which Jack
would resort to any stratagem, and, if necessary,
place himself under any man’s fire. Once the
warden issued a prohibition order against the
pubs, supplying Jack with liquor. On the ap-
proach of the Jubilee, Jack applied that the joy-
ful occasion might be honored by granting him six
days’ exemption. Order made accordingly.

Dear Bulletin, —Here is a vegetable-
oaterpillar yarn (I see a correspondent has been
sending you specimens of that interesting insect),
wa3 a t a dinner-table—year, ’B6—place, near
London. The proprietor of a just-then celebrated
London evening paper sat in front of me ; two
chairs from my right sat the boss man of South
Kensington Museum ; away up the table was an
ex _Minister and M.P. When the ladies rose, the
newspaper man lit a cigar and began to spin
elaborate lies—American lies—he had just been
across the Atlantic. The M.P. had travelled too,
mostly in India. He told Indian lies. The cura-
tor told geological, botanical, and general scientific
lies—he’s dead now, poor man. Our host was a
par«on—parsons never tell lies. At last I telt it
incumbent to keep step with the conversation. I
could tell lies, too—Moa lies, Cook Strait sea-
serpent lies, taniwha lies. But I did not need to
damn myself for nothing. I told them about the
vegetable-caterpillar ; how I used to dig it up at
the roots of trees, and give it to the Maoris to eat.
I explained its life history. The newspaper demi-
god snorted his disapprobation. He said, “ Don’t
you think you’d better go back to that country of
yours and dig up some more of them for us to
see?” The M.P. asked, “Aw, did you say they
were—aw —white caterpillars, or green ? ” I ex-
plained ; they laughed. I expostulated ; gave the
f
An t*\)
x _J nt Wn Tn*
A SKETCH BY HOP.’S UNDERSTUDY.
Maori name for the grub, and explained its mean-
ing. They remained jeeringly incredulous. Then
I said something disparaging to the British Philis-
tine. That brought out the curator ; he passed
for a great zoological authority. He said, “ I don’t,
as a rule, object to travellers’ tales ; they serve the
purpose of keeping people amused. But when I
see Science dragged into the vortex of Fiction,
then I expostulate. This theory of a vegetating -
caterpillar is extremely unscientific. It can have
no foundation except in the imagination of an
ignorant and barbaric people.” That settled the
matter. There was no more to be said. The
oracle had spoken. We rose and joined the ladies.
- -A.A.G., Maoriland.
• * * •
The new regulations of N.S.W. Board of
Health, after much friction, are being complied
with in the bush. Every cocky who
sells an occasional pound of butter has
erected covered bails with regulation
floor, and his neighbor, the butcher,
has sunk fifty hardly-earned pounds
in a model slaughter-house; both build-
ings are ready for the casual inspector
at any time, for their spotless purity
is preserved by the cows being milked
in t he same old bog, and the steer
slaughtered under the windlassed gum-
tree as of yore.

A Q. nigger-driver, having lately
used sulphurous language to a refrac-
tory “boy,” the latter went and com-
plained to the local Kanaka-commis-
sioner (a highly moral person) who
gave him the following note to take
back to his boss :
P.I. Office,
, 18th Aug., 1897.
To , per kind favor of
(the complainant Kanaka !)
I would ask you to be kind to the bearer
of this and not use language attributed to you
by him. He is religiously inclined, and seems
very shocked at such language.
, Inspector.
When the “ boss ” got this he knelt
down in a quiet place and prayed for
three-quarters of an hour without in-
termission. Then he saw the humor
of the. incident and sent the precious
epistle along to The Bulletin.

There is magnificent hapuka fish-
ing off Cape Maria Van Diemen
(M.L.), but the Maoris will not show
Europeans the precise spots where this
lordly fish may be captured. It ap-
pears that the pakeha would fry the
fish if he caught it, and the hapuka,
being a fastidious creature, likes to be
boiled or cooked in an oven of stones,
and would for ever desert his present
habitat if once degraded by association
with the white man’s frying-pan.
There is a pathetic old crank in a small
township in Ballarat district who used to be a
good farmer, but who now spends his t-ime taking
wild flights across country, and doing mad sprints
hither and thither. He was driven off his farm
after a long fight by Canadian thistles ; and now
believes that he is being chased by thistles. When
he clutches his hat and starts galloping madly
down the street, the people know that he imagines
great thistles as big as gum-trees are springing up
at his heels, no matter how fast he runs, and
reaching after him, trying to spike him with
prickles like bayonets. One sight of this frantic
old man is all
the encourage-
ment a young
a; riculturist
needs to in-
duce him to
become a
policeman at
once.

“ Australia’s most successful aboriginal
mission station,” writes “M.D.” to The Bulletin
from the Far West, “ is that controlled by Spanish
monks at New Norcia, Westralia. Over twenty
years ago, the monks settled in what was then a
most inhospitable region, on land given them by
Govt, conditionally upon their improving the con-
dition of the natives. After overcoming tre-
mendous difficulties, they have civilised large
numbers of the aborigines, and, with their assist-
ance, are making fertile a big tract of the wilder-
ness. They have large areas of land in high
cultivation, and are possessed of considerable
flocks and herds ranking among the best bred in
the province. They have a flour-mill and a
tannery, make their own clothes and boots, do
their own blacksmithing and carpentering—in
fact, make all that is possible for their require-
ments. They have splendid gardens and vine-
yards, and from a distance the mission looks like a
village. The brothers take pride in showing visitors
their old-time agricultural' implements—among
them a wooden plough with a wooden mouldboard,
and their first dwelling-house, still religiously cared
for,'and surrounded by buildings that would not
disgrace a modern town ; also, their school would
compare favorably with those of the State. The
married people live in rows of comfortable cot-
tages, and the grown-up males and females who
are single live in separate dwellings -apart. It is
a pleasant scene on a summer’s evening to see the
mission residents sitting under shady trees watch-
ing the young people dance to the music of a band
of wind instruments, played by aborigines, who
would make a small fortune in the streets of the
East, were it possible and desirable for them to go
on tour. Ecclesiastically, the mission is an abbey,
and its head —the venerable Bishop Salvado —is
the lord abbot, and, in imitation of the abbeys of
old, all wayfarers who desire it are entertained.
Those who stay for the night are given a sitting-
room and bedroom, and a bottle of good wine with
meals ; and, if they have horses, these are cared
for, no remuneration whatever being accepted.”

The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection —
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
Randolph Bedford, whose poetry has given
pleasure to many readers of this paper, is reported
to be a candidate for Geelong (Vic.). The bard is
a robust, tremendous man with the vigor of a loco-
motive, and The Bulletin, in its mind’s eye, sees
a picture of Bedford, M.L.A., bringing in a Bill
to give everything to everybody, and pounding
the table to flinders in his preliminary oration.
Probably Bedford could bring in such a fragile
thing as a bill without breaking it if he tried very
hard, but a man of his fighting temperament
would feel more at home bringing in an indignant
bull-alligator.

N.S.W. Art Society’s Show.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert . .
O desolation mingled with ™ch gmce^.^^^
Same text : same preacher : same old sermon. .
There are 231 numbers in the catalogue of paint-
ings at the Art Society’s exhibition ; and the work-
manship of three - fourths of them is admirable.
Probably so much excellent painting has never
been shown at any similar exhibition in Sydney.
Puzzle : Find the Art. Here is much to interest
and admire ; but where is that which exalts,
“spires, ennobles? You have to look long and
earnestly before you get one semi-thrill ot it at
79 Pitt-street.
Brunton Stephens defines poetry as something
that sends little wiggles down a fellows spine.
Painting should surely do as much : some paint-
ing can. Nero and burning Rome, tor example,
in a Melbourne gallery, are always pod for
wiggles for this fellow, the complainer and
caviller. Then why . . . ?
, * * •
Yet painting seems to pay in fair proportion to
the talent expended, if the prices the artists ask
are any criterion of what they get. Look down
the catalogue : hundred-guineas are not rare, and
fifties are quite common. Nearly everybody
jumps into double figures. And nearly everybody
seems industrious. Gordon Coutts, for example,
offers 11 pictures for .£BB7 ; and these, no doubt,
are only part of his year’s work. Without counting,
Albert Hanson seems to have about as many.
And painters of half-a-dozen are plenty. How
many Neros, one wonders, did Haydon paint in a
year ? Suppose, instead of dealing with a dozen
or two undistinguished subjects every twelve-
month, our artists dealt with one, would , . ■
‘ They can’t afford it : they paint to live ; and big
pictures at high prices are unsaleable, while small
ones at moderate prices may find a purchaser.
But, surely, after several years of painting for the
market, they might manage to spare one year to
paint for the Ideal ? ‘ And don’t they .
« * » *
Well, maybe they do ; but as yet, for the most
part, the Ideal escapes them. The picture which
N S.W. National Gallery has purchased from the
current show, for example, shows achievement
just eluding McCubbin’s capacity. Anyway, he
has tried ; and in some measure he has succeeded.,
The picture is called “ On the Wallaby Track,
which is not a very new or true title 5 and the
figures are two trampers and their child camping
in the bush. The woman sits in the foreground
of the picture, with her baby sprawling on her
lap, and she and it are admirably painted, and are
true and artistic as well. The woman s attitude,
full of weariness; her face, full of the monotony
of life ; and the figure of her, the dress of her, the
whole effect of her, do really make her live, bhe
sits there like Barnardine in “Measure for Mea-
gure careless, reckless, and fearless of what s
past, present, or to come ; insensible of mortality,
and desperately mortal.”
Given the woman and the baby and a gum-
tree, one could dispense with the rest of the pic-
ture. It hardly helps as much as it hinders, Ihe
man lighting a fire half-way up the canvas fixes
time and circumstances, and adds reality; but he
is a flat, characterless figure, painted out of per-
spective—too low for his size ; and the romantio
bower of over-arching foliage is out of harmony
with the spirit of the picture Beene is too
“freshly pretty,” instead of being “funereal,
secret, stern.” At seasons and places, of course,
there are many “ freshly pretty ” scenes in Aus-
tralia-through which the wallaby track might
very well travel; and McCubbin might justly
endeavor to secure the force of Heber’s con-
trast between vile man and pleasing prospect.
But that is a subtle effect, and only a great artist
could achieve it. McCubbin would probably have
succeeded better if he had tried for a simpler
result, and embodied a simple idea—making the
scene desolate as well as the persons. And his
picture would then have been, not more character-
istically Australian, but more typically Australian,
since it would have included a greater number of
characteristic instances.
* * * *
But McCubbin attempts an Idea—he makes a
step on the path to Art; and one is grateful for his
pains. Most of the other artists represented are
content to imitate Nature, or to photograph
Nature, or to pose Nature in one-figure subjects—
shirking composition, shirking also creation ; since
an artist does not create unless he brings some-
thing of himself to reinforce Nature, and exalts
her to a loftier plane in the glow of a personal en-
thusiasm. There is Piguenit, for example. Year
in and year out he paints beautiful bits of scenery
and landscape—viewed with an artist’s eye
and nainted with an artist’s touch; but he is
toujours perdrix —al ways landscape, and nearly
always (in essence) the same kind of landscape,
the landscape that interests and charms rather
than the landscape that impresses and astonishes
and awes. Of course, one takes the good the gods
provide, and is thankful: landscape is Piguenit’s
“line,” and in literature no one grumbles because
a novelist does not write history, or a historian
does not compose poems. It would be nice to
have a room full of brilliant pieces like “A
Northern Lagoon” or “Spring-time,” and these
in their degree would be artistic, and their mere
beauty would be soothing and refreshing. But,
in a cant literary phrase, the whole roomful would
be “thin”—while admitting their charm one
would remain unsatisfied, craving tor power.
* * * •
Yet Piguenit’s pictures sell—'fvhich is the mis-
chief of it. There is a promising young artist in
Sydney who started with enthusiasm and ideals,
and painted up to his ideals; and his pictures
graced his own walls, for the beginnings of power
in them were so repellent to the average patron
that year after year they went unsold. And
always his friends said, “ Why don’t you paint
something pretty? why don’t you paint some-
thing pretty ? ” And at last he grew so disgusted
that he dropped his ideals and enthusiasm, m his
small way-like Millais in his big way-and
turned to art that would sell.
* * * *
For the standard of the public in all forms of
art is the music-liall standard, winch makes
Shakespeare spell ruin while Flossie Flingtignts
draws her £lOO a week as a “ variety artist -she
is always an “ artist,” in irony ! And what would
it profit Piguenit if, like the quoted Haydon, he
refused advice to paint drawing-room pictures that
would sell because he “ felt large subjects in the
marrow of his bones ”; if, like Haydon, he
starved himself to complete his grand con-
ceptions, and took his bailiffs for his
models; if, like Haydon, he at last gave
up in despair, overcome by debt, disappointment,
and ingratitude, and groaning “ Stretch me no
longer on this rough world” ended his existence
with a pistol-shot ? Well, this would happen, he
would lose the whole world without gaming his
own soul, for an Artist’s accomplishment falls so
far short of his thought that he is pursued through
life by all the devils of dissatisfaction. Neverthe-
less, Haydon’s work is glorious, and Piguenit s
delightful—and there is something in that.
* * * * .
Pass on. The ever-promising Hanson is another
who wants to concentrate and aspire.. His
“Smithy” and “Under the Sea” are just on
the edge of being admirable ; there is always
something in his work that makes a stranger stop,
and look, and refer to the catalogue ; but he won t
take Emerson’s advice and “hitch his wagon to a
star.” Neither will Gordon Coutts, whose pictures
are full of excellent qualities without being worth
much after all. G.O. has a picture of an old mail,
for example, which has feeling, and character, and
truth. Nobody wants a better old man ; but then,
except the old man’s friends and relatives, nobody
wants a mere old man anyway. Now . . . sup-
pose Gordon Coutts had painted Lear; had brought
Shakespeare’s imagination to reinforce his own
technique ; and had shown us that immortal type
—an old man indeed, but an old man embodying
the tragedy of humanity, with the sufferings of a
race, an epoch, in his single figure . . . what
then? Well, Lear might have been a work of
art; the other old man is merely a good piece of
painting. Of course, you cannot get a model of
Lear, and copy his face line for line, trait for
trait; you have to paint him out of yourself,
create him as you go along. But a man can do it;
why don’t our men try ? George Haughton has
tried some such a theme, in his “ Solitude, and
has failed to get the force out of it, or into it.
Well, he has tried.
* * * *
There is as much more to say regarding 131 of
the 231 pictures; but it would be mere repetition.
Every artist who hasn’t hitched his wagon to a
star can read the foregoing remarks as referring
to him personally, with the necessary changes and
conditions. Only, among those whose wagons
seem worth hitching, it is fair to mention J. A.
Bennett, who can paint portraits so well that
he ought to paint better things better ; T. Dean,
whose moonlit water is beautifully painted, and
whose sky is unconvincing; A. R. Coffey, who is
clever without being artistically effective; and
Gertrude Williams, for something of virtue in
“ Languid Nature Sinks to Rest ” and ‘ Moored.
Also, Spence’s “ Evensong-time ” is attractive ;
George Forsyth and Mary Stoddart, junior, get
good technical results ; there is an uncommonly
good “ Head of a Girl ” by Mabel Hill, some neat
flowers from Margaret Floekton, and some fine
painting in E. E. Cusack’s “ Reading. And
there are many others as notable or nearly the
rooms are full of good technique and empty of
good art. A few black and white numbers are
skilful and attractive; the alleged sculpture is,
as usual, confined to representations of local
nonentities (artistically speaking). One goes
away rather gloomy.
A Woman.
A woman has many pleasures and much to be
thankful for ; but, alas ! she also has many pains,
A woman may not be the slave of mjan but,
where her affections are concerned she is devoted
to him, and often overtaxes herself thereby.
A woman will often, without knowing it,!commit
slow suicide for her children. She will thijnk, toil,
and shorten her life in their behalf. T6o often
they do not appreciate it.
A woman should not allow her colour to fade,
her cheeks to become sallow, her strength to be lost.
She is designed for attractiveness and ha<ppiness.
A woman need not allow any of these things to
happen if she will only obey the laws of health and
use the best means at her command for preserving
it.
A woman needs a friend upon whom she can
rely, and there is no friend which so surely aids
her when she is in need of aid as that great remedy,
Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure.
A woman who has never learned this great
truth, or who has failed to avail herself of it, has
lost a fine opportunity, and is doubtless less strong
and attractive to-day than she deserves to be and
might be.
A woman who follows the best hints that can
be given her, and who takes advantage of the
latest discoveries of science for helping her, is
certain to live longer, appear more attractive,
suffer less, and enjoy more happiness than one
who neglects her opportunities.

FINKLER ON PAPAIN.
After giving the result of a number of experiments, Finkler goes on:—“Papain,
therefore, shows a more energetic peptonising power than pepsine, and especially so when
the proportion of the liquid to the albumen is small—i.e., in the proportion of concentra-
tion in which food generally exists in the stomach and intestines. The great variability
In the quality of the commercial pepsine (some of which, under the most favorable circum-
stances, do not peptonise more than 20 per cent, of the albumen of the meat) gives Papain
( Paw-Paw ) also the preference, as it is always made of qpe uniform quality.”

aged 17 years, convicted of rape on a ohild at
Dubbo, in April, 1895, died in Parramatta gaol the
other day, from pneumonia. The aborigine
seldom lasts long in confinement, seven years
being about the limit.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Reid hoped N.S.W. Parliament
would be led by a triumvirate in his absence.
When he returned he found Want, Brunker, and
oarruthers would hardly speak to one another.
The Parkes-Robertson coalition (Dec., ’7B,
i° Jan. ’B3) has beaten all other N.S.W. Minis-
•nes to date in length of office. Unless the Pro-
lectiomsts find better leaders, however, this record
t> a - rs^'c H ss chance of being eclipsed by
8.0. Reid and his God-fearing crew.
Vic. Treasury should make a rise by for-
feiting a good many candidates’ deposits at the
lommg general election; in the multitude of
political hustlers some one is likely to be left. The
»wo deposits that Victoria most desires to forfeit
•re those of Duncan Gillies and T. Bent.
At a recent Federal Convention banquet
owo Australian Governors assured the company
that the people, in selecting the Convention dele-
gates, in every case picked the best ten men. The
utterance did not help the appetites of invited
guests who happened to have polled a few thou-
*ands leas than their successful rivals.
Melbourne Australasian remarks, for the
several-millionth time, that if Protection can’t
justify its existence by supplying the worker with
» fair wage, it ought to be abolished. What’s the
matter with the equally logical theory, that if
r reetrade can t justify its existence by supplying
the worker with a fair wage, it should be abolished ’
Protection isn’t the only thing that should be
oailed upon to justify its existence.
Down South, New South Wales is being
constantly held up as an exemplar in the matter of
religious instruction in schools. “ All teachers
irrespective of creed, are required to teach these
Scriptural lessons, and in no case has any refusal
to do so taken place, nor has any complaint ever
been made to the department that the lessons have
been made light of.” The Bulletin will be glad
to hear, m confidence, from N.S.W. public-school
teachers on this question.

The chilled-steel cheek of the syndicate
made what looks like an audacious attempt on the
public safe of Tasmania the other day. A depu-
tation meekly requested the Minister for Works
flo advance a A for A subsidy to pay the survey-
cost of part of the Great Western Gridiron Co.’s
route to the silver west ! As the Minister for
once happened to be in a wakeful mood the
monkey didn t get the nuts, but, as no Tasmanian
Minister—least of all Pillinger—can be expected
w> stay awake always, and as the formidable
Baker-Palmer syndicate never sleeps, the sooner
the Commonwealth establishes a suzerainty over
Tasmania’s money-box the better for Tasmania’s
taxpayers.
How can any Government, not able, with
the law at its hand, to suppress the Sunday selling
of liquor, hope to suppress liquor all the week by
any imaginable kind of local option? Notwith-
standing patrolling, and officious police, and a
drastic Sunday law, 23 out of every 25 pubs, in
N.S.W. carry on Sunday trading. Will not the
Joe Cook brigade, if successful legislatively,
merely spot the land with sly grog-shops ?
F. : “The poison works. The idea that
N.S.W. and Vic. should first federate, and dictate
terms of admission of other States to the federa-
tion, has become one of the possibilities of the
day. The two provinces are prominent factors of
the _ Australian system, and practically are Aus-
tralian. The accidental omission of Tas. and S. A.
would not matter much—and, for the present,
W.A. and Q. are better out of it. Q. has the
Kanakas, and W.A. has Forrest.”
Manager Lock, of Lucknow (N.S.W.),
solemnly warns the miners that, if they don’t
behave better, they will “ frighten away that
British capital which is the mainstay of the mining
industry.” Every time in the last 30 years that
there has been a labor difficulty in Australia the
tremendous British person has arisen to state
that if the worker didn’t knock under British
capital would leave the country, and the conse-
quences of its departure would be simply dreadful.
Why doesn’t the miserable thing go, instead of
always shaking an impotent fist at nothing in this
fashion ?
Yarrabanker Fleming indulged in some
pretty plain speaking at a late meeting of unem-
ployed. His remarks were very thinly veiled
allusions to the fate of highly-piaced people in
other lands who were negligent of their duties to
the poor. Melb. Herald published them, and
they looked so strong in cold print that much
surprise has been expressed that no official action
has followed. At the meeting itself abjections
were made to the speech, but were silenced by the
chairman’s remark that he couldn’t and wouldn’t
stop the speaker while the Govt, was so indifferent
to the claims and needs of the unemployed.
The appeal to the N.S.W. workers by
Hughes, M.L.A., to beware of Freetraders and
Protectionists alike is, in an indirect sort of way,
another evidence of the difficulty of “ sinking the
fiscal issue.” It is all very good to appeal to the
toiler to keep clear of both fiscal creeds and
parties, but unless a man proposes to prohibit im-
ports altogether he must, if he has any intelligence
at all, have some idea whether it is best to tax
them or not to tax them. While he has such an
idea he can’t sink the fiscal issue ; he may say
he does, but that is all. The fiscal issue is a
buoyant spectre which keeps rising no matter how
much it is sunk, and the Labor-party will have to
realise the fact some day.
SKETCHES AT THE CONVENTION.
Fourteen lawyers are already announced
as candidates at Vic. general election for Mel-
bourne and suburbs alone.
When Labor-member Turley arose, the
other evening, in Q. Assembly and moved his
resolution for the establishment of a minimum wage
it was considered “advisable” to talk the matter
out rather than go to a vote. The time for private
business expired at 6 p.m. and at four minutes to
six Mat Battersby was put up to fill in time. Poor
Battersby in his normal condition—and he was
painfully normal that night—is as nervous as a
hunted hen, and he talked coherently for iust
30sec. I hen he lost the thread of his discourse
lost his subject, his knowledge of the English
language—everything except his footing, and he
tottered even on that. For the remaining 3Amin
he only perspired and gibbered. And at last he
hadn t even another gibber in him, but just then
the long hand of the clock scraped into the middle
ot the A.M, and Speaker Cowley rose with a little
“ a " s dignity from his chair. As he did so
Matthew Battersby found his voice and said two
coherent words. They were “ Thank God > ”
If rumor correctly says that Jack Want is
going to give up the duties (mostly fishing !) of
N.S. W. Attorney-General, Reid will be the only
mere human being left in the Ministry. The
balance is all seraph !
AT THE ELECTRICAL EXHIBITION.
COOK (cooking by electricity) : “ Yes, ma’am, this
cake is cooked by the electric currents.”
INTERESTED VISITOR : “ Them wouldn’t be o’ much

electric carraway seeds?”
The Akgus now daily throws mud at its
old idol, Robert Harper. The day after the
general elections of ’94, when Patterson was
routed, it turned on him and rent him. The
next year Murray Smith accepted its dictum that
a 25 per cent, tariff was demanded by public and
Parliament. M. Smith moved accordingly in the
Assembly and was dished. Next morning the
Argus viciously turned on poor Smith. In the
same way it abandoned Freetrade for revenue cus-
toms, and now it is weakening in its adherence to
secular education, and evinces a sneaking intention
of going over to the Holy Joe crowd. The “ un-
bending Tories ” of Victoria are loud in complaint.
Whatever else happens it is hardly pro-
bable that Westralia will come into the Federation
for two or three years. The Red Dust Land is
willing to be taken oyer if the terms are based on
the assumption that its customs revenue will con-
tinue three times as large per head as that of the
other provinces, and to transfer its railways if it is
assumed that they will always earn 12 per cent, on
cost, the price to be fixed accordingly. In other
words, W. A. is willing to sell itself at boom prices.
But the other provinces “have been there,” and
understand that the boom is like the comet —a
hurried, bright thing with a long tail. A few
years hence W.A. will be going much cheaper,
and then the Commonwealth may do a deal.
Richards, the dreary British M.P. now
pervading this country, says he is agreeably sur-
prised at the politeness with which he has been
treated, although you have manhood suffrage
in Australia.” Apparently this strange person
thought that in a country where there was man-
hood suffrage the leading citizens always aimed
road-metal at the passing stranger. But what
Richards really meant to express was his astonish-
ment at finding that he, a 20th-rate member of
British Parliament, to whom no London daily
would give two lines per annum except in virtue
of his office, should be taken seriously by poli-
ticians and clubmen, and be interviewed by the
minor press, and have his ignorance solemnly
published to the world as if he was Somebody.
The Australian reverence for a foreign Ass in-
variably “agreeably surprises ” the ass.
Ben Tillett on the Queen and other things
(Adelaide Chronicle) :
No Democrat has a word to say against her. She is a
very brilliant woman, who has had around her men who
govern, and perhaps the best men she can get. They
have not been worth much, but they were the best she
could get. If there had been a king on the throne we
should in all probability have been governed by the worst
women he could get. That is the difference.
Que i"y : Does Ben Tillett talk like this so as to
get down to what he thinks is the level of the Aus-
tralian intelligence? Slush of the kind is good
enough in Peacock’s dreadful School Paper, but
the average grown man has a distinct idea that,
even if a British king got the very worst woman
he could find, Britain would be run by a Govern-
ment supported by a popular majority just the
same. How is it proposed that that particularly
bad woman would start to govern the empire, any-
Two years ago the Vie.-Bible-in-State-
school-advocates clamored for a plebiscite on the
question. Then the S. A. plebiscite was held, and,
by an overwhelming majority, the pietists were
beaten. Immediately the Vic. people decided that
they weren’t taking any ” more Referendum,
lhey are now making it one of the side-issues of
the general-elections, and voters are being can-
vassed to sign a pledge against opponents of State
aid to religion. The dodge is meeting with rather
f'han usual, the average opportunist
candidate finding it easier to placate the church
party than to give serious attention to the merits
of its case. Some of these candidates are now dis-
covering the mistake of surrender. The R.C.
bishops have issued a manifesto denouncing the
movement as threatening the “Catholic children,
of whom many thousands attend the State schools ”
so that the ‘Catholic Vote” seems lost to the
opportunists. This is considered a grave matter,
because the C.V. is reckoned to be numerically
stronger than the abject followers of Stiggins,
Chadband and co.

*?, *‘ls Murray and Darling, should be the site of

There are already signs—very, very faint,
is true, but signs none the less—of a change in
the Convention atmosphere. Westralia is a shade
less cocky than it was—with a failing revenue, and
a depleted exchequer, and the evident impossibility
of getting many more loans, the prospeot of
federation is a little more attractive. Victoria,
since the last Convention meeting, has produced
the biggest surplus in Australia, small as it was,
and with a certainty of returning prosperity it
feels more confident. N.S. W. has shown a falling
revenue and another deficit, and the other pro-
vinces are a trifle less inclined to put up with its
pretentions to be the one solvent member of the
group—the financial rock of the Commonwealth.
Ihe change is barely perceptible, yet there is a
difference none the less.
Among the Convention delegates Reid,
M‘Millan and Deakin promise rather better
than they did at last meeting. Deakin has
become a convert to railway federation, and Reid
and M Millan both state that they are now pre-
pared to trust the Federal Parliament to deal
fairly and honestly with the surplus instead of
tying it down to impossible conditions. As no
one can tell in advance what the Federal tariff will
be, how much it will yield, or how much of the
total will be furnished by each province (seeing

Mr. A. A. Atkinson, chief inspector
N.S.W. coal mines, arrived in Sydney a few days
ago, and forthwith enters upon the duties of about
as difficult an office as exists under Government.
The causes of friction between employers and
coal- miners in N.S.W. are not on all-fours with
English causes, and at present the friction in
N.S.W. is in many parts feverish. Mr. Atkinson
comes as the elect of 93 candidates, 11 of whom
were Australians. He will have an important
and practical say in the fate and fortunes of over
12,000 bitter-memoried men.
One of the ‘ ‘ fasting men ” of a few years
back was in the Sydney Divorce Court the other
day at least, his wife was there. There was an
“ Effie ” in the case, but nothing else remarkable
except that the fasting gentleman wrote over
lately from Maoriland to his “dear wife and
children,” asking if they had any objection to his
marrying again; then, after wishing them all
sorts of happiness, he signed himself—“ Still your
fond husband, Charlie.” The wife’s application
for divorce was about the most emphatic reply
possible that she had no objection.
Lord Kintore, ex-Gov. of S.A., lost his eld-
est son by death lately, and Adelaide City Coun-
cil proceeded to condole with him. Whereupon
Councillor Sellar proposed that the relatives of
some ordinary mister who had lately died, after
being a good citizen for many years, should also
rbe condoled with. The horror of the thing smote
the entire body. An Aid. Johnson gasped that it
was “ a, shame to burlesque a death which meant
such deep grief to the Earl of Kintore ’’—although
Sellar hadn’t burlesqued any death at all. When
the L others got their wind, they begged the re-
porters to suppress the incident, and Tucker,
representing dignity and virtue—Charles Tucker,
of the Queen Margaret No. 1 South—arose “ and
administered a dignified rebuke.” He “hoped
that he would never have occasion to so severely
reprimand a councillor again.” Then Adelaide’s
great heart beat again.
The bitter row which some loyal Victorians
and the Age and sundry casual Mayors have
raised over the drinking of the Queen’s health at
that Melbourne Trades Hall banquet seems a very
squalid affair. The row is in two chapters. One
is to the effect that when the health was proposed
there was a long, disloyal hesitation before any-
body rose, and the explanation given is that the
toast was proposed suddenly without the usual
previous invitation to fill up, and the banqueters
only delayed because they had to grab for the
bottle before they could go on with the ceremony.
Considering how the Tory press has gibed for
years past about the alleged beery and whisky-
■'fied habits of the Trades Hall people, it
is altogether too late for it to pretend that
the sons of toil hesitated at anything that
had liquor in it. The other half of the trouble is
over the fact that Ben Tillett called Her Gracious
an “ old lady.” As she has reigned 60 years she
presumably is old, and if the loyal press reckons
that she isn’t a lady it would be a great deal more
decent for it to lie low and say nothing about it.
Any woman who has lived to a good age, and
never done anything especially bad, and kept her-
self clean and tidy, and brought up a large family
respectably, and never allowed her male offspring
to fight with the boy next door while they were
making their mud-pie together in the gutter,
might be called a lady without straining the
British language to any very great extent.

Nine portraits of ex-presidents hang in the
Wellington (M.L.) Chamber of Commerce—three
are those of Jews, and four are of Scotchmen.
Graham Berry is practically guaranteed
re-election to the Vic. Speakership if his constitu-
ents send him back to the House. And they are
pretty certain to send the veteran back.
Albury (N.S.W.) Municipal Council now
pays 6s. lid. per day for the services of an able-
bodied man, horse and cart. The tenders ran
from 10s, to 6s. lid., and the Council took the
lowest.

conducted Sir George and Lady Turner over the
Tower of London.” And some have fame thrust
upon them.

The opponents of lawyer Sam Gillott, in
Melb. City Council, profess to think that lie was

and paragraph-notice in The Bulletin. No
public man inspires pars, about himself in this
scurrilous newspaper.
Glass tombstones are now being exten
sively manufactured in Birmingham, Eng. Plate-
glass resists wind, rain, heat and cold much better
than even granite, owing to the homogeneity of
the mass. One advantage of glass tombstones:
people will be able to see through the epitaphs.
The prospects of freetrader Max Hirsch
in Mandurang (Vic.) electorate are variously rep-
resented by Age and Argus. Max, according to
the Age, is the voice of one crying hopelessly in
the wilderness, whereas the Argus edition of Max
is a flourishing candidate who gets gigantic votes
of confidence from crowded meetings.
The Reid Govt., dragging desperately for
odd ha’pence, has begun to collect a £1 license fee
from all “whalers ” along the western rivers who
fish from boats. There probably is not a class in all
Australia who see less cash than these poor
beggars, and the only consolation is that the
license fee will take even more chasing than the
land-tax.
In one of Pastor Isitt’s Adelaide speeches
he railed at the parsons who crawled to the boss
brewers, and others interested in the tanglefoot
trade, for the sake of their support. In the same
column of the S. A. Register reporting the
speech appeared an account of an “At home,”
given by boss brewer, E. T. Smith, to the London
Missionary Society !

COACHING STALLION LORD BRASSEY.
Lord Bras-iey has taken many first prizes in Sydney
and surrounding districts, is a sure foal-getter, and
possesses an excellent temper.
The squabbles in S. A. Labor ranks brought
about a very mixed state of affairs. Charleston,
M.L.C., who resigned from the party, in his can-
didature for re-election had the support of the
Single Taxers, the National Ass crowd, and a
portion of the Democratic party —a truly lovely
alliance. Hutchison, the direct Labor candidate,
was supported by the main body of the Labor-
party and many bigoted Tories who couldn’t swal-
low Charleston’s single-tax views. Charleston was
elected.
“ Spoke to-day to a ’fore-the-mast survivor
of a recent sensational wreck, and tried hard to
pump him, but failed to extract anything beyond
the mere ‘ yes ’ or ‘ no,’ and a casual remark that
ship disasters were ‘ blanky good things to be out
of.’ I have never met a sailor-man who could
yarn intelligently of the sea, and conclude that
the garrulous salt constantly met with in novels is
either ‘ played out ’ or a sheer freak of the literary
imagination. ” —T.D.
Quote : The name of Mark Twain’s new
book is to be “ The Surviving Innocents
Abroad ”:—
Mrs. Clemens said, “It’s not true for you to call your-
self ‘The Surviving Innocent Abroad,’ because there’s
so-and-so in Cleveland, and that and the other in Phila-
delphia.” “ Oh,” replied Mark Twain, “ I’ll fix that.”
So in the new book there will be an explanatory note
pointing out that although there are still in existence
some eight or ten of the pilgrims who went on the Quaker
City expedition some 20 years ago, the author is the only
surviving one who has remained innocent.
“A well-known and popular clergyman—
Rev. James Kinghorn (Presbyterian), once of
Richmond, N.S.W.) has been presented, at
White Rock, in the Bathurst district, by parish-
ioners, with a writing-desk and stand. On seat-
ing himself in his buggy, prior to starting home,
the rev. gentleman found that a Chinaman whom
he had Christianised had put a present of a pair
of fowls in a bag under his seat.” If, in America,
you told that story, substituting a Christianised
nigger for a Christianised Chinaman, how Uncle
Sam would laugh 1

on debentures; and invests money on theirbehalf in public
securities in the Colonies or Great Britain; undertakes
the agency of other banks; issues drafts, circular notes
and letters of credit, and conducts all customary banking
business.
The Bank also issues Circular Notes in sums of £lO or
£2O, negotiable on the Continents of Europe and America,
or in India and the East.

after five supplies I was cured. I have kept well,
it being now over six months since 1 saw you last.

The crimson satin finish of the Red Bird commends
admiration, and proclaims its high estate as the per-
fect cycle.
CrcLES are coming down in price—that is, the kind
in opposition to Red Birds. Red Bird quality corres-
ponds with the Red Bird prices.
Be sure you’re right; and then don’t bet on it, es-
pecially if it is a match race.
One of the great features of the new Red Bird is
its bottom bracket. It is a triumph of modern skill
in cycle-buildinjr, and is a feature as far ahead of
bottom brackets in other cycles as the pneumatic tyre
is ahead of the solid. The two cups which form the
bearings are turned from the very finest grade of
steel possible to manufacture. They contain ten 516
balls. Each cup is threaded to a steel sleeve or barrel,
which adjusts inside the bottom bracket proper, and
is held in place by two bolts. With the bearing' it is
only necessary to oil the bearings about twice a year,
as they are thoroughly dust-proof and oil-retaining,
the balls running in a continuous oil bath. With
other cycles it is almost impossible to get a perfectly
true chain alignment, whereas the chain and the
bearings, with the aid of this most important im-
provement, can be adjusted to the l/1000th part of an
inch. This is only one of the many new and original
features on Red Bird new models.
Before you select your mount for the coming
season, call on all the cycle-dealers and examine all
their machines. Then pay us a visit, and you will see
the immense superiority of Red Bird over all other
cycles. Red Birds are the perfect cycles with the
crimson satin finish.
Some bicycles can be bought for a song ; but repairs
amount to a Melba song if the selection is not right
at the start. The Red Bird never requires repairs.
Riding a Red Bird with oil-retaining bearings is like
pushing another cycle down-hill.
Bill Martin is going for records on the new Red
Bird. Look out! Something is going to drop soon.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The A. J.O. Derby running makes Aurum look
more than ever n certainty for the Fleiuington blue-
ribbon, and a decidedly “good thing,” as nood things go,
for Melb. Cup Five months ago, when Amberite was
already shaping like the second-best of the youngsters,
he had two cuts at W. R. Wilson’s crack, ‘in the first
instance, Aurum gave the Carbine colt 101 b. and beat
him a length over six furlongs, and the next time they
met Aurum gave 141 b. away and won by three lengths,
the distance being one fur-longer than before.
* * * *
Maluma and Merman figure among the entries
for the big autumn struggles at Newmarket, and if Aus-
tralian racers are to assert themselves in England the
time for an assertion has nearly arrived. Merman, at
any rate, was quite sufficiently acclimatised to run away
with a minor prize at Lewes a few weeks back. If he goes
for the Ciesarewitch it will be on his merits, we may sup-
pose, and the weight served out to him will be lenient,
considering that he ranked as a “ crack” on his last Aus-
tralian performances. As for Maluma, the mare who
took first-class honors at Fleinington, now getting treated
as a third-rater by the English handicapper : unless she
can capture something of importance before the present
English season closes we must conclude that either the
change pf climate has shattered her constitution or that
“ form ” out here is not nearly up to the English standard.
Meantime we have some more unflattering evi-
dence on the latter point. Imported horse Positano
showed his heels last Saturday to a batch of five Aus-
tralian “cracks”— i.c., the five top-weights in the Melb.
Cup handicap. Nobody needs to make climatic excuses
for the English horses that came to Australia. Of the few
imported within the past 10 or 11 years, three, at least,
have shown fine performances—Lochiel, the infirm
Russley, and Positano. These three facts, together
with the more striking fact that Carbine was
quite English —by imported Musket from the
then new - chum Mersey suggest that thorough-
bred horse stock is not likely to reach its highest
pitch of development in Australia. What price Positano
for the Caulfield and Melb. Cups on his Saturday’s running
at Randwiek? Newhaven’s eas3’ capture of the Spring
Stakes a year ago was considered marvellous. Positano
wins the same event quite as easily, in somewhat faster
tune, and he will (bar penalties) meet Coil and co. on
nearly two-stone better terms in the Cups.
* » * *
As usual, Randwiek proved a facer for backers
on Saturday last. Alaska, an Bto 1 show, won the Hurdle
Race, and then, barring the Derby, which looked and
proved a walk-over for Amberite, nothing backed by the
public won any of the four remaining races. For the
Trial Stakes, Agate, Woodford, and Nor'-East were much
fancied, but Nor’-East nearly fell on his nose opposite the
Leger, and the 10 to 1 show, Zitella, won by a length and
a-half from Agate. Survivor started favorite for Epsom
Handicap at 3 to 1, hut it was not to be, as Robin Hood
(33 to 1) jumped off with the lead and was never headed,
beating the favorite by three lengths. Also, 6to 4 was
called against Coil for the Spring Stakes, but he could not
get any nearer than third. The unthought-of Positano, at
10 to 1, altered his position from last to first after round-
ing the home turn, winning as he liked from True Blue.
And then, just by way of filling the backers’ cup to the
brim, the absolutely friendless King Clovis ran away with
Kensington Handicap.
• * * *
The A.J.C. executive has earned the thanks of
its Leger patrons by now heisting jockeys' names with
numbers, and also registering the “bookies” who ply
their business in that enclosure. But can they not go
one better, and extend similar privileges to the numerous
“ bob ” patrons of the Flat ?
* * * *
“ Chueked-in ” oldsters formed two of the first
three in Epsom. Of late, owners of “good ’uns” have
frequently been beaten by this “postage-stamp” brigade.
* * * - *
The old glory of the Randwiek Derby seems to
have quite departed from it. Four runners this year,
and 7 to 4 laid upon a winner, who is probably not within
211 b. of the St. Albans’ champion.
* * * +
Bookmakers don’t know everything, even about
their own horses. Spindrift (owned by a Melb. bookie)
after being backed by him unsuccessfully on five occa-
sions, for “ tons of money,” romped in unbacked last week
at tens to one. The bookmaker’s disgust at the resurrec-
tion of the dead is accentuated by the knowledge that the
horse was “a starting price job,” and that he (the bookie)
had laid most of the money.
Considering the present state of the Australian
turf, Joe Thompson must be considered a far-seeing man
in going to England when he did.
» * • *
Eureka ! Rumored re the mysterious Reka that
he is in the hands of the vet. ; that he has been backed in
the right quarter to win a couple of the big spring races ;
that he has been “spelling” in Illawarn ; that he is
undergoing a big preparation at Sam Cook’s private San-
dringham course ; that he is located within a few miles of
the Sydney G.P.O. This much is certain, Reka has already
proved a dear horse to the public.
» * * *
Surcingle: “ ‘ The Bookmaker,’ at Melb. Bijou,
is a decent play with a smack of character. Refreshing,
indeed, and how is it nobody has held the mirror up to
Nature in a local fashion? What a mine to the future
dramatist is Joe Thompson Solomon, who said he first
landed at Sandridge as an escapee from shipboard, rolled
ashore by the waves in a barrel ! Then there is leviathan
Slack, whose wife maintained a Primitive Methodist chapel
at Prahran. He couldn’t write, but made hieroglyphs in
his book, which Ward the barber, of Bourke-street, wrote
up for him at night, because pious Mrs. Slack wouldn’t
lay a hand to it. Nathless the winnings came in tidy for
the chapel, which has long bunged, and been sold to
another denomination.”
« * * *
An effort is being made to introduce water-
jumps and furze hedges on Flemington Steeplechase
course. This will be foolish. Only a small proportion of
Australian leppers are hunters, and, even if they are,
hedges and small jumpable watercourses are as uncommon
here as snakes in Ireland. With English horses it is
different; they are born and bred among brooks and
hedges, and are consequently accustomed to “taking”
them before ever a man crosses their backs. The furze
fences on Ballarat racecourse are invariably ridden
through, and the water-jump at Moonee Valley is far
from being an unqualified success.
Apes of some of Stoddart’s team : —Stoddart,
34 ; Briggs, 34; Hearne, 30 ; Ranjitsinhji, 24 ; Hayward,
20 ; Hirst, 25; and Richardson, 26.
* * * *
St. Peter’s College, Adelaide, will shortly
benefit by a huge legacy left it years afro. Head Master
Girdlestone already talks of securing A. U. Maclaren, the
Lancashire batsman (who sighs for a collegiate billet in
Australia), and making athletics an attraction.
* * * *
Adelaide senior cricket starts this year on the
electorate system with seven clubs instead of six as here-
tofore. All the cracks play for different districts.
Another advantage arising from the change is the move-
ment for local grounds, which will greatly benefit S.A.
cricket, as it has that of N.S.W.
* * • •
Some of the Australians who have succumbed
to Hearne’s bowling in England don’t expect him to
trouble them much on our wickets, but this sort of ex-
pectation is very subject to disappointment. Veteran
Blackham of yore predicted that Richardson would have
the cover knocked off him when he came to Australia,
nevertheless swarthy Tom got through his first visit with
a fine average.

* * * *
Puff notices of the coming English Eleven des-
cribe Hearne as a dangerous bat, whereas the truth is
that he gobs in last man for Middlesex, and generally gets
disposed of for a unit. Richardson will probably take
precedence of Hearne in the Britisher’s order of batting,
and, with Briggs likewise on duty, the team will have a
distinct tail.
* * * *
The football-match between N.S.W. and Queens-
land, played at Brisbane on Saturday last in aid of the
Q. Rugby Union, proved a close and exciting game. At
half-time the game stood—N.S.W., 11 points; Q., 10
points. But N.S.W. had much the better of the second
half, and the game ended N.S.W. 19 points to Q s 13.
* * * »
Taking Vic. football season as a whole, the
games have been good, and though the wild, hilarious
times of ’B9-’95 are still afar, there has been publio enthu-
siasm if not excitement. That this is greatly due to the
football league, formed for money-grabbing purposes pure
and simple, is impossible to deny. The division of the
clubs into two departments—weak and strong—though it
has almost killed the weak, has made the strong stronger,
and has invested their matches with a degree of interest
unobtainable under the old order. If the doctrine of
the survival of the fittest, rather than that hitherto
accepted of fair play, be admissible in sport, Vic. Football
League is something to be praised. The Association,
made up of the old cast-out clubs, has gone one better
than the League in the theory of the game, though its
clubs do not possess the talent to demonstrate it in
practice. This is in the number of players. The League
clubs play 20 men a-side, the Association 18, two being
knocked off the number of the followers. This prevents
the crowding on the ball which so frequently spoils Vic.
football, and gives a clean, open game. It is to be hoped
that next year some combination of (he two bodies will
be formed under the League’s system of pooling all gate-
money and dividing it equally among the constituting
clubs.
» * * *
S.A. football premiership goes to Port Adelaide.
The first time the South has been deposed for years—a
good thing for what is left of the game.

in the middlin’ ones?”
* * • »
Road-racing by cyclists has become very common
in Albert Park, Melb. On Saturday week the park trustees-
caused the gates to be shut in the riders’ faces during the
progress of one of these races. Someone asks “Why
didn’t the park officials inform the club before the start V'
The answer is easy, and can best be put in question form r
“ Do road-racing cyclists really imagine that the highway
is their exclusive property? Why should anyone be en-
titled to a caution against indulging in furious cycle-
riding, when he is not entitled to a caution against the
committal of wilful murder or burglary?”
* * * *
The brothers James (Fred and Bert), late of
Melb. Rowing Club and well-known as members of differ-
ent Vic. iriterprovincial crews, have reached Coolgardie
as the first stage of a Perth to Melb. bicycle trip. The
Jameses are both big, strapping fellows—in fact, they
belong to that class who would be better athletes were
they weaker men. Being each possessed of a good pri-
vate income the Jameses have been in a position to do-
the trip irrespective of the bike-agent, and as there is no-
suspicion of trade about- it, their version may be looked
forward to with interest. A sister of the bikers, a dash-
ing girl of the Junoesque type and a olever whip, is one of
the handsomest of Melb.’s sassiety damsels.
* * '* *
Widgeree : Under amateur laws, a competitor-
must- not ‘ sell, realise, barter, or otherwise turn into cash;
any prize won by him ’ —yet so-called amateur races are
held with bicycles as trophies. Must the winner hang on
for life to the machines he wins, no matter how obsolete-
they may become—or, at any rate, does he? Again,
tyres are often given as trophies. Must the tvre-winner
watch those tyres throughout all time, mend their
punctures, and worry over them, and yet be unable to-
sell his cycle at the finish because it is shod with these
tyres? Amateurism seems to consist chiefly of giving a-
man something he doesn’t want.”
* * » *
“ Signs of trouble re next year’s Austral meet-
ing are still visible. The case stands thus: Vic. League-
was cajoled into giving its patronage to the Melb. Ground,
for the occasion, on condition that the track was im-
proved—the exact nature of such improvements not being
specified. Melb. Bicycle Club, having this patronage,
has gone ahead with the advertising, &c., and has
hinted that in the event of the League withdrawing
patronage it will sue for exes, to date. Legal opinion!
supports it. Now, the Racing Cyclists’ Association:
has considered the matter, and a mass meeting of com-
petitors has been arranged to consider the advisableness:
of declining to enter for the meeting, unless on a different
ground. No doubt the track is dangerous” (writes-
“ Handlebar” to Tiie Bulletin) “not because of the
surface so much as by reason of the want of banking.
In fact, at one corner the track is, so to speak, banked
the wrong way, and to attempt to negotiate this corner
at full speed is absolute madness. In one heat of last
year’s Austral no less than 15 competitors fell at this par-
ticular place.”
* * * *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League, in declining to in-
flict any punishment on Bilmartin for having “ gone for”
a hooter, acted in a sensible and sportsmanlike spirit.
Martin is now out with a challenge to any Australian
rider, including Reynolds, to ride a match of three paced
races of one, five; and ten miles, for £lOO aside. Nobody
responds, so plugger Bill sits round in the cool and fills
up with strawberries and cream. When Barden and
Platt-Betts arrive, however; Martin will have to think a
lot harder and run no mucks.

Thirty-six started for the N.S.W. League’s 55-
miles’ road-race, over the Parramatta-Windsor-Penrith
course, but punctured tyres, fee., brought the number
down to a score by the time Windsor was reached. D.
Harvison soon took the lead, finishing eventually with
any amount to spare, in 3h. 30sec.
Curious that the final of the world’s mile at
Glasgow should have been left to a German, an English-
man, and a Frenchman. Placed as named, too. No
beastly exclusiveness about the wheel !

The bike is playing-np with aquatic sports in
Sydney Harbor. Men who have been sailing fora life-
time have taken to the wheel. One of the old-time crack
rowing clubs is in a had way. The caretaker has not
received any pay for months, and the only people who
use the boats are half-a-dozen oldsters, who are trying to
keep down condition.

A. 11. Stewart, hero of the recent Sydney Gr.immar
School sports, went down before N. N. G. Levick in the

V V * *
As expected, Nnttall, the English champion,
“ swam ” away from the puffy Ernie Cavill in their
600vds. match. Sydney swimmers in the know laughed
at the idea of the free-living Cavill, who had pretty well
given over the game, coming out on the spur of the
moment to meet such a "tiger” as Nultall. A Sydney
publican and racehorse-owner seems to have put up a
portion of Cavill’s stake, which might just as well have
been made a present to the Englishman.
* * • *
The fact that one of the Cavill family is a pro.
and the other an amateur is another illustration of the
peculiarities of amateurism.
* • • •
Lou. Casimer, who, a few years ago, was, with
the exception of Tom Malone, considered the speediest
sprinter in Australia, has just been awarded the Royal
Humane Society’s medal for life-saving at Mudgee. He
rescued a child from a burning house and mace a gallant
attempt to preserve two other lives. His case is a
pleasant offset against those of the many peds. who have
“ gone to the dogs."
* * * *
An old-time foot-runner tells a story parallel to
the “ Billiard-marker’s Yarn.” It occurred in the days
when Frank Hewitt was still very fleet of foot. A big,
black-bearded “country-man” (one of the best athletes
who ever left Ireland) struck Hewitt and his crowd, and
showed the possession of considerable cash. Flynn, of
Gippsland, as he was known, was quickly led on to talk of
his running-powers, and a match was fixed up between
him and the Englishman for 50yds., at £'so a side. Flynn
put up his own £5O, while Hewitt’s was, as the mining-
promoters say, “ over-subscribed.” The day came, and
the race. Hewitt stood on the mark, full of easy con-
fidence. When the pistol fired Flynn bolted, and simply
lost his opponent. But he was over-honest; had he not
shown his hand thus, his beard would have let him into
several “ Botanys.”
* m * •
The other day four boys were prosecuted for
hunting a kangaroo at Frankston, Vic. The boys were
small, and they chased the kangaroo with an ungainly
and amiable pup. The kangaroo took refuge in a man’s
garden, and the man slew if, with a rail. The boys were
fined. A week or two earlier Melb. papers gave profuse
pictures and much thrilling letterpress to the pourtrayal
of a kangaroo-hunt in which members of the vice-regal
party and suite took a prominent part. This party killed
one kang. and captured a joey. Police prosecutions will
follow, of course, we don’t think. There is not one law for
the high and one for the low —there is only one for the
low.
* * * *
Billiardist John Roberts uses billiard-balls as
barometers, and can forecast the weather thereby for
hours ahead. When a storm approaches, the click of the
balls loses its sharpness, and the rebound lessens in elas-
ticity. As the weather improves, the click and rebound
both rise toward the normal.

This is a point which economists should study.

Only common consistency in that American
shutting-down upon the cinematographic reproduction of
the Oorbett-Fitzsimmons battle. If an actual fight, of an
hour or so is immoral and illegal, why less so a photo-
graphic encounter that would never come to an end as
long as people liked to pay a shilling to witness it ?

* • • •
Four reporters attended a prize-fight at Buffalo
(U.S.A.) in a professional capacity and were captured by
the police. They were brought' up before a Judge, who
promptly released them, declaring that it was a principle
of law, as well as of common-sense, that three kinds of
men were permitted to go anywhere without blame-
doctors, clergymen, and reporters.

to 1 he’ll go back to work again after sport has taken all
the good out of him.”
