A notice in the advertising columns of this
issue invites the public not to “ bet in the dark” but buy
the “Turf Pilot” and be enlightened and illuminated.
The idea of being illuminated by a pilot instead of being
kept off the rocks is rather a confusion of metaphor, but
possibly the pilot lights up the scene by spontaneous
combustion arising through a long career of hard gin-
drinking. Anyhow, there is a lot of valuable information
in the “ Pilot,” and if anything can shed the light of
reason on so irresponsible an animal as the racehorse this
volume will do it.


The £250 compensation awarded the peti-
tioner in a recent Sydney divorce-case was well-
intended by the jury, but how will it look when,
after the costs, as taxed, have been paid by the
other side, petitioner has to shell-out, himself, for
the balance as between attorney and client of his
side’s expenses of a four-days’ trial 1
A recent issue of Sydney Star, comment-
ing on the population-returns, showed that, of
13,000 odd increase, over 12,000 odd were attribut-
able to natural increase—births over deaths. Next
day’s Telegraph came out with the startling
statement that the increase of over 13,000 showed
that “ our manufacturing industries were in a
healthy state ” !
The latest development in Papua.—A few
days ago McGregor ascended the Membare with
intent to punish the Papuan “murderers ’’ of Mr.
Green and party. Dropped across the offending
tribe, and found about eight of them armed with
rifles, some with two apiece. . . . The Acting-
Cormnandant and party actually fled a consider-
able distance, but the dour old Scotchman insisted
on a return, and a combat eventuated, which re-
sulted in sundry dead niggers and the dispersal of
the offending elan. But rifles ! This is some-
thing new for the Papuan !
No greater luxury can be imagined than a
<jool drink to a thirsty animal, and, apart from the
humanitarian view of the matter, Pitt, Son and
Badgery Ltd. are making their water supply for
stock profitable to owners. It is surprising the
improvement in appearance of long rail journey
stock when they get a good drink, and now the
hot weather is coming all stock looking dusty or
rough get a drink and a swim at their big dam.
This firm has spent £1)000 in providing water and
other accommodation ; and it has the only water
adjoining the sale yards, hence good results.*
A stranger arose at a recent meeting of
Melb. Freetrade Democratic Association and
gravely requested to be allowed to relieve himself
of some opinions. Then he went on in a weighty
manner to inform the meeting that at one time he
was a Protectionist; he once believed, he said,
that Freetrade was only a beautiful theory, utterly
impracticable. He enlarged at much length on
his former opinions, the inference being that in
those days he was a mental incapable, an ignora-
mus, and several kinds of an ass. “ Gentlemen,”
he said, in conclusion, “I believed in those times
that Freetrade was arrant folly, and that the only
hope for the country lay in a Protective policy,
and it gives me great pleasure to be able to declare
now before you all— that I believe so still.” Before
the meeting had recovered sufficiently to kick him
the solemn orator had passed out.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
An interesting railway case was lately heard
in Melb. A woman had her fingers jammed by a
closing door, and negligence was alleged against
the railway department on account of the fact that
the edge of the seat on which she sat was flush
with the door. The Department fought hard for
a non-suit, and put several big guns into the box
to give evidence on the point. Plaintiff’s case
was well fought by Andrews, a recently-admitted
barrister, who managed to win on the non-suit,
but to the Department’s great relief the jury
found in its favor on the facts. A defeat would
have meant a reconstruction of very many Vic.,
and a still greater number of N.S.W. and Tas.,
carriages. And the point’s not really settled yet.
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes: “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address —J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*

“ How to double our party,” was the in-
teresting theme of an anxious Tory’s letter in a
recent Abgus. The obvious plan of only looking
at the party when suffering the |visual conse-
quences of a good, old champagne drunk, was not
suggested, and yet it is the only plan.
Dear Bulletin, —Prayer for rain does
sometimes answer, despite the scoffing of The
Bulletin and other unbelievers. We had a
drought recently in Maoriland —not much of a
drought, perhaps, but a very good one for us.
One Methodist sect in a Hawkes Bay township
proposed having a school picnic, and a rival
Dipper sect prayed for rain—presumably to spoil
the show. But the weather kept fine, and as the
next best thing the Dippers arranged a picnic of
their own for the same day, to keep their people
away from the opposition orgy. It was held in a
secluded gully, and that afternoon, just as the
shivoo was in full blast, it rained like H—any-
thing. The moist saints started for home with
torrents rushing off their monkey beards, and as
they hoisted the heavy saintesses over streams of
rain-water, and feasted their eyes on Sister Mary
Hann’s muddy underskirts and skinny ankles,
they realised the efficacy of prayer and were not
at all comforted.—R.T
Ed. Bulletin, —Now this cruel crawl is
over, and the might of the record-reign extolled
to a finish, it is worth while glancing at the record
of a queen who occupied the throne for the
shortest term among our female monarchs. Of
kings and queens, the truth is not spoken of the
quick but of the dead. Of Mary the Second, it is
recorded that she deprived herself, by taking
money from her household funds, to buy bread
for the poor. In this, there is an approach to the
widow s mite in contradistinction to the Windsor
widow’s might. There is not much gaud and
glitter about Mary’s short record, but it shone
bright in the memory of her poorer subjects. Says
Macaulay—“ Mary’s blameless life, her large
charities, and her winning manners had con-
quered the hearts of her people.” She hadn’t 60
years and unlimited opportunities to do it in.
Again, the historian says that “no scheme had
been so much her own, none had been so near her
heart, as that of converting the palace at Green-
wich into a retreat for seamen.” Where’s Victoria’s
anty to Mary’s chip? Victoria “isn’t in it” with
Mary : Mary is praised for the good she did, Vic-
toria for the bad she didn’t.—Tui.

An original petition. One ward at the
big Salpetriere hospital, Paris, is old and dilapi-
dated, and the bug which walks by night lately
rendered sleep impossible to its occupants. Re-
monstrances and requests for relief being unavail-
ing, a large bug-hunt was organised one night by
all the patients who could walk or crawl, and a
bowlful of the smellsome insects was sent with
their compliments to the head of the Board of
Health, with a note to the effect that he would
receive a similar present every morning till things
were righted. That one bowlful did the trick.

Mr. Button writes later:-" The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Bose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

A Valuable Vessel Nearly Lost.
In the olden days when steam power was not so
universally applied to the propulsion of ocean
carriers as it is now, long voyages were seldom
undertaken in sailing ships excepting when neces-
sitated by matters of urgency and where no alter
native existed. Mr. Ernest Buckhold, residing at
226 George-street, Sydney, late chief steward of
s.s. “ Menmuir,” of the E. and A.S.S. Company,
is now 51 years of age and has been seafaring fot
32 years. The story of his miraculous escape from
death will, no doubt, prove acceptable to readers.
To a reporter he made the following statement
“ I arrived in Sydney in May, 1890, from Japan,
suffering severely from pains in the right leg.
The trouble increased daily to an acute stage. I
ascribe the malady to exposure and wet feet.
The pains were first in the hip, and occasionally
shot to the calf and the foot. I had no peace or
rest night or day. I could not sit down to a meal,
but had to lean heavily against the table. Tin-
pain took away all desire for food, and I could not
sleep. I had to leave the ship, and take to becfat
Ashfield. I got worse, and was bed-ridden, and
in great agony for three months. My hip grew
out, and the joint bone in the back protruded. I
endeavored to get about the house with the assist-
ance of two sticks. But it was a great struggle to
move, and caused excruciating pam. I could not
bear to put the foot to the floor. Two months
afterwards, in September, I had to take to bed
again, and for 70 nights morphia was injected. I
remained in bed till the 31st December last. In
January of this year I saw Captain Green, the
Superintendent of the E. and A.S.S. Company,
and he granted me a passage to Japan. This was
done with the hope that the change would effect a
cure. Five days after leaving Sydney the full
fury of the malady returned. In fact, it was
worse than ever. I was unable to go to the table
for food, and would rather go without a meal than
walk a short distance. The perspiration would-
pour out of me with the pain, which doubled me
up. The slightest movement meant- indescribable
torture. Yet there was no cessation from suffer-
ing while I kept quiet. When I arrived in Japan
I thought to take a position at Kobe, but I found
that the incessant pain incapacitated me for any
work. I was Compelled to return by the Aus-
tralian, and arrived in Sydney on the 13th May
last, a physical wreck, and a bag of bones, and
with undiminished pain. Two days later I met
Captain H. Craig, of the s.s. Guthrie, and he
advised me to try Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. At the same time he handed me £l, and
told me to get the pills at once, which I did. This
was a critical moment and I despaired of recovery.
I was like a vessel suddenly taken aback ; every-
thing seemed against me. 1 followed the directions
for taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and, after
using three boxes, I felt much improved. I con-
tinued with the fourth, with happy results. I had
four pills left from the 6th box, when I had
become perfectly strong, healthy, and robust. I
can now walk any distance without fatigue, and
suffer no pain whatever. My appetite is keen.
The recovery was gradual at first, and then rapid
towards absolute cure. I began the pills on the
15th May, and put the unswallowed four aside on
the 14th Jline. I have taken none since, for my
health has been thoroughly re-established. I
arranged with Captain Legg, of the s.s. Thornley,
to go as chief steward as far as Adelaide, to ascer-
tain how I would get along on a sea-voyage.
Well, the work was hard, and the weather bad,
and iny feet and clothing were often wet, but I
fared first-class, and suffered no ill-effects. I
returned to Sydney on the sth July by the
A.U.S.N. Company’s steamer Waroonga in per-
fect health and strength. I now feel fit for any
work.
“Yes, I passed through twelve months of un-
speakable suffering, which, I am sure, would have
been averted had I known of the extraordinary
efficacy of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. However, I trust my experience may
bring hope to the hearts of others suffering from
sciatica.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People posi-
tively cure rheumatism, sciatica, lumbago, and
neuralgia, and influenza’s after effects, and are a
specific for all diseases of women, such as anaemia,
poor and watery blood, female irregularities,
nervous headache and hysteria, and are sold by
chemists and storekeepers generally, or the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Company, Sydney, will for-
ward, on receipt of stamps or post order one box
for 2s. 9d. or half-dozen for 15s. 3d.

This association is being formed for the purpose
of acquiring and working auriferous ground in the
Klondyke (Yukon) District, N.W. Terrritory,
British Columbia, and for this purpose the services
of two skilled men, whose credentials are unim-
peachable, have already been retained, who will
proceed to the Klondyke when the necessary for-
malities in respect to registration have been com-
plied with. These men are thoroughly acquainted
with the character and mining laws of the Do-
minion, and every facility will be given by the
authorities on the other side to ensure their speedy
arri val on the field.
The share-list will shortly close, and intending
shareholders are notified that a full and decrip-
tive prospectus dealing with the geological fea-
tures, auriferous areas, climate, route to the fields,
and statistical information, with returns of gold
from advices just received from official sources in
Canada, may be obtained on application to

An interesting wail :
Editor Bulletin,— l presume you take it that The Bul-
letin is a fit and proper paper for any young- man to read ?
Cursed be the day I first read it! I was happy till then.
X believed in God, in priests and parsons, now I believe in
—nothing but Revolution. According to The Bulletin,
there was but one decent mah in the world —George
Higinbotham! When I was a “right-thinking” young
man I got on well in the world ; but now, alas! my Bul-
letin ideas get me the cold shoulder of decent, respect-
able people. I get into numerous rows with “ scabs ” and
other crawlers, which is rather awkward considering
how numerous they are. The Bulletin ideas are all right
if ohe was in an independent position. I have been
struggling now for 17 years and have been reading The
B. regularly, and I can’t get lower down in the world
than I am. So I think I will “ turn it up ” and take to
the Protestant Standard or the Catholic Times. A cove
told me once I would be “ always poor so long as I read
The Bulletin.” He Was a well-to-do Scotchman. Some
poor, deluded creatures call The Bulletin the “ Poor-
man’s Bible 1 ” This game of writing is not in my line,
but I thought I’d let you know I am beginning to think
The B. ain’t God no more than j. Bull is Creator of the
earth.— Parasite.
‘ ‘ Broken Hill ” on an interesting pastoral
question :
The long - continued deterioration of far-inland Aus-
tralian pastoral country, especially the saltbush lands of
H.S.W. and S.A., has revived the movement in favor of
small paddocks (small paddocks out yonder meaning any-
thing up to 5000 aores). Peter Waite, principal holder of
Momba, N.S.W.’s broadest squattage, is experimenting at
Ophara, and, to some extent, also on Momba itself; and,
mainly because Peter Waite is reckoned about the best
dry-country man in Australia, small paddocks threaten to
become a craze. Counting up their virtues on the fingers,
you get well on to the second hand, too. The great con-
sideration is that vermin are under control in small pad-
docks—and, it might be added, in no other way. Also,
country can then be most effectively ahd economically
spelled ; and, with small paddocks, the lambing ought to
be better. But paddocks, big or little, are not much
good without water; and little tanks or dams are
utterly unreliable in those droughty parts, while it
is very doubtful whether it will pay to put-
down big works in small paddocks whoseo carry
ing capacity is strictly limited. Besides, cat hments
for even small tahks are hot to he picked up on every
three or five Or 20,000 acre block ; and when you’ve got
your catchment, it doesn’t follow that you’ve got holding
ground. One after another, appraisers and assessors with
coastal and mountain experience, who have been sent out-
back under various Lands Acts, have started with a cock-
sure advocacy of small paddocks, in a month or two have
been doubtful about it, ahd before they have completed
their inspections have concluded that, after all and gener-
ally speaking, it might be just as well to let the thing
slide.

A Bulletin correspondent:—
Ever drive a traction engine in the backblocks 7 O, it’s
gay. Taking her down from Sydney to Cootamundra we
met a man on horseback ; and, as usual, the horse shied.
The rider, as much astonished as his beast, tied up to a
fence, and came to inspect us. As he did so
horse dropped with a thud and remained motionless.
“ Your moke is dead,” says my mate. He looked round,
then stared at the engine again, and we left him staring.
Further on we wanted to take a short cut through a
station, but were forbidden by the boundary-rider, till
we threatened to get a map and open up every road on
the run, when he yielded so far as to show us a green line
of timber, which he told us to “ foller right along.” We
did, and I wouldn’t like to say how many fences we went
through—so easily that you wouldn’t know you touched
anything. We camped that night near a dam, and the
squatter rode up to warn us against taking any water.
We kept him on the other side of the engine for fear he’d
see the hose pulsing up and down like a snake in the
grass. By the time he’d finished the tank was full, so we
cheerfully agreed not to take a drop out of his dam tank.
One morning, in passing through Coota, with three loaded
waggons, we turned a corner rather sharply and brought
away some fencing and part of a house. We didn’t know
anything about it till we came back in the evening, when
“ our attention was drawn to the matter ” as politely as
the Irish blood of a Mrs. O’Something would allow. We
made money when the drought came, and the language
of the weatherbound bullockies, as we passed by, was
crimson. We were taking one load across the river at
Booligal, but the bridge was ricketty, and the authorities
refused to allow the engine to cross, so we had to camp.
The bullockies were cheerful, for although there was a
good crossing, the water was deep and would put out our
fire if we tried to cross. About midnight we got up full
steam, detached the waggons, fastened one end of a rope
to a tree on the other side, and fixed the other on to our
winding-drum. Turning on steam, we began to wind
ourselves over the crossing. About half-way over the
fires were out, but there was enough steam in the steam-
chest to drag us half-way up the other bank—where we
relit our furnace and drew up to the top. Then, passing
a line over the bridge, we pulled the waggons across, and
waited for day to dawn on the profane bullockies. Some
few weeks afterwards a pub. was burnt down, just after
we had passed it, and the owners got damages against us.
To pay, we had to sell the engine.— Eurotah.

HOME TALK.
MUSHROOMS AND EGGS.
Take several large mushrooms, and with a spo*n
scrape from the inside all the pink fur; chop it
small. Take the stalks and white outside and boil
in a pint of milk for one hour, then strain. Put
the fur of th* mushroom into the milk, and cook
gently for ten minutes ; thicken with flour into a
sauce, and place four hard-boiled eggs, cut in
halves, on a dish, edge upwards ; pour the sauce
over.
THE WARM CORNER
is the welcome corner in these draughty days of
Winter, when the fire glows warm and cheerful.
But—this fire,
and its warmth,
draw the cold air
into the room
whenever a door
is opened, and
underneath the
door all the time
if the door does
not fit tightly.
A loose mat laid
against the entry
is pushed away
every time any-
one comes in. It
is better to tack
a little strip of
felt along the
bottom of the
door itself. A
scre e n, again,
does wonders in keeping draughts out; and there
is no more cheerful sight for an incomer than a
comfortable armchair and a screen.
THAT ARMCHAIR,
however, should not slope too much in the back, or
it becomes uncomfortable to read or work in.
A WINTER TROUBLE
that affects many of us is cold, chilly hands and
feet. They come from defective circulation and
poverty of the
blood. Poor
blood is what the
doctors call anae-
mia, always a pre-
valent trouble
where women are
concerned. It
gives pallid
cheeks, flat figure,
languidness, and
a way of being
easily tired. Very
often it is the
first step towards
“ decline,” or, as
people call it, con-
sumption. Better
stop it in time.
It is good to be
rosy and well if
you can. Here
is a true story : Miss Richardson, daughter of
the manager of a large brickworks at King’s Dyke,
Whittlesey, in Cambridgeshire, is a picture of
health. Yet a little while ago she was in a con-
sumption. Doctors attended her, but the girl
made no progress, and became so weak that all
quite expected her to die as consumptive people
do die. By the merest accident the father found
out what can be done by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People. He read it, and said to his wife,
“ Here’s somebody advertising, and if only half
what it says is true, there’s hope for Jane
yet.” The same night he sent for a box of
Pills, and before half the box was gene saw a
marked change. Miss Richardson is, in her
father’s words, “ A woman again, and quite well.
Her disease was a decline —consumption; but
there are no signs of that now. She was so weak
that she could do nothing ; it appeared as if she
hadn’t the strength to walk. But Dr. Williams
Pills entirely cured her.”
This is an example for all women who feel lan-
guid, have a pain in the chest or the back, or fell
the need of a tonic.

Woman at 45.
What Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills will do for
Women at the change of life is shown in a case
reported in the Dewsbury (Eng.) Chronicle. It
is that of Mrs. Benjamin Smith, who lives at
Hanover-street, Dewsbury. The change of life
had left her weak and miserable ; with constant
pains, dizziness, and shortness of breath on the
slightest exertion, together with chronic rheuma-
tism in the head and eyes. The pain in her head
was too acute for words; she got no sleep either
by day or by night, and her agony was at times
so great that it took two people to hold her in bed.
A skilful doctor was called in, but did no good.
“ My cheeks would swell up as big as saucers,
says Mrs. Smith ; “my eyes were as red as fire,
and there was no getting rid of the pains in the
head. I also suffered from a sluggish liver and a
weak heart, and at times my eyesight was so bad
that objects before me seemed but dim shadows,
and there were none of my friends who looked for
my restoration to health.”
“I saw an account in a newspaper of a cure
similar to mine through the use of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, and my husband urged me to try
them. Before two boxes were used the pains had
disappeared as if by magic, and the continued use
of the Pills for a little more than a month has left
me enjoying as good health as ever I had in my
life. I consider Dr. Williams Pink Pills a bless-
ing and as I know there are thousands of women
who suffer in silence from troubles having the
same origin as mine, I am grateful enough for
what they have done for me to tell my story in
the hope that some suffering sister may be
eimilarly helped, and you have full permission to
publish what I have told you, which statements
can be verified by any of my neighbors. .tv ll3
wonderful remedy is offered to the public with a
full confidence that there_ is no disease arising
from a watery or impoverished condition of the
blood, or shattered nervous forces, which it will
not cure if the directions are faithfully followed
and the treatment persisted in.
The Terrible Influenza.
ITS RAVAGES ARE APPALLING—ONLY
PROMPT MEASURES CAN RESTORE
THE SUFFERER TO HEALTH.
The people of this country have good cause to
view with alarm an outbreak of influenza, as it
leaves behind it more shattered constitutions than
any other known disease. Mr. Edward Dotting,
for ten years a councillor of the united townships
of Bedford, Olden, and Palmerston, in Frontenac
county Ontario, Canada, is one who nearly fell a
victim to the scourge. To a Whig correspondent
Mr. Botting said“ About two years ago I had
a bad attack of influenza, and the after effects
of that malignant trouble brought me so low
that my friends despaired of my recovery. I was
troubled with severe and constant pains in the
back, sensations of extreme dizziness, weakness,
and was in fact in a generally used-up condition. I
had read frequently of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
and felt they must have some special virtues else
they could not obtain such strong endorsations in
all parts of the country. I determined to try
them, and I bless the day that I came to that con-
clusion. Before the first box was finished I felt
benefited, and I continued their use until I was as
strong as ever. I consider Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills the best medicine sold, and I would not be
without them in the house if they cost 5 dollars
a box.
Washing Day.
In this inclement weather, when so many are
suffering from colds, coughs, and rheumatism, the
story of Mrs. Mary Brown, of 71 Simmons-street,
Enmore, Sydney, will be read with devouring
interest.
When a reporter called at this lady’s resi-
dence he found her busily engaged at the wash-
tub, what she called doing a “ day’s washing.” It
was, indeed, difficult to believe by the amount of
“ elbow grease ” she laid into her labors that this
interesting personage had lately been informed by
medical men that here was a case which would
probably develop into chronic rheumatism.
“ I have called, Mrs. Brown,” said the reporter,
“to ascertain the truth of the rumor that has
reached me of your having recently suffered from
a severe attack of rheumatism.”
“ It is quite true, but I am now happy to relate
that I feel as well as I ever did. I have been
suffering on and off from rheumatism for the last
five years, and the slightest change in the weather
used to give me most awful twinges. It chiefly
affected my arms. If it had not been for some of
my friends I could never have got through my
household duties. This complaint appeared to
affect my entire system, and, as the money I had
spent in doctor’s advice and so-called remedies—
why, it makes me positively disgusted to think of.
I began to think that, if my sufferings continued,
life was not worth living. I remember one day,
when reading the Evening News, that an ad-
vertisement caught my eye, headed, ‘ Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cure Rheumatism,
Sciatica, &c.’ Now, having wasted so much money
already, I was at first loth to try any more adver-
tised medicines, but I thought, ‘ in for a penny in
for a penny,’ so I invested in three boxes of this
specific.
“ My husband laughed at me when I told him I
was going in for a course of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, and said, ‘ Oh, you are going to throw away
some more money, are you ? ’
“I said, ‘Yes, I am,’ and right glad am I that
I did so, for I am happy to tell you that they have
done me all the good in the world. Why, I feel
now a brand new woman, and my husband says I
look the ‘ pink ’of perfection now. Not only have
I lost my rheumatic pains, but my appetite has
returned, and I can now enjoy my food. In fact,
my appetite is so good that Brown says I will eat
him out of house and home. This change is all
due to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
“ How many boxes have you taken, Mrs.
Brown ? ”
“I have had seven boxes in all, and I have so
much belief in them that I intend always to keep
a box by me, and I shall recommend them to all
my friends. You are quite at liberty to publish
this statement, as I believe in giving credit to
whom credit is due.”
Victorian Home Life.
Mrs. Margaret Ross is a fine sample of the class
that has done so much to turn the wastes of in-
land Victoria into “smilinghomesteads.” Origin-
ally she resided at Heathcote, and there, some two
decades since, witnessed the fluctuation of mining
fortune that made the locality historical in Vic-
torian mining annals. Some years later she re-
moved to Alexandra, and in the vicinity of that
pretty township—close to the Acheron —Mrs. Ross
and family lived up to two years since, when,
coming to Melbourne with her husband, they
purchased the well-known grocery store at the
junction of Rathdown and Palmerston streets,
where a representative of the Carlton Gazette
found her ready and willing to discourse on the
immense benefits she had received from a course
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. Mrs.
Ross looked the picture of health, and courteously
requested the representative to be seated; and
there was a smile of genuine welcome when his
mission was explained and the history of her par-
ticular malady and its cure solicited.
“Neuralgia,” replied Mrs. Ross, “was the com-
plaint I suffered from for a period of ten or
twelve years. During the day time my sufferings
were painful, but on retiring to rest the agony
I suffered was excruciating, reducing me to a state
of general collapse. I felt it during the day
slightly, but most severely after I got warm at
night. ”
“Did you consult any doctors at all?”
“ Many; but received no permanent benefit.
They gave me draughts and powders, but the
effect was really nothing. ”
“You, of course, have tried other remedies ? ”
“Yes, I tried everything, but obtained no
relief.”
“ What induced you to try Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People ? ”
“An elderly lady, resident at Heathcote, 74
years of age, and who had been a sufferer from
rheumatism for 40 years, and who has been abso-
lutely cured by their use, recommended me to try
Pink Pills for Pale People.”
“ How long since might that be ? ”
“Six weeks since—the beginning of July.
During the time I was taking the first box I
lost only one night’s rest; but since then have
never had the least return of the old complaint.”
“ Have you used much of the medicine ? ”
“About four boxes. Previous to my taking
them the pain in my head reduced me to such a
state that I was on the verge of madness. The
last three years I had been very bad, so you can
imagine the pleasure it was to me to find a remedy
that has actually made a new woman of me.
“That is so,’’"remarked Mr. Ross, who was pre-
sent. “ Had you seen my wife a few months since
and now you would scarcely believe her to be the
same woman.”
“ Yes,” rejoined Mrs. Ross, “my recovery has
been most pronounced and satisfactory to me.
I have great faith in Dr. Williams’ Pills for Pale
People, and recommend them wherever possible.”
Congratulating Mrs. Ross on her recovery, and
expressing a hope that she would long live to
enjoy the blessings of good health, the reporter
withdrew.
Washing Baby’s Hair.
Whenever baby’s hair is washed it should be
very carefully dried afterwards. Neglect of this
will frequently give the child a cold in the head,
which if neglected may lay the foundation of a
deafness that will go on increasing until, in later
life, it may become permanent. It is best that
the baby’s hair should be washed at bedtime, so
that there may be no danger of draughts or cold
afterwards.
Bo As You Would Be
Bone By.
Marrickville has not a happier, brighter resi-
dent than Mrs. Edwards, wife of the Marrickville
Town Hall caretaker. Hearing that Mrs. Edwards
for years had been a just sufferer from indigestion
and dyspepsia, a reporter called on her to get
the facts of the case, particularly as he had also
heard that she had suddenly been cured. Mrs.
Edwards very courteously received the representa-
tive, and gave him an account of her ailments and
their cure.
“ First of all,” she proceeded, “ I suffered from
sleeplessness, ringing noises in my head, and an
overbearing pain at my chest. Furthermore, my
appetite failed, and all that would rest on my
stomach was soft food such as milk and arrowroot.
But what alarmed me most was the way my heart
would go pit-a-pat on the very slightest exertion.
I can assure you if I walked a quarter of a mile
these distressing symptoms would come on. This
frightened me very much, for I feared I was suf-
fering from heart disease. As to the remedies I
swallowed, why, their name is legion, but unfor-
tunately no good effects resulted, and I feared my
troubles were neither to be cured nor alleviated.
I consequently began to feel very low and de-
pressed.
“ One day I read of some cures effected by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and indeed
it proved a joyous day for me. I determined to
try a few boxes of this specific, bought them, and
commenced to take them according to the direc-
tions which I noticed were around each box.
After the first two boxes I felt a decided improve-
ment, and decided to continue with this medicine.
As I persevered with the treatment I made satis-
factory progress.”
“ You said that your heart gave you consider-
able anxiety, Mrs. Edwards.”
“ Yes, I was afraid it was diseased, and I'm
glad to find I was mistaken. It took a little time
to pull me together again, for you must remember
I had sunk very low ; but I am happy to st ite that
I am fairly on my feet again, and am so .satisfied
that this cure has been effected by Dr. William#’
Pink Pills for Pale People that I have recom-
mended them to all my friends. Yes, yon are at
perfect liberty to publish this testimonial, for I
consider it a duty that I owe to the world at
large.”
Anyone with a particle of sympathy in his com-
position will readily concede that life under the
circumstances narrated in Mrs. Edwards’ case is
a failure, and death holds little terror for one so
situated. Imagine even such an existence, and
the thought brings terror with it. It is like bear-
ing a heavy load with little strength to manage it,
consequent upon the denial of food and sleep. No
wonder the heart rebels and seems determined
upon bursting bounds.

Important to Mothers.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth ?
Instead of bright eyes, glowing rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, sallow,
and greenish complexion, and a languidness
of step that bespeaks disease and an early death
if proper treatment is not promptly resorted to
and persisted in until the impoverished blood is
enriched and the functions of life become regular.
TO PARENTS AND GUARDIANS.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at the
time their daughters are budding into womanhood
If your daughter is pale, complains of weakness,
is “ tired out ” upon the slightest exertion ; if she
is troubled with headache, backache, pain in the
side ; if her temper is fitful, and her appetite poor,
she is in a condition of extreme peril, a fit subject
for the development of that most dreaded of all
diseases—consumption. If you notice any of these
symptoms, lose no time in procuring Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. They will assist the patient to develop
properly -and regularly ; they will enrich the
blood, and restore health’s roses to the cheek, and
all dangers of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
womanhood, and thus avoid the risks of disease
or early decay.
Bale and Bloodless.
Mrs. Boyd, of Darvel, Ayrshire, Scotland, who
was interviewed with her husband, by a special
correspondent of the Ayr Observer, may be
said to have obtained a new lease of life through
the instrumentality of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. “ I have seen my wife,” said her
husband, “ like that wall (pointing to a wall
which was practically colorless), but now she
is herself again. As the old Scotch saying is, Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills have been a ‘ God-send ’ to
us.” For seven years, Mrs. Boyd had suffered
frightfully from pains in her body, aggravated by
tortures of neuralgia in the head, and benumbing of
the limbs. In her own words she “was mad with
suffering.” Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, however,
restored her health, appetite, and nerves, and
brought back the colour to her face.
Sparkling Eyes and Rosy Cheeks.
The acquisition of perfect health is the aim of
everyone. A few remarks, therefore, on the sub-
ject will be acceptable to all young ladies who are
not the happy possessors of sparkling eyes and
rosy cheeks. Mrs. Bowe, of North Maldon, Vic-
toria, whose daughter, Miss May, had been
seriously ill for some time, gives the following
account of her restoration to health and strength
again :
“ My daughter had been strong and healthy
until almost two years ago, when she grew very
pale and thin. She became weak and low spirited,
lost her appetite ; her sleep was -troubled and
restless, and the least exertion fatigued her—in
fact, she had to take her sister’s arm for support
when out walking. She was of a wonderfully active
and cheerful disposition before this change took
place, and the
a Iteration,
therefore,
caused us
great con-
cern, and
medical aid
was sought,
but no good
resulted. Se-
veral medi-
cines were
tried, but she
became
weaker and
more listless
every day.
The doctor
said she was
suffering
from anaemia
or poorness
of blood.
We then became seriously alarmed, and con-
sulted different medical men, who prescribed
and told Miss May to take as much exer-
cise as possible. She, being fond of flowers,
naturally turned her attention to the garden,
but had to abandon the work, as the least
exertion exhausted her. Often, when I thought
she was out taking exercise in the fresh air I have
found her in the drawing-room with her head
resting on her hands, and in a somnolent state.
Then, when disturbed, she would steal quietly to
her room, and remain there for hours at time. At
this stage we became extremely anxious concern-
ing her health, and again sought medical advice,
as°we thought she was going into a decline. It
was of no use though, nothing did her any good.
She was then so thin and weak that the slightest
exertion tired her. She also suffered from severe
cramps in the legs. .
“ Friends then recommended patent medicines,
and two or three kinds were tried unsuccessfully.
“At last I decided to take her away to the
country for a change, but just as I was about to do
so I saw in the Tarrangower Times an advertise-
ment about Dr. Williams 3 Pink Pills for Pale
People, and noticing that people with symptoms
similar to those of my daughter had been cured, I
determined to give them a trial.
“ After using half-a-box a great improvement
was noticeable, and after using three boxes she
regained her appetite, was able to sleep with com-
fort, and was so strong and well that we con-
sidered there was no necessity for her continuing
to take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. So she discon-
tinued them. I honestly believe my daughter’s
recovery was due to this great remedy. She is
now as well as ever she was in her life. She has a
fine color, is able to eat and sleep well, and can
take any amount of outdoor. exercise without feel-
ing fatigue. I feel very grateful to Dr. Williams
for my daughter’s recovery, and _ would like to
have the case made known, as it may induce
others who suffer as my daughter did to try this
remedy to their benefit, and thus perhaps bring
sunshine and happiness to their homes.”
Great satisfaction has been expressed by Miss
Bowe’s large circle of friends that she is again
enjoying good health, and able to take her accus'
toined plaee at local social gatherings.

They’ve swallered every drain j
So jump from off my knee a jiff
An’ fill, old girl, again.
Ter-morrer mornin’ off we clear ;
This evenin’, it’s our shout ;
So give our cheques ter missus, dear,
An’ say we’ll take ’em out.
For we’re off to the Speewaa in the Never Never
Land,
Over the Cooper an’ beyond the belt o’ sand ;
We’re chock-a-block o’ graftin’ in the same old track,
So we’ll make a break for Speewaa in the land Oat
Back.
The run’s three hundred mile across,
With dams sunk every chain,
An’ huts for travellers to doss
Built all across the plain.
The saltbush there has spread an’ riz
Until the track’s obscure ;
An’ fifty sheep an acre is
A thing they reckon poor.
So we’re off to the Speewaa, where the bush grows
wide,
Crossing the track till a man can hardly ride ;
We’re full up o’ foolin’ in a backyard run,
So we're polin’ off to Speewaa, where the game ain t
done.
They keep on lambin’ all the year
An’ yet they never lose.
A one per cent, o’ what they rear
And half a hundred ewes.
An’ though there’s twenty million sheep
That wear the Speewaa brand,
You’ll never find a dozen dead
In all .the Speewaa land.
So we’re off to the Speewaa where the sheep are
tough ;
We’ve stuck to other stations quite long enough.
But now we’ve got some sense at last — you’ll see us
step it out
For Speewaa in the Never, where there ain’t no
drought.
The boss has whips o’ stuff about,
An’ miles an’ miles o’ land ;
He’d buy old Jimmy Tyson out,
An’ sell up Hungry Rand.
He keeps the pound-a-himdr.-1 pay
With all the chaps he’s got,
An’ leaves it ter themselves ter say
If sheep are wet or not.
So we're off to the Speewaa, where there’s bunce to
scoff,
With pay at union prices an' the boss a real toff ;
No more thirty bob a week for twelve hours’ graft a
day,
We’ll start at the Speewaa on the good old pay.
The huts are painted pinks and blues,
With mirrors on the walls,
An’ niggers hurry in with booza
When any shearer calls.
There’s plates o’ icecream in the shed,
An’ on the hottest days
Long shandies with a foamin’ head
Are handed round on trays.
So we're off to the Speewaa where the fun’s all right ;
There’s a grim' planner tootin’ in the huts at night,
And a dandy girl ter play it, *oo, with lovely goldiny
hair ;
So we're off to the Speewaa where they treat yer fair.
So give us just a kiss or two,
And wish us luck ahead ;
In six months we’ll be safely through,
Or else we’ll both be dead.
But we ain’t goin’ ter feed no crow,
Nor meet no sudden ends,
An’ when we’re back ye’ll never know
Ye had such toffs for friends.
So collar the hilly an' pick up the swag again,
We're goin’ to pad the same old hoof across the same
old plain ;
But cornin' hack /—just look at us a-drivin' four-in-
hand ;
For we’ll make our piles at Speewaa in the Never
Never Land.

The Duchess of York is four inches taller
than the Duke.
C.J.H. : “ At Pera bore (eight miles from
■ Bourke, N.S.W.) Algerian date-palms, four years
old, are bearing fruit. Quidnuncs said they would
need 14 years at least. Superintendent Gorman
is enthusiastic and confident of ultimate good
results, but admits that soda and sun solidify
sandy loam into lumps as hard as the hobs of
Hades. Interesting to tea-swillers; half the
quantity goes twice as far with Pera water, and it
enhances flavor.
Alice Springs, pretty close to the centre of
Australia, which the overlanding cyclists from
Port Darwin to Adelaide make their half-way
house, is about the most interesting far-back
township in Australia. It is in the heart of the
Mao Donnell Ranges—a big chain 400 miles or so
long, forming the continent’s backbone—which
are being slowly explored for gold, mica, and
other things. All round is sandy desert; but the
ranges themselves consist of alternating rocky
peaks and saddles and wonderfully fertile flats.
Some of the rock formations are most fantastic,
and many wildly-picturesque springs and rocky
water-holes and caves with native drawings have
been discovered. The climate is pretty good ;
and the blacks, who are fairly numerous, belong
mostly to the Arunto tribe, over whom Station-
master Gillen, the boss of these parts, has won
m'uch influence. Further back in the ranges,
however, the natives are troublesome : they, last
month, compelled an Adelaide-Broken Hill pro-
specting party to retreat. So far as prospecting is
concerned, the place suffers sadly from the want
of expert assistance—and its 1200 miles from the
capital, of course.
The Fat Woman .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Old Devine wiped the dishes carefully, and then
came back to the little window that opened into
the bar. An 18st. woman filled the draughty door-
way ; in her apron half-a-dozen apples, a packet
of sweets and some gingerbreads—the pick of an
Italian’s basket. She lay back in a canvas chair
at peace with the world. Old Devine opened the
window slightly and listened. Above the zmg-
zing of locusts and the buzz of flies came a
crunch, crunch, and smacking of lips; shifting a
little, he saw the slow-moving jowl and the fat
relaxing under her left ear. A nervous tremor
shot across his face. He closed the window
hurriedly. “ Morning and night—morning and
night. God! how she eats ! ”
The pub. was built on the edge of a creek where
the bridge joined the great white road running
east and west. Men and boys, often women,
tramped over it all the year round. Some of them
stopped at Devine’s, and some dragged their
starved bodies to the stinking lagoon opposite.
A man limps to the door, nodding carelessly to
the fat woman, and squeezes past into the bar.
Already she has noted wire in his boots and string
in his clothes. Suddenly he makes up his mind.
‘Beer —one long beer.”
His overbearing confidence jars; she has seen it
before —a, mere pitfall for the inexperienced. A
harsh demand for sixpence down slews him round.
“ I was about to mention it. To-morrow I pass
here at 11.30 ; you will be paid.”
The fat woman appears to forget him ; her eyes
dose, her fingers drop amongst the gingerbreads.
He reflects.
Beer costs a fraction under twopence. To-mor-
row she is paid 6d.—cent, per cent, twice over
times are dull; a few would snap at it.
“ Yes or no? ” No answer except a crunch and
a gurgle. . .
“ Yes or no ? ” His voice is calm.
“ No.” „
“ Half-a-glass, then. I’ve walked 20 miles.
She falls into a doze. He smites his hands
cheerfully.
“ Look here, old girl, I’ll ask you once more.
Half-a-glass, and Tommy will pay you when he
comes home.”
“ What Tommy ? ”
He faces her a little irritably.
“Your son Tommy ; met him on the ’Bidgee in
’9o—fed him, carried his swag, and paid his score
manv a time. Lord strike me dead ! ”
She folds her arms, nodding at every word.
“On the ’Bidgee in ’9O. A fine couple you’d
make ; he’d run up scores ; you’d come in out of
the rain. A pair of lovely birds.”
“ Some mothers like to hear now and then,” he
ventured.
“ Eight years and never a line,” she muttered.
Then, half savagely, “Every tug on the road
knows him, and every damned one walks from the
Gulf to Menindie to find me out. You’re the fifth
bummer this year.”
After this outburst she consoles herself with an
apple ; strange gurglings follow. Then in a man’s
voice she breaks oat—“ Did he ever tell you about
Emily?” The stranger thought he had.
“Ah he did, eh ? She was a white-faced chit,
anyhow, and I found her in the gutter and tried to
bring her up. We all make mistakes. You ough#
to have seen her before she grew up—-nothing but
a God-forsaken whimpering little ghost a Chow
wouldn’t tread on ; and the crawling mita springs
into a full-lipped, big-bosomed woman before you
could wink. ‘Keep your eye on Tommy! sez Ito
myself, ‘and a whip-hand on her.’ Young man,
X was born slow, she had him running after her in
no time. I stopped it! ”
“ You stopped it! ” reiterated the man.
“ You bet. What right had she to him ? ”
“Go on.” , . ,
The fat woman lay back breathing heavily.
“ I gave her notice to quit. It was my only
chance, and Tommy was away at the time.
Having suspicions, I called in a trooper the day
she was leaving. We searched her box six silver
spoons and a heap of my linen. I pressed the
“ And now you’re happy ? ” His fingers played
in a reddish beard.
She nodded. “You’d better have that drink
now ; I didn’t intend ...” She floundered from
the chair, revolving slowly into the bar.
“ Beer you said : if ”
» * * *
He had slipped out and was walking swiftly
away. Something gripped his arm. He faced
old Devine. . „
“My God! Tommy, I knew your voice. How
you sold her ! ” He looked into his son s brooding
face ; his own was lit up and feverish. .
“ You’re coming back, Tommy, now. ihings
ain’t so bad.” . . . , .
Tommy’s lips screwed into an unmistakable
“ ’Dad, it would mean hell to me. Think of it:
she planted things in Emily’s box, and I never
guessed the truth till Don’t, Dad—don t
make it worse. Good-bye ! ”
* * * *
The old man stared under his shaking palm at
the ragged sleeve making farewell gestures from
the middle of the road. He hurried back feebly
to where the fat woman was staring across the
dull red plain.

An Oversight of Steelman's.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Steelman and Smith —professional wanderers—
were making back for Wellington, M.L., down
through the wide and rather dreary-looking Hutt
Valley. They were “ broke.” They carried their
few remaining belongings in two skimpy, ama-
teurish-looking swags. Steelman had fourpence
left. They were very tired and very thirsty—at
least Steelman was, and he answered for both.
It was Smith’s policy to feel and think just
exactly as Steelman did. Said Steelman—
“ The landlord of the next pub. is not a bad
sort. I won’t go in—he might remember me.
You’d best go in. You’ve been tramping round
in the Wairarapa district for the last six months,
looking for work. You’re going back to Welling-
ton now, to try and get on the new corporation
works just being started there—the sewage works.
You think you’ve got a show. You’ve got some
mates in Wellington, and they’re looking out for
a chance for you. You did get a job last week on
a sawmill at Silverstream, and the boss sacked
you after three days and wouldn’t pay you a penny.
That’s just his way. I know him—at least a
mate of mine does. I’ve heard of him often
enough. His name’s Cowman. Don’t forget the
name, whatever you do. The landlord on here
hates him like poison ; he’ll sympathise with you.
Tell him you’ve got a mate with you; he’s gone
on ahead—took a short cut across the paddocks.
Tell him you’ve only got fourpence left, and see if
he’ll give you a drop in a bottle. Says you :
‘ Well, boss, the fact is we’ve only got fourpence,
but you might let us have a drop in a bottle ’; and
very likely he’ll stand you a couple of pints in a
gin-bottle. You can fling the coppers on the
counter, but the chances are he won’t take them.
He’s not a bad sort. Beer’s fourpence a pint out
here, same’s in Wellington. See that gin-bottle
lying there by the stump; get it and we’ll take it
down to the river with us and rinse it out.”
They reached the river bank.
“You’d better take my swag—it looks itiore
decent,” said Steelman. “ No, I’ll tell you what
we’ll do: we’ll undo both swags and make them
into one—one decent swag, and I’ll cut round
through the lanes and wait for you on the road
ahead of the pub.”
He rolled up the swag with much care and
deliberation and considerable judgment. He
fastened Smith’s belt round one end of it, and the
handkerchiefs round the other and made a towel
serve as a shoulder-strap.
“I wish we had a canvas bag to put it in,” he
said, “ora cover of some sort. But never mind.
The landlord’s an old Australian bushman, now I
come to think of it ; the swag looks Australian
enough, and it might appeal to his feelings, you
know—bring up old recollections like. But you’d
best not say you come from Australia, because he’s
been there, and he’d soon trip you up. He might
have been where you’ve been, you know, so don’t
try to do too much. You always do mug-up the
business when you try to do more than I tell you.
You might tell him your mate came from Aus-
tralia—but no, he might want you to bring me in.
Better stick to Maoriland. I don’t believe in too
much ornamentation. Plain lies are the best.”
“ What’s the landlord’s name ? ” asked Smith.
“Never mind that. You don’t want to know
that. You are not supposed to know him at all.
It might look suspicious if you called him by his
name, and lead to awkward questions ; then you’d
be sure to put your foot into it.”
“ I could say I read it over the door.”
“ Bosh. Travellers don’t read the names over
the doors, when they go into pubs. You’re an
entire stranger to him. Call him ‘ Boss.’ Say
‘ Good-day, Boss,’ when you go in, and swing down
your swag as if you’re used to it. Ease it down
like this. Then straighten yourself up, stick your
hat back, and wipe your forehead, and try to look
as hearty and independent and cheerful as you pos-
sibly can ! Curse the Government and say the
country’s done ! It don’t matter what Govern-
ment it is, for he’s always against it. I never
knew a real Australian that wasn’t. Say that
you’re thinking about trying to get over to Aus-
tralia, and then listen to him talking about it—
and try to look interested, too ! Get that damned
stone-deaf expression off your face ! . . . He’ll
run Australia down most likely (I never knew an
Other-sider that had settled down over here who
didn’t). But don't you make any mistake and
agree with him, because, although successful
Australians over here like to run their own
country down, there’s very few of them that care to
hear anybody else do it. . . . Don’t come away
as soon as you get your beer. Stay and listen to him
for awhile, as if you’re interested in his yarning,
and give him time to put you on to a job, or offer
you one. Give him a chance to ask how you and
your mate are off for tobacco or tucker. Like as
not he’ll sling you half-a-crown when you come
away—that is if you work it all right. Now' try to
think of something to say to him, and make your-
self a bit interesting—if you possibly can. Tell
him about the fight we saw back at the pub. the
other day. He might know some of the chaps.
This is a sleepy hole, and there ain’t much news
knocking round. ... I wish I could go in
myself, but lie’s sure to remember me. I’m afraid
he got left the last time I stayed there (so did one
or two others); and, besides, I came away without
saying good-bye to him, and he might feel a bit
sore about it. That’s the worst of travelling on
the old road. Come on now', wake up ! ”
“ Bet I’ll get a quart,” said Smith, brightening
up, “ and some tucker for it to wash down.”
“If you don’t,” said Steelman, “I’ll stoush
you. Never mind the bottle ; fling it away. It
doesn’t look well for a traveller to go into a pub.
with an empty bottle in his hand. A real swag-
man never does. It looks much better to come
out with a couple of full ones. That’s what you’ve
got to do. Now, come along.”
Steelman turned off into a lane, cut across the
paddocks to the road again, and waited for Smith.
He hadn’t long to wait.
Smith went on towards the public-house, re-
hearsing his part as he walked—repeating his
“ lines ” to himself so as to be sure of remember-
ing all that Steelman had told him to say to the
landlord, and adding, with what he considered
appropriate gestures, some fancy touches of his
own, which he determined to throw in in
spite of Steelman’s advice and warning., ‘l’ll
tell him (this)—l’ll tell him (that). Well, look
here, boss, I’ll say you’re pretty right and I quite
agree with you as far as that’s concerned, but,”
&c. And so, murmuring and mumbling to him-
self, Smith reached the hotel. The day was late,
and the bar was small, and low, and dark. Smith
walked in with all the assurance he could muster,
eased down his swag in a corner in what he no
doubt considered the true professional style, and,
swinging round to the bar, said in a loud voice
which he intended to be cheerful, independent and
hearty:
“ Good-day, boss ! ”
But it wasn’t a “boss.” It was about the
hardest-faced old woman that Smith had ever
seen. The pub. had changed hands.
♦ • * *
“I—l beg your pardon, missus,” stam-
mered poor Smith.
It was a knock-down blow for Smith. He
couldn’t come to time. He and Steelman had had
a landlord in their minds all the time, and laid
their plans accordingly; the possibility of having a
she—and one like this—to deal with never entered
into their calculations. Smith had no time to
reorganise, even if he had the brains to do so,
without the assistance of his mate’s knowledge of
human nature.
“ I I beg your pardon, missus,” he stammered.
Painful pause. She sized him up.
“ Well, what do you want? ”
“ Well, missus—l—the fact is—will you give me
a bottle of beer for fourpence ? ”
“ Wha —what? ”
‘ ‘ I mean . The fact is, we’ve only got four-
pence left, and—l’ve got a mate outside, and you
might let us have a quart or so, in a bottle, for
that . I mean—anyway, you might let us
have a pint. I’m very sorry to you, missus.”
But she couldn’t do it. No. Certainly not.
Decidedly not. All her drinks were sixpence.
She had her license to pay, and the rent, and a
family to keep. It wouldn’t pay out there—it
wasn’t worth her while. It wouldn’t pay the cost
of carting the liquor out, &c., &c.
“ Well, missus,” poor Smith blurted out at last,
in sheer desperation, “ give me what you can in a
bottle for this. I’ve —I’ve got a mate outside.”
And he put the four coppers on the bar.
“ Have you got a bottle ? ”
“ No—but ”
“If I give you one, will you bring it back?
You can’t expect me to give you a bottle as well
as a drink.”
“Yes, mum ; I’ll bring it back directly.”
She reached out a bottle from under the bar,
and very deliberately measured out a little over
a pint and poured it into the bottle, which she
handed to Smith without a cork.
Smith went his way without rejoicing. It
struck him forcibly'that he should have saved the
money until they reached Petone, or the city,
where Steelman would be sure to get a decent
drink. But how was he (Smith) to know ? He
had chanced it, and lost; Steelman might have
done the same. What troubled Smith most was
the thought of what Steelman would say; he
already heard him, in imagination, saying:
You re a mug, Smith—Smith, you are a mug.”
But Steelman didn 5 t say much. He was pre-
pared for the worst by seeing Smith come along so
soon. He listened to his story with an air of
gentle sadness, even as a stern father might listen
to the voluntary confession of a wayward child •
then he held the bottle up to the fading light of
departing day, looked through it (the bottle), and
said :
* Well - it ain’t worth while dividing it.”
Smith s heart sank right down through a hole
in the sole of his left boot into the hard road.
Here, Smith,” said Steelman, handing him
the bottle, “ Drink it, old man, you want it. It
wasn t altogether your fault; it! was an oversight
of mine. I didn t bargain for a woman of that
kind and of course, you couldn’t be expected to
think of it. Drink it! Drink it down, Smith,
out ™ ana^e wor k an oracle before this night is
Smith was forced to believe his ears, and, re-
covering from his surprise, he drank.
•*uVu r ° n i lsed *° tak . e back the bottle,” he said,
with the ghost of a smile.
Steelman took the bottle by the neck and broke
it on the fence.
a ° n ' ’ til carry the swag fof
And they tramped on in the gathering starlight.
Moral : Deep sorrow makes us gentle.
HENRY LAWSON.
“The Bohemian prefers his inside to his
outside. ” The best definition yet.
Which should know a thing best—the man
who tries it and succeeds or the man who tries it
and fails? Two well-known S.A. squatters were,
the other day, arguing a pastoral matter, and,
said one, to clinch his view—“ Well, Peter I
ought to know, for I lost .£70,000 on my stations ”
The retort was : “I should be the better judge,
for I made .£70,000 on mine.”
M. Tilon, the French critic, in his recent
book on the English stage, mentions that the
Dramatic Censor has “ forbidden to put a comic
bishop on the stage—unless it were a colonial
bishop, in which case the Censor would give his
sanction.” This certainly shows, on the Censor’s
part, a lively sense of the superior ridiculousness
of colonial bishops. Probably the Censor had
seen £ leld I lowers Goe in his apron and gaiters.
“Civilisation generally will read with a
wild whoop of horror that the Spanish authorities
have tortured and branded their gaoled anarchists.
The modes of anarchists, when they are top dog,
are not remarkable for refinement, but the fact will
not deter the British professional philanthropist
from shrieking, as he did at the time of the Arme-
nian atrocities, that his brother Briton, Tommy
Atkins, should instantly be led out to make mince
pies of someone—in this instance the cruel hidalgo
How soon, how very soon, Exeter Hall forgets !
When Chinese Gordon, the Christian Hero
carried the sword of a British officer and a gentle-
man to the service of the abominable Manchu Tar-
tar he invariably branded on the left cheek all the
Taiping prisoners he captured in an engagement.
These he forced to fight on his side, placing them
as the Royal Psalmist did Uriah, husband of
Bathsheba, in the forefront of the battle. Did
they attempt to run they were bayoneted by the
Mantchu ‘lmps.’ of the rear rank. When they
went forward, as they invariably did, they were
bayoneted by their old Taiping comrades in front.
They died as brave men die—without whimper-
ing.”—“ Zed” in Australian Star.
